Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
4.1  Introduction
Metabolism of xenobiotics, including therapeutic drugs, is the 
process whereby their chemical structures are enzymatically 
altered, usually to more water-soluble metabolites that are sub-
sequently eliminated from the body. Approximately 75% of 
the top 200 therapeutic drugs prescribed in the United States 
are cleared by metabolism. However, metabolism of xenobiot-
ics can also give rise to reactive and toxic chemicals that cause 
organ toxicities. This chapter reviews the major pathways of 
xenobiotic metabolism, discusses the different mechanisms of 
metabolite-induced organ toxicities, and introduces some of 
the new tools used to study xenobiotic metabolism. In recent 
years, pharmacogenomics has become a rapidly expanding 
field of genetics that can predict individuals’ responses to drug 
metabolism and the resulting variable effects on efficacy and 
toxicity. As part of the field of precision medicine, pharma-
cogenomic testing helps clinicians tailor drug prescriptions for 
individual patients to maximize therapeutic efficacy and avoid 
unnecessary toxicities. This chapter reviews the pharmacoge-
nomics of xenobiotic metabolism in detail. In spring 2020, 
the U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA) issued three 
guidance documents on the safety evaluation of drug metabo-
lites and the investigation of metabolism-mediated drug-drug 
interaction potential of investigational new drug candidates. 
This chapter also discusses regulatory recommendations for 
the pharmaceutical and biotechnology industry regarding 
when and how to identify and characterize drug metabolites 
and drug-drug interactions.
4.2  General Features and Basic Concepts 
of Xenobiotic Metabolism
Metabolism plays a crucial role in clearing xenobiotics from 
the body and regulating endogenous substrate hemostasis. 
Upon administration, hydrophilic xenobiotics can be readily 
excreted through the urine. However, circulating lipophilic 
xenobiotics can undergo passive reabsorption from renal 
tubules and accumulate in the body to levels that can lead 
to toxicity. As the first step in detoxification, metabolizing 
enzymes in the liver, intestines, and many other organs met-
abolically convert hydrophobic xenobiotics to metabolites 
that are more polar and less hydrophobic (a biotransforma-
tion process). The reduced lipophilicity renders metabolites 
intrinsically less toxic and facilitates clearance from the body 
through physiological processes, including renal and biliary 
excretion. Although metabolism, in general, is considered 
a “detoxification mechanism”, exceptions are not uncom-
mon. In some cases, metabolism may introduce undesired 
adverse effects due to “metabolic bioactivation”. Examples 
include the association of severe liver toxicity from metabo-
lites of acetaminophen,1 polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons 
(PAHs), and tamoxifen in humans.2 As shown in Figure 4.1, 
tamoxifen is metabolized through several different pathways. 
The metabolites generated in humans show a great differ-
ence in their toxicity potential. Cytochrome P450 (CYP450) 
enzymes, flavin-containing monooxygenase (FMO), and uri-
dine diphosphate (UDP)-glucuronosyltransferases (UGTs) 
convert tamoxifen to polar metabolites that are efficiently 
excreted from the body. By contrast, alternative metabolic 
pathways involving CYP3A4 and sulfotransferases (SULTs) 
generate reactive intermediates that form DNA or protein 
adducts. It is generally accepted that the formation of DNA 
or protein adducts is mechanistically linked to downstream 
toxic effects, which is considered to be the culprit for tamox-
ifen hepatotoxicity. Thus, for new drug development, it is 
critical to understand parameters controlling the metabolic 
fate of a new chemical entity in species used for nonclinical 
testing and in humans.
As pointed out by Paracelsus in the 16th century, “All 
substances are poisons; there is none which is not a poison. 
The right dose differentiates a poison and a remedy”, adverse 
effects exerted by xenobiotics or their metabolites depend on 
their intrinsic pharmacological or toxicological potency and 
exposure (concentration level and duration at the target organ). 
Thus, several factors can affect the likelihood of toxicity 
occurring as a result of metabolism:
•	 Primary metabolites generated from a parent com-
pound have toxicological or pharmacological activ-
ity, causing off-target or on-target toxicity.
•	 Reactive intermediates formed during the biotrans-
formation steps, albeit transient, covalently attack 
macromolecular components, such as proteins, 
resulting in altered cellular function or oxidative 
stress.

Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
•	 In vivo disposition of a xenobiotic (or endogenous 
substrate), known as the “victim”, is changed by 
another xenobiotic (known as the “perpetrator”), 
resulting in elevated in vivo exposures of the “vic-
tim” above the safety margin threshold.
•	 Genetic polymorphism characterized by ultrarapid 
or poor metabolism of a given therapeutic dose of 
xenobiotics can lead to drastically different in vivo 
exposures of the parent xenobiotic or its metabolites 
among treated individuals.
Biotransformation of xenobiotics is generally classified as 
Phase I and Phase II metabolism. Common Phase I and Phase 
II metabolism pathways, metabolizing enzymes, and their 
locations are listed in Table 4.1. Phase I reactions are con-
sidered “functionalization reactions” that alter the functional 
groups in the structures of the parent compounds. Metabolic 
reactions catalyzed by Phase I enzymes include oxidation, 
reduction, and hydrolysis. Oxidation is commonly catalyzed 
by CYP450s, FMOs, monoamine oxidases, alcohol dehydro-
genases (ADHs), and others. Examples of enzymes involved 
in reduction pathways are CYP450s, carbonyl reductase, and 
NADPH: quinone reductase. Hydrolysis reactions are typi-
cally mediated by esterases and hydrolases. While Phase I 
metabolism introduces polar functionality directly on the 
parent molecules, Phase II metabolism can act on parent mol-
ecules or their Phase I metabolites. The reactions catalyzed 
by Phase II metabolic enzymes are conjugations of substrates 
with relatively large structural moieties (e.g., sugars, sulfates, 
or amino acids) added to parent molecule structures. These 
chemical side chains are usually ionized at physiological pH, 
facilitating subsequent excretion through renal or hepatobi-
liary pathways. Enzymes involved in Phase II metabolism 
mainly include UGTs, SULTs, glutathione S-transferases 
(GSTs), N-acetyltransferases (NATs), and methyltransferases. 
Phase II metabolites can also be further metabolized through 
additional steps of Phase I metabolism.3 A 2012 survey of 
over 1,000 different compounds revealed that 70% of these 
substrates are metabolized by Phase I enzymes as the first step 
in biotransformation. The remaining 30% are directly metab-
olized by Phase II conjugation without going through Phase 
I biotransformation.2 Oxidation and glucuronidation are the 
major Phase I and Phase II reactions, respectively.
Phase I metabolites in certain instances retain pharmaco-
logical and toxicological activities. Conjugated metabolites 
from Phase II metabolism are unlikely to be pharmacologi-
cally or toxicologically active with exceptions. This concept is 
generally true except for prodrug metabolism. (A prodrug is a 
pharmacologically inactive chemical derivative of a drug that 
requires biotransformation to release the active drug. Prodrugs 
are designed to overcome pharmaceutical and/or pharmaco-
kinetic limitations of the active drug.) Although a majority of 
metabolites have little pharmacological or toxicological activ-
ity, approximately 7% of metabolites are toxic or chemically 
reactive.2 Clearly, biotransformation of xenobiotics allows for 
both detoxification and safe clearance of parent compounds 
and metabolites in most cases. Among the toxic metabolites or 
chemically reactive intermediates, Phase I metabolic bioacti-
vation, particularly the pathways involving quinone formation, 
accounts for 89% of the toxic reactive metabolites, whereas 
Phase II conjugation account for the other 11%.2 The risk of 
toxic or reactive metabolite formation must be carefully con-
sidered and weighed against the therapeutic benefit to avoid 
unnecessary adverse drug reactions associated with metabo-
lism. The frequency or severity of such adverse drug reactions 
is often difficult to monitor and frequently not detected until a 
later stage of drug development.
Most of the functionalizations (Phase I metabolism) and 
conjugations (Phase II metabolism) of xenobiotics and drugs 
occur in the liver, though many extrahepatic tissues such as 
the intestine, lung, kidney, brain, and blood also contribute to 
xenobiotic metabolism. Drug transporters are the key deter-
minant of xenobiotics entry into and exit from cells. Since 
transporters do not introduce structural changes on xenobiot-
ics, transporters will not be discussed in this chapter. However, 
the importance of drug transporters in the disposition of 
FIGURE 4.1  Metabolism pathways of tamoxifen and differential outcomes.

146
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
xenobiotics has been increasingly acknowledged in recent 
years.3–6 Metabolizing enzymes can be induced or inhibited 
by a large number of different chemicals, such as drugs, pesti-
cides, nutritional supplements, industrial chemicals, and pol-
lutants. Enzyme induction is a process in which the expression 
of an enzyme (amount of enzyme protein) is induced by xeno-
biotics, leading to an increase in metabolic activity. Induction 
of a metabolizing enzyme typically results in decreased in 
vivo exposures to the parent compound being metabolized and 
increased metabolites. Inhibition of a metabolizing enzyme, on 
the other hand, refers to a decrease in enzyme activity that can 
significantly increase the exposure to the parent compound. 
Induction and reversible enzyme inhibition can be transient 
in duration. When inducers or inhibitors are eliminated from 
the body, enzymatic activities return to background levels. In 
the case of irreversible inhibition, modulation of enzymatic 
activity is more sustained, and will not be restored until new 
enzymes are synthesized in vivo. Another important aspect 
is that genetic polymorphisms also alter enzymatic activities. 
Variant alleles of xenobiotic-metabolizing genes can result in 
different levels of expression or encode enzymes with altered 
enzymatic activities. Variants can be classified as functionally 
deficient or enhanced with respect to the metabolism of xeno-
biotics and endogenous substances. Allelic variance is com-
monly observed for many drug-metabolizing enzymes, such as 
CYP450s, UGTs, and GSTs. As a result, inter-subject variabil-
ity in enzyme activities can potentially introduce significant 
adverse effects of drugs (see 4.5 for more details).
4.3  Phase I Metabolism
The primary enzymatic reactions involved in Phase I metabo-
lism include oxidation, reduction, and hydrolysis, which result 
in the “functionalization” of the parent compound with new 
hydrophilic moieties, such as –NH2, –COOH, and –OH. These 
metabolic reactions are usually catalyzed by CYP450s in the 
liver and extrahepatic organs. Other enzymes such as FMOs, 
aldehyde oxidase (AO), and monoamine oxidases can also con-
tribute to Phase I metabolic reactions. Oxidation is the most 
common Phase I metabolic reaction. Typical oxidative reac-
tions include hydroxylation of aromatic or aliphatic carbon 
centers, dealkylation of C-N, C-S, and C-O bonds, oxidation 
of N-C and S-C bonds, epoxidation of unsaturated carbons as 
well as dehalogenation. Phase I reduction typically occurs on 
carbonyl, azo, and N-oxide groups. Reduction can also take 
place in halogen centers of some drugs, such as halothane.7 
Hydrolysis occurs at function groups such as amide, ether, and 
ester, which are commonly exploited in prodrug design.

Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
4.3.1  CYP450-Dependent Monooxygenase System
The majority of Phase I metabolizing enzymes belong to the 
CYP450 superfamily. Discovered more than 60 years ago, 
CYP450 enzymes are membrane-bound proteins present in the 
endoplasmic reticulum and mitochondria within a cell (“cyto”). 
They contain a heme pigment (“chrome”) and absorb light at a 
wavelength of 450 nm (“P450”) when they are complexed with 
carbon monoxide. These hemoprotein enzymes range from 45 
to 60 kDa in size and are found in many tissues. Partnering with 
other endoplasmic reticulum membrane-bound proteins such 
as NADPH-CYP450 reductase and cytochrome b5, CYP450s 
function as monooxygenases that incorporate one oxygen 
atom from molecular oxygen into substrates. They catalyze 
the metabolism of the majority of xenobiotics and contribute 
to the biosynthesis of physiologically important compounds 
including steroid hormones, cholesterol, bile acids, and some 
fat-soluble vitamins. In humans, six isoforms of CYP450s, 
namely CYP1A2, CYP2C9, CYP2C19, CYP2D6, CYP2E1, 
and CYP3A4, are responsible for the metabolism of ~80% of 
drugs in clinical use.8
The first three-dimensional crystal structure of a mam-
malian CYP450 protein was identified in 1985. Since then, 
crystal structures of more than a dozen CYP450s have been 
reported.9,10 The active site of CYP450 contains a heme-iron 
center. The overall three-dimensional structure approximates 
a triangular shape with a relatively planar heme group nearly 
parallel to the plane of the triangle. The heme prosthetic group 
is linked to the protein via both a cysteine thiolate ligand and 
a network of hydrogen bonds among its propionate side chains 
and polar amino acid residues of the protein. The heme group 
is deeply embedded within the hydrophobic structure with no 
significant exposure to the protein surface, a structure expected 
for optimal binding and catalytic reactions with lipophilic sub-
strates. A few important catalytic residues, such as threonine 
(Thr252), glutamic acid (E366), and aspartic acid (D251), are 
highly conserved throughout the CYP450 superfamily. Despite 
the highly conversed structural similarity among CYP450 
enzymes, there is tremendous diversity within the superfamily 
in terms of chemical selectivity and substrate specificity. The 
size of CYP450 substrates ranges from molecules as small as 
ethanol (substrate of CYP2E1) to hexapeptide-peptide carrier 
protein conjugate (substrate of CYP165B1).11 The chemical 
selectivity of different CYP450s also varies from non-specific 
(e.g., human CYP3A4) to highly regio- and stereo-specific 
(e.g., CYP450cam also called CYP101). Within CYP450 
enzymes, there exist multiple domains comprising sequence 
recognition sites that are important in determining substrate 
specificity for different CYP450s.12
Microsomal CYP450s reside in the endoplasmic reticulum. 
Their redox partners, NADPH-dependent CYP450 reductase 
and cytochrome b5, are membrane-bound proteins localized in 
the endoplasmic reticulum membrane. Interactions of CYP450s 
with NADPH-CYP450 reductase and cytochrome b5 occur 
within the lipid membrane. With respect to their protein-pro-
tein and protein-membrane interactions of CYP450 function, 
the molecular organization among CYP450, cytochrome b5, 
and NADPH-CYP450 reductase is not well understood. X-ray 
crystallographers faced challenges associated with the complex 
binding interactions among proteins, lipids, cholesterol, poly-
saccharides, and water, in addition to the intrinsic proper-
ties of the cell membrane.13 Nuclear magnetic resonance and 
computation methods revealed details of protein organization, 
particularly the orientation and stabilization of the proximal 
and distal regions of CYP450 proteins in the lipid membrane.14 
Studies have shown that NADPH-dependent CYP450 reduc-
tase moves in and out of the membrane, a response referred to 
as helicoptering. In the presence of CYP450, the helicoptering 
movement around the lipid membrane is significantly reduced 
or diminished, signifying the binding between CYP450 and 
the reductase. Data also indicate the heterogeneous nature of 
the endoplasmic reticulum with highly ordered regions con-
taining cholesterol and more disordered regions. Intriguingly, 
the closely related CYP1A1 and CYP1A2 reside in different 
regions of the endoplasmic reticulum, further highlighting the 
importance of CYP450 protein organization to their function. 
While the bulk of CYP450-catalyzed reactions comprises 
oxidative reactions that include hydroxylation, dealkylation, 
N-oxidation, S-oxidation, epoxidation, and dehalogenation, 
these enzymes can also catalyze reduction, ring expansion, 
hydrolysis, deformylation, and peroxidation. Altogether, more 
than 20 different reactions can be catalyzed by enzymes in 
CYP450 superfamily.15–20
4.3.2  Biochemistry of CYP450 Reaction
The reaction catalyzed by the CYP450-dependent mono-
oxygenase system and its stoichiometry are illustrated in 
Figure 4.2. The catalytic reaction requires NADPH-CYP450 
reductase. In the presence of CYP450s, one molecule of the 
substrate (RH) reacts with molecular oxygen and NADPH to 
yield an oxidized substrate containing one atom from molecu-
lar oxygen (ROH), water (containing the other oxygen atom), 
and NADP+. The term monooxygenase is derived from the 
incorporation of one oxygen atom from molecular oxygen into 
the substrate.21 A given substrate can often bind to the active 
sites of different CYP450s, albeit with different affinities. As a 
result, multiple CYP450s could be involved in the metabolism 
of a given substrate. For example, as shown in Figure 4.3, at 
least four different CYP450 enzymes (CYP2C9, 2C19, 2C8, 
and 3A4) are involved in the oxidation of specific carbon resi-
dues in the S-warfarin.22 Multiple substrates can also bind to 
different active sites of a given CYP450, inducing a conforma-
tional change of the enzyme’s active site. Midazolam and tes-
tosterone both bind to CYP3A4 but at different regions within 
the active site.23
Figure 4.4 illustrates the catalytic cycle of CYP450. The ini-
tial step of the cycle is binding of the substrate (RH) to the 
heme group in the oxidized (Fe3+) resting state of CYP450 to 
FIGURE 
4.2  Global reaction and stoichiometry of CYP450.

form a substrate-CYP450 complex (Fe3+:RH) (step A). In step 
B, the Fe3+:RH complex is reduced to Fe2+:RH by NADPH-
CYP450 reductase which is responsible for electron transfer 
from NADPH to the substrate-CYP450 complex. In step C, 
molecular oxygen binds to the reduced Fe2+:RH complex to 
form Fe2+-O2:RH. In step D, the Fe2+-O2:RH tertiary complex 
accepts another electron transferred from NADPH-CYP450 
reductase and/or NADH-cytochrome b5 to form a peroxy 
CYP450-substrate complex (Fe2+-O2
−: RH). The peroxy com-
plex is rapidly protonated twice (step E) to form RH:Fe3+=O. In 
step F, a radical intermediate (R • :Fe3+-OH) is formed through 
hydrogen atom abstraction (or one electron abstraction). In step 
G, one atom of oxygen is introduced into the substrate to form 
an oxidized substrate product, and the regenerated oxidized 
ferric CYP450 (Fe3+) is ready for the next catalytic cycle.
4.3.3  Family of CYP450 and Their Role in 
Drug Metabolism and Toxicity
CYP450s are among the ubiquitous enzymes in nature and exist 
in most species, including microorganisms. They are catego-
rized based on their amino acid sequences. In humans, there 
are 57 putatively functional CYP450 enzymes grouped into 18 
families and 44 subfamilies. CYP450s that share at least 40% 
amino acid identity are considered members of the same fam-
ily and denoted by a numerical number after the CYP acro-
nym, for example, CYP1, CYP2, and CYP3. The subfamily of 
each CYP450 family shares 40%–70% of amino acid sequence 
identity, designated by a letter after the family number, for 
example, CYP1A, CYP2C, and CYP3A. Last, individual iso-
forms that share >70% of amino acid identity are differenti-
ated by another numerical value at the end, such as CYP1A1, 
CYP2C9, and CYP3A4. A few key CYP450 isoforms, such 
as CYP1A2, CYP2C9, CYP2C19, CYP2D6, CYP2E1, and 
FIGURE 4.4  Catalytic cycle of CYP450-mediated substrate oxidation. Abbreviations: NADP+, nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate; 
NADPH, reduced form of NADP+.
FIGURE 4.3  CYP450s involved in oxidation of specific carbon residues in S-warfarin.

Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
CYP3A4, are responsible for the metabolism of 95% of drugs 
and other xenobiotics.24–26 These crucial CYP450 subfamilies 
and isoforms are the focus of this chapter. The structural and 
enzymatic characteristics of other CYP450s can be found in 
other publications.16,27 CYP450s are present in many tissues 
with the highest abundance and largest number of individual 
isoforms being expressed in the liver. Total CYP450 contents 
in different human tissues are shown in Figure 4.5.28 Due to 
the high-level expression of CYP450s in the liver, the bulk 
of systemic clearance of xenobiotics is hepatic. As shown in 
Figure 4.6, CYP3A has the highest substrate promiscuity and 
is also the most abundant CYP450 in the liver, followed by 
2C9, 2E1, 1A2, 2A6, 2C8, 2C19, 2D6, 2B6, and others (not 
listed). CYP450s are also present significantly in the small 
intestines with 3A4 as the most abundant isozyme, and less 
abundant CYP450s in the intestine include 2C9, 1A1, and 
others. Extrahepatic clearances via intestinal CYP450s can 
therefore contribute significantly to drug metabolism, espe-
cially for oral drugs. Figure 4.7 illustrates the fraction of dif-
ferent CYP450 isoforms involved in xenobiotic metabolism.25 
Among 248 drugs with known CYP450 involvement in their 
biotransformation pathways, CYP3A4/5 appeared to be the 
predominant isoforms responsible for Phase I metabolism. 
Other CYP450s also contributed in the order of 2D6 > 2C9 > 
1A2 > 2B6 > 2C19 > 2C8 > 2A6 > 2J2. There has been particu-
lar interest in the metabolism contribution by CYP2C8 and 
2J2 in the last decade. Among the top 200 prescribed drugs 
in the United States, fractions of drugs metabolized by dif-
ferent CYP450 isoforms in 200229 and 201430 were compared 
(Figure 4.7). There appeared to have been little change in the 
fraction metabolized by CYP3A4, 2C19, and 1A2 in 2014 
compared to a decade ago. A slight decrease in the fraction of 
CYP2C9 contribution and a noticeable increase in the metabo-
lism contribution due to CYP2D6 and CYP2C8 were observed 
(Figure 4.8). 
4.3.3.1  The CYP1 Family
There are two important isoforms in CYP450 family 1, 
CYP1A1 and CYP1A2. They are responsible for the metabo-
lism of less than 10% of market drugs.
4.3.3.1.1  CYP1A1 and CYP1A2
CYP1A1, an aryl hydrocarbon hydroxylase, is known to 
metabolize PAHs, amides, arylamines, and mycotoxins (e.g., 
aflatoxin B1).31 The metabolic activation of PAHs by CYP1A1 
FIGURE 4.6  Fraction of different CYP450 isoforms involved in xenobiotic metabolism.
FIGURE 4.5  Abundance of CYP450s in human liver and intestines.

150
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
is considered to play an important role in human carcino-
genesis initiated by environmental pollutants.32 For example, 
CYP1A1-mediated epoxidation of benzo[a]pyrene leads to 
carcinogen benzo[a]pyrene-7,8-dihydrodiol-9,10-epoxide that 
can covalently form adducts with cellular macromolecules 
(Figure 4.9). CYP1A1 is mainly expressed extrahepatically 
with the highest levels in the gastrointestinal tract, placenta, 
lung, and skin.33 Unlike CYP1A1, CYP1A2 is predomi-
nately a hepatic enzyme that accounts for 11% of CYP450s 
content in the human liver. CYP1A2 primarily metabolizes 
arylamines and heterocyclic arylamines, such as caffeine, 
phenacetin, lidocaine, tacrine, and theophylline. Good sub-
strates for CYP1A2 tend to be planar molecules containing 
one or more hydrogen-bond donors.34 CYP1A2 can bioacti-
vate (N-oxidize) certain arylamines to metabolites that are 
potent mutagenic or carcinogenic compounds.17 Two examples 
of enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP1A2 are provided 
in Figure 4.10. Tacrine, a potent acetylcholinesterase inhibi-
tor for Alzheimer’s disease treatment, is a standard CYP1A2 
substrate. Tacrine was removed from the U.S. market in 2013 
due to hepatotoxicity. Metabolism of tacrine by CYP1A2 leads 
to the formation of toxic quinone methide species that can 
form irreversible adducts with cellular components, resulting 
in hepatocellular necrosis.35 Besides xenobiotics, endogenous 
substances including melatonin, phosphatidylcholine, uro-
porphyrinogen, pregnenolone, progesterone, estradiol, and 
estrogen are also substrates of CYP1A. Polymorphism of 
CYP1A1/2 exists, and some are suggested to correlate with a 
number of chronic human diseases, including subject suscepti-
bility to lung and breast cancer.36–39 Both CYP1A isoforms are 
highly inducible by PAHs through aryl hydrocarbon receptor 
(AhR), and other inducers include phenobarbital and phenyt-
oin. Inhibitors of CYP1A2 include flufylline, cimetidine, and 
tolfenamic acid.
4.3.3.2  The CYP2 Family
CYP2 family contains the largest number of CYP450s with 
13 subfamilies and 16 functional genes. Important CYP2 
isoforms responsible for metabolism of xenobiotics include 
CYP2A6, CYP2B6, CYP2C8, CYP2C9, CYP2C19, CYP2D6, 
CYP2E1, and CYP2J2. Polymorphism in the CYP2C family 
(i.e., CYP2C9, CYP2C19, and CYP2D6) is the most exten-
sively studied within CYP450 superfamily.
4.3.3.2.1  CYP2A6
CYP2A6 is mainly expressed in human liver and accounts 
for approximately 9% of human hepatic CYP450. It is also 
present in the lung, skin, and nasal membrane where meta-
bolic activation of tobacco-related carcinogens is believed to 
occur.40,41 Substrates of CYP2A6, including nicotine, quino-
line, cotinine, acetaminophen, and coumarin, tend to have 
small, nonplanar aromatic ring structures.42 In addition, bili-
rubin and procarcinogens (aminochrysene and aflatoxin B1) 
are also metabolized by CYP2A6. CYP2A6 enzyme can be 
induced by phenobarbital and rifampicin. Approximately 
0.3% of African Americans and 1.4% of Caucasians are poor 
metabolizer phenotype, characterized by low or nonexistent 
7-hydroxylation of coumarin.43 Genetic variations have also 
been observed in individuals of Japanese descent, with a sig-
nificant portion of the population (~50%) lacking or showing 
reduced CYP2A6 activity.7 Examples of enzymatic reactions 
catalyzed by CYP2A6 are provided in Figure 4.11. Inhibitors 
of CYP2A6 include 8-methoxypsoralen, tranylcypromine, and 
selegiline, and example inducers are phenobarbital, rifampi-
cin, and estrogens.

Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
4.3.3.2.2  CYP2B6
Expression of CYP2B6 in human liver is low. It is also found 
in the gastrointestinal tract, lungs, and brain.41,44 CYP2B6 con-
tributes to the metabolism of approximately 2% of marketed 
drugs. The common structural characteristics of CYP2B6 
substrates include medium-size, angular, neutral, or weakly 
basic molecules with one or two hydrogen-bond acceptors.42 
Example substrates include S-mephenytoin, bupropion, efavi-
renz, and nicotine. CYP2B6 is inducible by St. John’s wort and 
drugs such as dexamethasone, phenobarbital, phenytoin, and 
rifampicin. It can be inhibited by 2-phenyl-2-(1-piperdinyl)
propane, thiotepa, and mifepristone. CYP2B6 polymorphism 
has been reported, and some genetic mutations (e.g., C1459T) 
are frequently associated with low 2B6 protein expression and 
poor metabolism of S-mephenytoin.45 Examples of enzymatic 
reactions catalyzed by CYP2B6 are provided in Figure 4.12.
4.3.3.2.3  CYP2C8
CYP2C subfamily has three important isoforms, CYP2C8, 
CYP2C9, and CYP2C19. Together, these isoforms are esti-
mated to account for the metabolism of approximately 24% 
of marketed drugs. Different isoforms in the CYP2C family 
share approximately over 80% amino acid sequence, how-
ever, each exhibits significant substrate specificity.46 During 
the past decade, CYP2C8 has emerged as an important 
drug-metabolizing enzyme. More drugs are found to be sub-
strates of CYP2C8, including those initially not considered 
to be metabolized by CYP2C8, e.g., amodiaquine (ADQ), 
FIGURE 4.11  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2A6.
FIGURE 4.10  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP1A2.

153
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
paclitaxel, repaglinide, pioglitazone, and montelukast.47 The 
number of drugs that are metabolized by CYP2C8 is continu-
ously increasing.48 CYP2C8 is primarily a hepatic enzyme 
that metabolizes substrates with a large, elongated structure. 
Examples of enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2C8 are 
provided in Figure 4.13. Most of the CYP2C8 substrates are 
also substrates of other CYP450 enzymes such as CYP3A4 
and CYP2C9. ADQ, an antimalaria drug widely popular in 
Africa, can cause liver toxicity that is attributed to the metabo-
lism of ADQ by CYP2C8 in the liver. CYP2C8 bioactivates 
ADQ to its major metabolite N-desethylamodiaquine which 
can form protein adducts, a process that likely contributes to 
the hepatotoxicity observed in humans.49 Using cell lines over-
expressed with CYP2C8, the cytotoxicity of ADQ was mark-
edly increased compared to the control group. Co-treatment 
of CYP2C8 inhibitor and ADQ significantly decreased the 
cytotoxicity induced by ADQ. Thus, it is highly likely that 
CYP2C8-mediated metabolism of ADQ is one major con-
tributor to hepatotoxicity in humans.50 In vitro studies using 
human hepatocytes revealed that CYP2C8 is the most induc-
ible member of CYP2C subfamily. Phenobarbital, hyperforin, 
and rifampin have been shown to increase CYP2C8 expres-
sion at mRNA and protein levels as well as enzymatic activity. 
Some CYP2C8 inhibitors include montelukast, gemfibrozil, 
and trimethoprim. Polymorphisms in CYP2C8 gene have been 
reported and linked to certain disease states or adverse drug 
reactions.51
4.3.3.2.4  CYP2C9
Approximately 9% of marketed drugs are metabolized by 
CYP2C9.30,52,53 In the liver and intestines, CYP2C9 accounts 
for 18% and 11% of total human CYP450 enzymes, respec-
tively. Common substrates of CYP2C9 are medium-size, 
lipophilic, and weak acids with one to two hydrogen-bond 
donors, such as warfarin, barbiturate sedatives, phenytoin, 
tolbutamide, fluvastatin, diclofenac, ibuprofen, and losar-
tan. Examples of enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2C9 
are provided in Figure 4.14. Diclofenac is a model substrate 
of CYP2C9 with 4′-hydroxydiclofenac as one of the major 
metabolites. There are reports of serious idiosyncratic hepa-
totoxicity due to diclofenac metabolism. The quinoneimine 
formed during diclofenac metabolism is considered to be one 
of the causes responsible for its hepatotoxicity.54 CYP2C9 
is inducible by classic inducers such as rifampicin and phe-
nobarbital through pregnane X receptor (PXR).55 CYP2C9 
can be inhibited by sulfaphenazole, tienilic acid, and fluco-
nazole. Functional CYP2C9 polymorphisms are rare (0.25% 
in Caucasians), but with severe clinical consequences.56,57 For 
example, poor metabolizers of CYP2C9 can have the risk of 
life-threatening bleeding episodes after the use of warfarin.58
4.3.3.2.5  CYP2C19
CYP2C19 accounts for approximately 2.7% and 1.3% of total 
CYP450s in the human liver and intestines, respectively. 
As one of the primary metabolizing enzymes, CYP2C19 is 
involved in the metabolism of approximately 11% of mar-
keted drugs including S-mephenytoin, diazepam, omeprazole, 
R-warfarin, and clomipramine. CYP2C19 substrates are typi-
cally moderately lipophilic with two to three hydrogen-bond 
acceptors. Examples of enzymatic reactions catalyzed by 
CYP2C19 are provided in Figure 4.15. CYP2C19 polymor-
phism was found in approximately 3%–5% of Caucasians and 
up to 20% of Asians. Genetic polymorphism of CYP2C19 can 
have a significant impact on drugs with narrow therapeutic 
indexes.59,60 CYP2C19 is inducible by rifampicin, dicloxacil-
lin, efavirenz, barbiturates, and St. John’s wort.61–63 It can be 
inhibited by naringenin, omeprazole, and ticlopidine.
4.3.3.2.6  CYP2D6
CYP2D6, also known as debrisoquine hydroxylase, is the most 
important isoform in the CYP2D subfamily,64 particularly 
with respect to pharmacogenetics. It is one of the most impor-
tant CYP450 isoforms for xenobiotics metabolism, responsible 
for 21% of marketed drug metabolism. Expressed primarily in 
FIGURE 4.12  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2B6.

154
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
the liver and at a low level in the intestines, CYP2D6 is esti-
mated to account for 2.1% and 0.7% of total hepatic and intes-
tinal CYP450s, respectively. CYP2D6 is also expressed in the 
central nervous system with the highest level observed in the 
cerebellum. CYP2D6 has been shown to have an important 
physiological role due to its involvement in neurotransmitter 
biotransformation.65–68 CYP2D6 displays a substrate prefer-
ence for basic, medium-size molecules with 5–7 Å distance 
FIGURE 4.14  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2C9.
FIGURE 4.13  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2C8.

155
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
between the basic nitrogen and the site of oxidation.69 Typical 
substrates of CYP2D6 include antidepressants (amitriptyline, 
fluoxetine, Prozac), antiarrhythmic agents (flecainide, propafe-
none, sparteine), β-blockers (propranolol), antipsychotics (e.g., 
thioridazine), antihypertensive agents (e.g., debrisoquine), and 
opioids. CYP2D6 also regulates endogenous substrates such 
as neurotransmitters, neurosteroids, pinoline, progesterone, 
and lipids.70 Examples of enzymatic reactions catalyzed by 
CYP2D6 are provided in Figure 4.16. CYP2D6 is the human 
isoform that is not regulated by classic CYP450 inducers. 
It can be inhibited by quinidine, bupropion, and haloperi-
dol. CYP2D6 is highly polymorphic in the human popula-
tion with marked inter-racial variations, which include poor 
metabolizers (6% of Caucasians and is less common in Asian 
and African populations) and ultrarapid metabolizers (4% of 
Caucasians and up to 29% and 21% of Ethiopian and Saudi 
Arabian populations, respectively).71 CYP2D6 polymorphism 
and susceptibility have been correlated with certain neurode-
generative diseases and lung cancers.72
4.3.3.2.7  CYP2E1
CYP2E1 accounts for the metabolism of 2% of total marketed 
drugs and has been found in the liver, lung, placenta, and 
brain. In the liver, approximately 15% of the total CYP450s 
belong to CYP2E1. Similar to the CYP1A subfamily, CYP2E1 
is responsible for the metabolism of many known environ-
mental chemicals and toxicants, such as organic solvents (e.g., 
acetone),73,74 procarcinogens in tobacco smoke,75 and xenobi-
otics (e.g., nicotine, acetaminophen, chlorzoxazone, and phe-
nobarbital). Typical substrates of CYP2E1 are low molecular 
weight, relatively planar, and hydrophilic molecules. CYP2E1 
plays an important role in ethanol metabolism, particularly at 
elevated concentrations and during chronic ethanol consump-
tion.76 Examples of enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2B6 
FIGURE 4.15  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2C19.
FIGURE 4.16  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2D6.

156
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
are provided in Figure 4.17. The bioactivation by CYP2E1 
involves the generation of reactive oxygen species (ROS) that 
could introduce liver damage. As a result, CYP2E1 is impli-
cated as a causative enzyme in alcoholic liver disease, nonal-
coholic fatty liver diseases, and nonalcoholic steatohepatitis. 
In addition, acetaminophen-mediated hepatotoxicity has also 
been associated with metabolic bioactivation by CYP2E1. 
CYP2E1 is also implicated in the metabolism of halothane to 
its reactive metabolite that can acetylate liver proteins, leading 
to the stimulation of immune reactions and subsequent hepatic 
necrosis.77 CYP2E1 is inducible by many molecules includ-
ing ethanol and nicotine.78,79 Interestingly, many substrates of 
CYP2E1, such as pyridine and isoniazid, also induce CYP2E1. 
CYP2E1 inhibitors include diethyldithiocarbamate, disulfi-
ram, and 4-methylpyrazole. Polymorphism is more common 
in Asian populations than in Caucasians80 and has been linked 
with urinary cancer susceptibility.81
4.3.3.2.8  CYP2J2
CYP2J2 is the only isoform in CYP2J family. It has drawn par-
ticular attention in the past decade due to its role in epoxidizing 
arachidonic acid regioselectively to epoxyeicosatrienoic acids 
(EETs).82 These EETs are important regulators in angiogen-
esis, vasodilation, as well as protection against hypoxia-reox-
ygenation injury and doxorubicin-induced cardiotoxicity.83–85 
CYP2J2 is primarily expressed extrahepatically. It is the most 
abundant epoxygenase in the heart and plays a central and 
multifaceted role in cardiomyocyte homeostasis.86 CYP2J2 
can also be found in the skeletal, muscle, placenta, small 
intestines, kidney, lung, and brain.87,88 The subcellular loca-
tion of CYP2J2 is believed to be in the endoplasmic reticulum. 
In silico modeling suggested structural similarity between 
CYP2J2 and CYP3A4 which may explain a large overlap in 
the substrates of these two enzymes.86 CYP2J2-mediated bio-
transformation tends to be more restricted to a single location 
on the substrate structure, as compared to CYP3A4 which 
commonly metabolizes substrates at multiple locations.89 
Typical substrates include antihistamines, the anticancer drug 
tamoxifen, and other drugs such as cyclosporine. Examples 
of enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2J2 are provided 
in Figure 4.18. CYP2J2 inducers have not been identified. 
Some of the inhibitors include danazol, ketoconazole, and 
terfenadine. CYP2J2 is polymorphic. Functionally relevant 
genetic variation in CYP2J2 level has been associated with an 
increased risk of coronary artery disease.90 CYP2J2 can be 
inhibited by danazol, ketoconazole, and terfenadine.25,91
4.3.3.3  The CYP3 Family
The CYP3A subfamily is the most abundant of all the CYP450 
subfamilies in the human liver and intestines. There are four 
functional genes (CYP3A4, CYP3A5, CYP3A7, CYP3A43) 
and two pseudogenes (3A5P1, 3A5P2) in the CYP3A family. 
CYP3A7 is mainly found in neonates and is downregulated 
soon after birth with negligible enzymatic activity in adult-
hood. The enzymatic function of CYP3A43 is less understood 
than other CYP3A isoforms. CYP3A5 is expressed in the liver 
and extrahepatic tissues such as the lung and kidney. Among 
all CYP450s, CYP3A4 is the most important isoform and is 
responsible for the metabolism of approximately one-third 
of the marketed drugs and many endogenous compounds. 
CYP3A4 is predominantly expressed in the liver and at low lev-
els in the small intestines. It constitutes about 30%–40% of the 
total CYP450s in the liver and approximately 60% of the total 
CYP450s in the human intestines. Due to the large expression 
in the human small intestines, first-pass metabolism within 
enterocytes can significantly reduce the oral bioavailability 
of drugs that are substrates of CYP3A4. As a result, inhibi-
tion of CYP3A4 in the intestines could significantly increase 
circulating exposures of CYP3A4 substrates.92 CYP3A4 is 
also expressed in other organs such as the lung and kidneys. 
Lipophilic and large acid, basic, and neutral compounds can 
be good CYP3A4 substrates. Examples of CYP3A4 substrates 
include anticancer drugs (e.g., Taxol); calcium channel blockers 
(e.g., nifedipine, verapamil), antihistamines (e.g., astemizole, 
terfenadine), HMG CoA reductase inhibitors (e.g., lovastatin, 
atorvastatin), antiviral agents (e.g., ritonavir and saquinavir), 
antiarrhythmic (e.g., quinidine), sedatives (e.g., diazepam and 
midazolam), antibiotics (e.g., erythromycin), and immunosup-
pressants (e.g., cyclosporine). CYP3A4 also plays an important 
role in modulating endogenous steroids such as testosterone. 
Multiple binding sites are available within the structure of 
FIGURE 4.17  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2E1.

157
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
CYP3A4. For example, the classic CYP3A4 substrates, mid-
azolam and testosterone, are metabolized by different active 
sites within CYP3A4. Simultaneous bindings of multiple 
ligands were also observed with increased CYP3A4 enzy-
matic activity through heterotrophic cooperativity.93 Examples 
of enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP3A4 are provided in 
Figure 4.19. CYP3A4 is susceptible to enzyme induction, and 
a number of drugs are known to be potent CYP3A4 inducers, 
such as rifampin, carbamazepine, and phenytoin. Some drugs, 
such as ketoconazole, clarithromycin, and itraconazole, are 
potent inhibitors of CYP3A4, and individuals receiving these 
drugs may have significantly decreased CYP3A4 activity. 
Grapefruit juice is also a potent CYP3A4 inhibitor. CYP3A4 
and 3A5 typically have overlapping substrates but differ in 
their catalytic efficiency.94 CYP3A5 is expressed polymorphi-
cally in adult liver.95
4.3.4  Species, Strain, and Gender Differences 
in Monooxygenase Activity
CYP450s in different species that originated and evolved from 
similar ancestral genes are called orthologous forms. Their 
nomenclature and sequence homologies may appear to be 
closely related; however, their substrate, inhibition, and induc-
tion specificity may not necessarily be similar. For example, 
CYP1A2 enzyme is conserved across species with aromatic 
amines as the typical substrates. CYP2E1 enzyme is known 
to be inducible by ethanol across species. However, for oth-
ers like CYP3A, significant species difference exists. The rat 
ortholog of CYP3A1 is not a target for the typical CYP3A 
inducer rifampicin. Bisphenol A increases CYP3A4 mRNA 
expression and enzyme activity in humans, while CYP3A 
in rats is not induced by bisphenol A.96 Many dihydropyri-
dine calcium channel blockers that are common substrates 
of human CYP3A4 are not metabolized by rat CYP3A1. The 
human CYP2D6 orthologous enzyme in the rat is CYP2D1. 
Both enzymes have different inhibition profiles, for example, 
quinidine, a specific inhibitor of human CYP2D6, has little 
inhibition activity on CYP2D1 (Table 4.2).
There are also inter-species differences in CYP450 enzymatic 
activities toward given substrate probes. Liver microsomes 
from mice, rats, rabbits, minipigs, dogs, monkeys, and humans 
were tested to assess hepatic isoform enzymatic activity with 
11 classes of CYP450 substrates. The results revealed sig-
nificant and unique hepatic drug metabolism characteristics 
among different species.98,99 Particularly, CYP450-mediated 
FIGURE 4.18  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by CYP2J2.

158
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
metabolism data in humans do not correlate more closely with 
data from monkeys than with data from mice. In fact, in vitro 
studies using microsomes indicated that the CYP450 activity 
profile in mice best resembled those in humans.
As expected, it is not uncommon that inter-species dif-
ferences in metabolism contribute significantly to species-­
specific susceptibilities to toxicities. For example, 4-ipomeanol 
is a reference compound for drug-induced lung injury100 
(Figure 4.20). The reactive enedial intermediate from the 
bioactivation of 4-ipomeanol can cause lung toxicity for farm 
animals if ingested unintentionally through contaminated 
feed.101 The underlying cause of lung toxicity in animals is due 
to CYP4B1-mediated bioactivation of 4-ipomeanol converting 
ipomeanol to a reactive enedial after epoxidation on the furan 
ring.102 In contrast, no lung toxicity was observed in humans 
because the human pulmonary CYP4B1 enzyme has no cata-
lytic activity.103 Interestingly, 4-ipomeanol-induced liver toxic-
ity was observed in humans indicating that bioactivation can 
be catalyzed by human hepatic CYP450s.3
The complexity of drug-metabolizing enzyme systems 
and inter-species differences make animal-to-human predic-
tion challenging at best. Inter-species variabilities of CYP450 
expression levels, enzymatic activities, substrate specificities, 
and biotransformation pathways all play a role in the species 
translational complexity. Species differences in metabolism 
should be carefully considered when choosing the appropriate 
animal species for toxicology studies to support human clini-
cal trials, or when assessing different toxicity responses across 
species. For example, appropriate rodent and nonrodent spe-
cies may provide adequate in vivo metabolite exposure cover-
age relevant to the amount of metabolite formed in humans. 
Once a circulating human metabolite has been deemed to be 
“disproportionate”, meaning that metabolites formed only in 
humans or present at higher levels in humans than in animal 
species, nonclinical toxicology studies of the human metabo-
lites need to be conducted to thoroughly evaluate the safety 
risk. Most Phase II conjugation metabolites can be excluded

from metabolite safety testing since they are generally consid-
ered pharmacologically inactive.104–107
Gender-dependent differences in metabolizing enzymes 
are seen across species.108,109 For example, enzymatic activi-
ties of CYP1A2 and 2E1 are found to be lower in women than 
in men.110,111 Antihistamines, typical CYP2D6 substrates, also 
exhibit slower metabolic clearance in women than in men. For 
CYP3A4, females tend to have higher activity than males. A 
clinical study indicated that women have approximately two-
fold higher levels of CYP3A4 enzyme compared to men.112 
As a result, higher amounts of metabolites from protein pump 
inhibitors are found in female subjects compared to males 
receiving the same dose of the drug.113 Another clinical analy-
sis of CYP3A4 substrate metabolism revealed that 15 out of the 
24 substrates showed higher clearance in women than in men. 
In rodents, gender-dependent toxicological or pharmacologic 
actions were observed as early as the 1930s.114–116 The phar-
macologically effective dose of barbiturates in female rats was 
half of the amount needed by male rats. The gender-dependent 
toxicological or pharmacologic effects in rats have been linked 
to the differences in the expression of hepatic enzymes. For 
example, the sex-specific CYP2C11, CYP2C13, and CYP3A2 
are expressed in males, whereas CYP2C12 is expressed pre-
dominantly in female rats only. Male rats are found to be more 
susceptible to chloroform-induced liver injury than female 
rats. The gender-specific toxicological response to chloro-
form is attributed to the fact that hepatic CYP450 isoforms 
(CYP2C11, CYP2C13, and CYP3A2) that bioactivate chlo-
roform117 are expressed specifically in males rats. Table 4.3 
summarizes major CYP450 enzyme expression preferences in 
different genders of humans and rats.
Another factor that can alter the expression of metabolizing 
enzymes is disease states. The inherent inter-subject variabil-
ity of drug-metabolizing enzymes can be further exacerbated 
by co-morbid disease. The cellular function of metaboliz-
ing enzymes including expression levels and activities can 
be impacted by pathophysiological conditions. For example, 
hepatic CYP3A4 activity is subject to variation in hepatic 
hemodynamics, hepatocellular function, nutrition, circulating 
hormones, and others.25 Acute systemic hypoxia was shown 
to upregulate CYP450 enzyme activities.123 In cancer patients, 
tumor-induced inflammation downregulates CYP450 enzyme 
activities. These immune response-mediated changes in 
CYP450 enzyme activities can pose significant risk and clini-
cal implications in dosing regimens and drug-drug interaction 
consideration.124–126
4.3.5  Induction and Inhibition of CYP450
Drug-drug interaction refers to the alternation of enzyme cata-
lytic activities due to the interaction of two or more drugs that 
are administered concurrently. The drug that alters enzyme 
activity involved in metabolizing other drugs is called “perpe-
trator”. The drug with an altered disposition due to drug-drug 
interaction is called “victim”. As a result, blood concentration 
and clearance of the victim drug can be significantly altered 
due to the nature of enzyme modulation. Enzymatic activ-
ity can be induced or inhibited by a large number of differ-
ent chemicals, such as drugs, pesticides, dietary food intakes, 
nutritional supplements, industrial chemicals, and pollut-
ants. Caution is necessary to avoid significant enzyme activ-
ity modulations when multiple drugs are co-administrated.

Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Drug-drug interaction has long been a key research consider-
ation in drug development. Drug-drug interaction has increas-
ing toxicity implications, partially because polypharmacy has 
become a common clinical practice. In patients with chronic 
diseases, managing adverse drug effects from polypharmacy 
has become a major concern for physicians and pharmacists 
and is also an important consideration for the pharmaceutical 
industry when developing new medicines. The importance of 
managing drug-drug interaction risks is well recognized by 
regulatory agencies and the pharmaceutical industry. Multiple 
regulatory guidance and whitepapers from the Pharmaceutical 
Research and Manufacturers of America are available to sup-
port these efforts.
4.3.5.1  Induction
Enzyme induction is a process in which the expression of an 
enzyme (amount of enzyme protein) is induced by xenobiot-
ics, leading to an increase in metabolic activity. For example, 
CYP3A4 can be induced by phenytoin, phenobarbital, and St. 
John’s wort. Ethanol is known to elevate CYP2E1 catalytic 
activity. PAHs and barbiturates are strong inducers of CYP1A1 
and CYP2B1/2B2, respectively. Not all forms of CYP450 are 
inducible; for example, robust inducers of 2D6 have not been 
identified. In most cases, induction of a CYP450 isoform starts 
with the binding of an inducer to the nuclear receptor of the 
isozyme to trigger transcriptional activation of the CYP450 
gene, followed by an increase in the corresponding mRNA 
level, leading to an increase in de novo protein synthesis of 
the CYP450 isoform. In some cases, enzyme induction is due 
to both transcriptional and post-transcriptional mechanisms. 
For example, induction of CYP2E1 involves both elevated pro-
duction of the enzyme and prolonged enzyme half-life.127 In 
rodents, several-fold increases in post-transcriptional mRNA 
stability have been observed following fasting or induced dia-
betes.74 Mechanistically, enzyme induction can be mediated by 
the following two groups of receptors and their heterodimer 
partners: (1) AhR and (2) nuclear receptors that include consti-
tutive androstane receptor (CAR), PXR, and peroxisome-pro-
liferator–activated receptor (PPAR). The AhR stimulates the 
transcription of CYP1 family genes. Activation of AhR results 
in the induction of CYP1A1/2, 1B1, and certain Phase II con-
jugation enzymes such as UGTs and GSTs. Nuclear receptors 
(PXR, CAR, and PPAR) regulate both Phase I (e.g., CYP450) 
and Phase II (e.g., UGT) xenobiotic-metabolizing genes.128,129 
PXR plays an important role in the induction of CYP3A by 
a number of structurally diverse xenobiotics.130 Major species 
differences exist in enzyme induction. For example, rifampi-
cin induces CYP3A in humans (3A4) and rabbits (3A6) but 
has no inducing activity in the rat (3A23).131 CAR is a key fac-
tor in the regulation of CYP2B6, 2C9, 2C19, and 3A4.132–136 
Additionally, CAR is involved in the induction of Phase II 
enzymes such as UGTs.128,137 No endogenous ligand of CAR 
and PXR has been identified, therefore, they are also called 
“orphan” nuclear receptors. Although there were reports link-
ing PPARs to the upregulation of CYP450s, the definitive 
role of PPARs in the induction of metabolizing enzymes is 
not clear.138,139 Enzyme induction is a process that typically 
takes multiple days to reach the maximal induction level after 
exposure to an inducer. Induced levels can take about the same 
amount of time to dissipate once the inducer is removed, but 
this can vary based on the half-life of the induced enzyme.140 
For example, induction of CYP3A4 by rifampicin in healthy 
volunteers takes 6 days of inducer exposure to develop, and 
11 days to return to baseline after discontinued treatment with 
the inducer (Table 4.4).141
An increase in enzyme activity implies higher and faster 
clearance of xenobiotics, which can lead to insufficient drug 
exposure, and consequently a suboptimal pharmacological 
effect at a standard therapeutic dose. In some cases, enzyme 
induction can be associated with increased toxicity if the 
enzyme-mediated metabolic pathway generates toxic inter-
mediates. For example, CYP2E1-mediated bioactivation of 
acetaminophen involves the formation of a toxic metabolite, 
N-acetyl-p-benzoquinoneimine (NAPQI), which can cause 
liver damage. Repeated administration of ethanol is associ-
ated with CYP2E1 induction and increased acetaminophen-
mediated hepatotoxicity and lethality in rodents.
4.3.5.2  Inhibition
The metabolic activity of CYP450 enzymes can be sup-
pressed by xenobiotic inhibitors. Since a majority of marketed 
drugs are metabolized in the body, inhibition of metabolizing 
enzymes typically translates to increased substrate exposure 
that can lead to undesired toxicity.142 Determinants for the 
magnitude of in vivo exposure from enzyme inhibition include 
inhibitor concentration and potency, temporal profile between 
an inhibitor and a substrate, mechanism of action, and the 
fraction of total clearance due to the specific enzymatic path-
way. In 1998, mibefradil (Posicor), a calcium channel blocker, 
was withdrawn from the U.S. market. Mibefradil is a potent 
CYP3A4 inhibitor that can cause serious adverse drug interac-
tions, including rhabdomyolysis, renal failure, or bradycardia 
in the elderly when co-administered with other drugs that are 
CYP3A4 substrates (Table 4.5).143–145
The mechanisms of CYP450 inhibition are divided into three 
categories: reversible inhibition (competitive, non-competitive, 
and uncompetitive), quasi-irreversible inhibition, and irrevers-
ible inhibition (also known as mechanism-based inhibition).5 
Reversible inhibition is due to the reversible binding of a target 
CYP450 isoenzyme with an inhibitor. Inhibitors (or “perpe-
trators”) inhibit enzyme activity by binding to the active or

161
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
allosteric sites within the enzyme structure. An inhibitor can 
also be a substrate for that same enzyme.146 Reversible inhi-
bition can be further categorized into three different types: 
competitive, non-competitive, and uncompetitive, based on 
how the victim and perpetrator drugs bind to target enzymes 
(Figure 4.21). The most common type of reversible inhibition 
is competitive inhibition. In the competitive type, an inhibi-
tor (resembles the substrate) and a substrate compete for the 
same active site of the metabolizing enzyme. In this mode, the 
inhibitor occupies the active site thus preventing the substrate 
from binding to the same site. The outcome of the competi-
tion depends on the respective affinities of the inhibitor and 
the substrate for the binding site and their concentrations in 
the proximity of the binding region. Kinetically, competitive 
inhibition decreases the affinity of a victim drug to the target 
enzyme. One example is the inhibition of CYP3A4 by cipro-
floxacin. At sufficient concentration of the ciprofloxacin, bio-
activation of erythromycin by human microsomal CYP3A4 or 
by rat microsomal CYP3A2 is significantly reduced.147 Non-
competitive inhibition occurs when an inhibitor and a sub-
strate bind reversibly and independently to the same enzyme. 
The inhibitor binds to an enzyme at an allosteric site, and the 
substrate binds to the same enzyme at the active site. They do 
not compete for the same binding site in the enzyme. Binding 
from the inhibitor results in a conformational change in the 
active site of the enzyme, which leads to reduced affinity to the 
substrate. In the case of non-competitive inhibition, reduced 
metabolic clearance on the substrate is due to a decrease 
in the maximum reaction rate of the substrate and enzyme. 
Examples of non-competitive inhibitors include fluvoxamine 
(CYP2C19) and terbinafine (CYP2D6).62,148 Both competitive 
and non-competitive inhibition can have an immediate effect 
on CYP450 enzyme activities. There is no time-dependent 
change in the level of inhibition, as long as the inhibitor con-
centration is constant. Reversible uncompetitive inhibition 
occurs when an inhibitor binds only to the enzyme-substrate 
complex and not the free enzyme. Drug-drug interactions due 
to uncompetitive inhibition are very rare.
Mechanism-based inhibition has two different classes: irre-
versible and quasi-irreversible inhibition. Both involve the 
production of metabolic intermediates that bind to the heme 
or apoprotein of CYP450s and subsequently destroy enzyme 
activities. In contrast to reversible inhibition, irreversible 
and quasi-irreversible inhibitions are time-, concentration-, 
and NADPH-dependent enzyme inactivation. The onset and 
dissipation of enzyme inhibition are typically longer than 
reversible inhibition. These inhibitors are also called suicide 
inhibitors or time-dependent inhibitors. In quasi-irreversible 
inhibition, an inhibitor is first metabolized to an intermediate 
that does not readily leave the enzyme, instead, it forms an 
enzyme-intermediate complex that disables enzymatic activ-
ity. One example is troleandomycin. The amino group in tro-
leandomycin can be oxidized by CYP3A4 to a nitroso group 
that can bind to the heme-iron center in CYP3A4, forming an 
enzyme-intermediate complex, and disabling CYP3A4 activ-
ity. Other examples include paroxetine and tadalafil which 
inactivate CYP2D6 and CYP3A4, respectively, via enzyme-
intermediate complex formation through a carbene interme-
diate.149,150 In irreversible inhibition, the metabolic pathway 
of inhibitors involves formation of a reactive intermediate

162
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
that covalently binds to the enzyme. The covalently modified 
enzyme loses catalytic capability. One example is the rapid 
loss of CYP1A2 enzymatic activity due to irreversible inhi-
bition by furafylline. CYP1A2 oxidizes the 8-methyl group 
of furafylline to an intermediate, which reacts with an active 
amino acid in CYP1A2, subsequently deactivating CYP1A2 
enzyme activity.151 Other examples of enzyme inhibitors that 
can cause irreversible covalent modification of heme and/or 
apoprotein in CYP450s include ticlopidine and clopidogrel 
(2C19 and 2B6),152 Dasatinib (CYP3A4),153 sorafenib (3A4),154 
and nefazodone (CYP3A4).155
Mechanistically, reversible inhibition is distinctly differ-
ent from irreversible inhibition. Reversible inhibition does 
not require biotransformation of the inhibitor, while irrevers-
ible inhibition usually involves metabolic bioactivation of the 
inhibitor by the targeted enzyme. Reversible inhibition can 
be alleviated, and enzymatic activity can be restored quickly 
by removing the inhibitor from the targeted enzyme. In con-
trast, irreversible inhibition blocks enzymatic activity through 
covalent binding, resulting in irreversible inactivation of the 
enzyme. Inactivated enzymes are removed from the active 
enzymatic pool, and metabolic activity is not restored until 
new enzymes are synthesized.
The outcome of both reversible and irreversible inhibitions is 
increased circulating exposure of the substrate that is the target 
of the inhibited metabolizing enzyme. The increased and more 
prolonged xenobiotics exposures in the blood circulation can 
potentially introduce undesired toxicity. The negative effects 
from irreversible inhibition are more prominent and last longer 
than those from reversible inhibition. Irreversible and quasi-
irreversible inhibition has the potential to trigger heme degra-
dation, releasing heme precursor and breakdown products into 
the systemic circulation.156,157 Additionally, irreversible and 
quasi-irreversible inhibitions also pose risk for idiosyncratic 
drug toxicity due to the generation of reactive intermediate 
and subsequent formation of protein adducts. Nefazodone has 
been withdrawn from the market in many regions and coun-
tries due to its irreversible inhibition of CYP3A4. The meta-
bolic pathways of nefazodone include metabolic bioactivation 
of hydroxynefadozone to reactive quinonoid intermediate 
that covalently modifies CYP3A4 and other hepatic proteins. 
When nefazodone is co-administered with simvastatin which 
is metabolized by CYP3A4, significantly increased simvas-
tatin concentration was detected in plasma, leading to severe 
rhabdomyolysis.158,159 Clinical use of nefazodone has also been 
associated with idiosyncratic hepatotoxicity that required liver 
transplantation.160,161
Besides xenobiotics, food, herbicides, and environmen-
tal toxins, herbal supplements can also inhibit metabolizing 
enzymes and pose a risk of herbal supplement-drug interac-
tions. For example, cruciferous vegetables have been shown to 
interact with CYP1A1.162 Repeated administration of garlic oil 
can reduce CYP2E1 activity by 39% in healthy adult subjects.163 
St. John’s wort is one of the most studied herbal supplements 
for metabolizing enzyme interaction. Hyperforin, the active 
component in St. John’s wort, induces CYP3A4, CYP2C19, 
CYP2C9, and P-glycoprotein transporter. Co-administration 
of St. John’s wort and drugs that are bioactivated by CYP3A4, 
CYP2C19, or CYP2C9 can increase the drug clearance and 
reduce the bioavailability of various drugs.164 For example, 
cyclosporine A is an immunosuppressant used to prevent 
graft-versus-host disease in bone marrow transplantation and 
to prevent rejection of liver, kidney, and heart transplants. 
Cyclosporine A is mainly metabolized by CYP3A4 in the 
liver and intestine. Concomitant use of St. John’s wort and 
cyclosporine A was reported to produce a marked reduction 
in blood levels of cyclosporin A in organ transplant recipients, 
resulting in graft rejection.165,166 Evidence from clinical stud-
ies has suggested that products with less than 1% hyperforin 
are less likely to cause herbal supplement-drug interaction.167 
Due to the popularity of herbal supplements in some popula-
tions, herbal supplement-drug interaction should be carefully 
considered to ensure greater drug safety and therapeutic effi-
cacy. Another well-known example is grapefruit juice which 
can alter enzyme activity. Compounds present in grapefruit 
can inhibit intestinal CYP3A4 and P-glycoprotein, causing 
clinically significant adverse drug reactions. Terfenadine (a 
non-sedative antihistamine) is cardiotoxic at higher doses, and 
it is metabolized by CYP3A4 to its nontoxic metabolite. The 
grapefruit-drug interaction with terfenadine has led to a higher 
concentration of terfenadine in the blood circulation, and sub-
sequent cardiotoxicity.168
4.3.5.3  Technical Assays for Inhibition 
and Induction Assessments
Many different in vitro assays are available to assess drug-
drug interaction potential of xenobiotics. The commonly used 
assays are listed in Table 4.6. In general, test compounds are 
incubated with an appropriate medium, such as microsomes, 
hepatocytes, or transfected cell lines. Changes in enzymatic 
catalytic activities are monitored with selective probe sub-
strates. For reversible inhibition, the half-maximal inhibitory 
concentration (IC50) is commonly used to define the inhibition 
potency of a test compound on a specific CYP450 enzyme. 
When time-dependent inhibition is of concern, the IC50 shift 
assay is commonly utilized during the screening stage. A pre-
incubation of test compounds and liver microsomes is applied 
to initiate the bioactivation. A probe substrate is then applied 
to assess enzyme catalytic activity. If there is a notable shift 
(e.g. >2) in the IC50 after the first 30-min preincubation, time-
dependent inhibition is likely the case. To definitively assess 
the time-dependent inhibition kinetics, a more thorough and 
labor-intensive assay can be applied by varying preincubation 
times and concentrations of the testing xenobiotics. The time-
dependent inhibition kinetics are defined by kinact (the maxi-
mal inactivation rate) and KI (concentration at half kinact). For 
induction, xenobiotics can be tested in luciferase reporter gene 
assays to evaluate their potential to activate receptors. More 
definitive assays include mRNA and enzymatic activity evalu-
ation using human hepatocytes.
4.3.6  Microsomal FMO
FMOs are another family of monooxygenase enzymes that 
catalyze the oxidation of numerous xenobiotics and environ-
mental toxicants, particularly soft nucleophiles containing N- 
and S-moieties.169,170 Marketed drugs that are FMO substrates

163
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
include cimetidine, sulindac sulfide, fenbendazole, albenda-
zole, dasatinib, and others.
In humans, there are five functional (FMO1, FMO2, FMO3, 
FMO4, and FMO5) and six nonfunctional FMO isoforms. 
Among the functional FMO isoforms, FMO1, FMO2, and 
FMO3 are important drug-metabolizing enzymes, whereas 
FMO4 and FMO5 do not contribute significantly to xenobiotic 
metabolism. FMO1 has more broad substrate specificity than 
FMO3 due to differences in the substrate-binding channels.171 
FMO3 exhibits genetic polymorphism. However, the role and 
significance of the polymorphism are not as well understood 
as CYP450s. Mutant alleles of FMO3 have been linked to tri-
methylaminuria, also known as fish-odor syndrome. In the 
presence of mutant FMO3, the body cannot metabolize odor-
ous trimethylamine (TMA) from dietary digestion into tri-
methylamine oxide. Affected individuals exude a fishy body 
odor because of the secretion of accumulated TMA in their 
breath, sweat, and urine, leading to a variety of psychosocial 
problems.172
FMOs are membrane bound to the cytoplasmic side of the 
endoplasmic reticulum. They are found in different organs 
such as liver, kidney, intestine, lung, and brain. FMO expres-
sion levels vary markedly in organs across species. For 
example, FMO3 is a hepatic enzyme, but FMO1 and FMO2 
are mainly expressed extrahepatically in the kidney and 
lung, respectively. Gender difference has been reported in 
rodents. Female mice have higher expression of FMO3 and 
FMO5. Young male rats were found to have five- to ten-fold 
less FMO3 activity compared to young female rats.173 Similar 
to CYP450s, the monooxygenase reaction cycle catalyzed by 
FMOs uses NADPH as the cofactor. The catalytic cycle of the 
FMO proceeds as follows (Figure 4.22): FMO enzymes con-
tain a tightly bound flavin adenine dinucleotide (FAD). The 
cofactor NADPH binds to FMO-FAD and reduces the FAD to 
FADH2. The oxidized NADP+ remains bound as an (NADP+)-
FMO-FADH2 complex (step A). Oxygen then binds to the 
reduced FDAH2 to yield FAD hydroperoxide, FAD-OOH (step 
B). Only when the FAD is in the hydroperoxide form, can it 
react with xenobiotic substrates. In the presence of a substrate 
(RH), one oxygen atom from the FAD hydroperoxide is then 
transferred to the substrate to generate an oxidized substrate, 
leaving the hydroxyflavin (FADH-OH) (step C). The hydroxy-
flavin product then breaks down and releases water (step D). 
The final step of the cycle is the release of NADP+. Because 
of the low dissociation rate of the NADP+, it is released at the 
end of the catalytic cycle and the enzyme is regenerated (step 
E). NADH can substitute for NADPH, but with lower affinity 
and activity. During the oxidative reaction, hydrophobic sub-
strates are converted to more polar metabolites. In contrast to 
CYP450s, FMOs do not require additional redox partners (e.g., 
a reductase) to transfer electrons from NADPH, nor do they 
require the presence of a substrate for activation. FMO and 
CYP450 overlap with respect to substrates, but the biotransfor-
mation pathways are different. For example, tertiary amines are 
generally oxidized to N-oxides through metabolism mediated 
by FMOs, whereas CYP450-mediated metabolism typically
