results in N-dealkylation. FMOs are unlikely to be inhibited 
nor easily induced. Methimazole has been shown as a model 
but non-selective inhibitor of FMO-mediated metabolism. 
Compared to CYP450-mediated bioactivation, FMO oxidiza-
tion, in general, is less likely to form reactive metabolites. The 
risk of drug-drug interactions due to FMO metabolic pathways 
is also much lower compared to CYP450s metabolism.174–176
4.3.7  Xanthine Oxidoreductase
Xanthine oxidase (XO) is a flavin-containing enzyme in the 
molybdenum family. XO is expressed in different tissues 
including the liver, small intestine, and lactating mammary 
gland with the highest activity in the liver and intestine.177 
Substrates of XO include nitrogen-containing heterocyclics 
(e.g., purine, pyrimidines, 6-mercaptopurine, pyrazinamide, 
and allopurinol).178 The catabolism of purine nucleotide to 
uric acid is primarily mediated by XO. Too much uric acid 
and the oxygen species released during the metabolism can 
lead to gout. As a result, inhibiting XO could have therapeutic 
benefits for gout treatment. XO polymorphisms, which have 
been reported in approximately 20% of Caucasians and 11% 
of the Japanese population, have been linked to adjustment of 
drug therapeutic doses.179 Type I xanthinuria is thought due to 
XO-encoding gene mutations.180
4.3.8  Amine Oxidase
Oxidative deamination of endogenous and exogenous amines 
can be catalyzed by several amine oxidases, including mono-
amine oxidase, diamine oxidase, polyamine oxidase, and 
semicarbazide-sensitive amine oxidase. Amine oxidases are 
either flavin-containing or copper-containing enzymes that are 
considered important biological regulators of cell growth and 
differentiation. The oxidation reactions catalyzed by amine oxi-
dases convert amines to aldehyde with the release of ammonia 
and hydrogen peroxide. The substrates of amine oxidases are 
primary, secondary, and tertiary amines, including many neu-
rotransmitters, neurotoxicants, and xenobiotics amines. A role 
of monoamine oxidase in psychiatric disorders has been sug-
gested. Monoamine oxidase is a flavin-containing, membrane-
bound enzyme located in the outer membrane of mitochondria 
in many tissues. The important metabolizing monoamine oxi-
dases are monoamine oxidase-A and -B. In general, many sub-
strates and inhibitors are common to all monoamine oxidases. 
Monoamine oxidase-A is the sole enzyme in sympathetic 
neurons capable of metabolizing catecholamine, convert-
ing catecholamine to aldehyde intermediates that are further 
metabolized by catechol-O-methyltransferase. Monoamine 
oxidase-A is present mostly in the liver, small intestine, and 
placenta, while only monoamine oxidase-B is found in plate-
lets. In the brain, monoamine oxidase-A and -B are preferably 
expressed in dopaminergic-noradrenergic neurons and seroto-
nergic neurons, respectively.181 Monoamine oxidase inhibitors 
have shown therapeutic effects in the treatment of depres-
sion. These therapeutic inhibitors act on monoamine oxidase 
irreversibly, which could take up to two weeks to restore the 
enzyme activity until new enzymes are synthesized in situ. The 
irreversible inhibition of monoamine oxidases could cause life-
threatening serotonin toxicity when the oxidase inhibitors are 
co-administered with serotonin-reuptake inhibitors.182
4.3.9  Aldehyde Oxidase (AO)
AOs are cytosolic enzymes that belong to the XO family.183 
AOs not only catalyze the oxidation of aldehydes to carbox-
ylic acid but also play an important role in oxidizing nitrogen-
containing heterocyclic compounds that are commonly seen in 
FIGURE 4.22  Catalytic cycle of the FMOs. Abbreviations: FAD, flavin adenine dinucleotide; R, substrate; NADP+, nicotinamide adenine dinucleo-
tide phosphate; NADPH, nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate hydrogen (reduced NADP+).

165
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
xenobiotics. In addition to oxidation, AOs also reduce N- and 
S-oxides and hydrolyze amides.184 AOs metabolize a number of 
environmental chemicals, such as nitropyrenes, nitrofluorene, 
vanillin, and phthalazines.185,186 AOs are primarily expressed 
in the liver, kidney, and brain. They can also be found in the 
eye and small intestine. The key feature of AOs is significant 
species difference. AOs have high activity in monkeys and 
humans compared to other animal species. The dog lacks AO 
activity. Due to the large species difference, particularly the 
lack of activity in some of the common species used in toxi-
cology testing, there have been a few clinical failures due to 
high AO metabolism contribution in humans that was missed 
in animal testing during nonclinical development.187 Genetic 
polymorphism of human AOs has been reported, however, the 
implications of human AO polymorphism on drug metabolism 
and toxicity remain unclear.
4.3.10  Alcohol Dehydrogenase
ADHs are cytosolic dehydrogenases expressed in different tis-
sues such as the liver, kidney, lung, brain, and small intestine. 
The highest level of ADH is found in the liver. The nomen-
clature of human ADHs is somewhat complex. They can be 
classified into five classes (ADH I-V) based on the subunits 
composition found in their dimeric structures or categorized 
according to genes into seven enzymes (ADH1A, ADH1B, 
ADH1C, ADH4, ADH5, ADH6, and ADH7). ADH6 has not 
been found as a protein in vivo.188,189 ADH1A, ADH1B, and 
ADH1C are responsible for the majority of ethanol oxidation 
in the liver.36 ADH7 is mainly expressed in the stomach and 
esophagus. ADHs catalyze the oxidation of alcohol to aldehyde 
or ketones with NAD+ as a cofactor. Due to steric hindrance, 
substrates such as primary alcohols (RCH2OH) are preferred 
over secondary alcohols (RCH(R1)OH) for the oxidation reac-
tion. There are large ethnic differences in expression levels of 
ADHs. For example, ADH1B*2 has a high frequency in the 
Chinese and Japanese populations. A study has suggested a 
strong correlation between the presence of ADH1B*2 allele 
with reduced risk for alcoholism.190
4.3.11  Aldehyde Dehydrogenase
Aldehyde dehydrogenase (ALDH) is important in the detoxi-
fication of acetaldehyde by converting aldehyde to carboxylic 
acids. There are 19 human aldehyde dehydratases. Among 
them, ALDH1A1, 1B1, and ALDH2 are the most important 
enzymes and have broad substrate specificity. In addition to 
oxidizing exogenous aldehyde, ALDHs also contribute to the 
regulation of important endogenous molecules such as retinoic 
acid and betaine.191 ALDHs are expressed in the cytosol, mito-
chondria, and endoplasmic reticulum of different tissues.
ALDH2 has a high affinity and reaction velocity for acet-
aldehyde metabolism. Disulfiram is used to mitigate chronic 
alcoholism by discouraging the consumption of alcohol. 
Disulfiram inhibits ALDH2 and subsequently impairs acet-
aldehyde elimination. Accumulation of acetaldehyde in the 
body is associated with unpleasant side effects called flushing 
reactions (e.g., increased skin temperature, nausea, vomiting, 
headaches, and increased pulse rate). The compromised ability 
of ALDH2 to detoxify acetaldehyde can also be caused by an 
inactivating mutation with a nonfunctional ALDH2*2 variant 
that is commonly found in the Asian population. Subjects with 
this variant can have blood acetaldehyde levels more than ten 
times higher than the normal level.192,193
4.3.12  Epoxide Hydrolase
Epoxide hydrolase (EH) is an important detoxifying enzyme 
from the alpha/beta hydrolase superfamily that includes ester-
ase and amidases.194 EH catalyzes enzymatic reactions that 
add water to the three-membered cycle epoxide to form 1,2-
diol. Such reaction is energetically favorable with water as 
the only co-substrate.195 Electrophilic epoxides are potentially 
genotoxic, and they can be converted to less reactive vicinal 
diols. EH is expressed in the liver, kidney, lung, intestine, and 
brain with a broad substrate selectivity for epoxide-containing 
compounds.194 EHs are either membrane-bound or located in 
the cytosol. The cytosolic EHs are also involved in control-
ling the turnover of lipid-derived epoxides that are function-
ally critical in regulating blood pressure, cell proliferation, 
and nociception. In recent years, the soluble cytosolic form has 
been shown as a promising therapeutic target to treat hyperten-
sion, inflammation, pain, and neurodegenerative disease.196–199 
Although EHs are polymorphic in humans, the clinical signifi-
cance of such genetic variation remains to be decided. EHs can 
be induced by classic inducers such as phenobarbital. There 
is evidence to suggest that the induction of EH activity cor-
relates with the increases in Phase II metabolizing enzyme 
activities.200,201
4.3.13  Esterase and Amidase
Esterases and amidases catalyze the hydrolysis reaction of 
molecules containing ester, amide, carbamate, and hydrazide 
moieties. The enzymatic reaction uses H2O as a cofactor to 
catalyze the addition of water molecules to their substrates. 
The products contain the corresponding acids and alcohols (or 
amines). Esterases and amidases are members of the alpha/
beta hydrolase superfamily. They are important in detoxify-
ing xenobiotics and maintaining physiological hemostasis. 
The family of carboxylesterases contains four isoform classes, 
carboxylesterases 1–4. Human carboxylesterase-1 and carbo-
xylesterase-2 are the two major isoforms in humans. Human 
carboxylesterase-1 is highly expressed in the liver but low 
in the small intestines. Human carboxylesterase-2 is more 
widely distributed in different tissues such as the small intes-
tine, colon, kidney, liver, heart, brain, and testes. Both have 
slightly different substrate preferences. For example, human 
carboxylesterase-1 substrates typically contain a small alcohol 
group, whereas human carboxylesterase-2 tends to hydrolyze 
substrates with a large alcohol group and a small acryl group.
4.4  Phase II Metabolism
Phase II metabolism involves several superfamilies of con-
jugating enzymes, including mainly UGTs, SULTs, GSTs, 
NATs, and methyltransferase. These enzymes can catalyze

166
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Phase I metabolites or directly metabolize parent molecules. 
Typical Phase II metabolic reactions include glucuronidation, 
sulfonation, methylation, acetylation, and glutathione conju-
gation. The structural moieties conjugated through Phase II 
metabolism, including amino acids, glucuronic acid, gluta-
thione, acetate, and sulfate. These moieties, in general, are 
larger in size and more polar than Phase I functionalization 
moieties, thus further facilitating subsequent renal or bili-
ary excretion of the metabolized molecules. Another major 
difference between Phase II and Phase I metabolites is that, 
due to the change in physiochemical properties introduced 
by Phase II conjugation reaction, Phase II metabolites tend to 
have poor membrane permeability. As a result, it can be dif-
ficult for Phase II conjugates to penetrate the cell membrane 
via passive permeation. Instead, carrier-mediated transport 
of Phase II conjugates is necessary for biliary or urinary 
elimination.202,203
4.4.1  Uridine Diphosphate- 
Glucuronosyltransferase
Phase II conjugation reactions catalyzed by the UGT super-
family are among the key detoxification pathways for a 
broad spectrum of exogenous and endogenous substances. 
Although studies of UGT-mediated glucuronidation and 
CYP450-mediated oxidation started simultaneously in the 
1960s, the current knowledge in glucuronidation is far less 
than that in CYP450-mediated metabolism.135 Many xeno-
biotics and their Phase I metabolites, dietary components, 
and environmental chemicals can be metabolized by UGTs. 
UGTs are also involved in the regulation of endogenous com-
pounds such as bile acids, bilirubin, estrogens, and gluco-
corticoids. A recent review indicates that UGT constitutes 
approximately 60%–70% of non-CYP450-mediate metabo-
lism processes.30 Four families of human UGTs have been 
identified, UGT1, UGT2, UGT3, and UGT8. Among these 
families, only UGT1 and UGT2 use UDP-glucuronic acid as 
co-substrate to catalyze glucuronidation reaction. The UGT1 
gene located on the human chromosome 2q37 produces 
nine functional UGT1A isoforms (UGT1A1 and UGT1A3-
UGT1A10) that account for 50% of the glucuronidation reac-
tion of marketed drugs.29 All nine isoforms share a conserved 
C-terminal anchoring domain but differ in their N-terminal 
domain which is thought to be the catalytic domain respon-
sible for aglycone substrate binding. There is considerable 
substrate overlap among isoforms in the UGT1A subfamily. 
UGT1A1 is responsible for the glucuronidation of bilirubin, 
thus deficiency or inhibition of UGT1A1 can cause hyperbili-
rubinemia. The UGT2B gene located on chromosome 4q13 
is responsible for seven functional UGT2B isoforms. UGTs 
are mostly expressed in the liver with the highest activity 
observed in the perivenous region. Hepatic expression of 
UGT2B4, 2B7, 2B10, and 2B15 is higher than the expression 
level of UGT1A enzymes. Large inter-subject variability in 
hepatic UGT expression exists. UGTs are also expressed in 
other tissues such as the kidney, brain, bile duct, and intes-
tine. Similar to CYP450 enzymes, inhibition and induction of 
the Phase II enzymes are possible.
4.4.1.1  Glucuronidation Reaction
UGT-mediated glucuronidation is the dominant Phase II 
metabolism pathway. Xenobiotic glucuronidation occurs 
exclusively inside the endoplasmic reticulum. UGTs add a 
glucuronide moiety either directly to the parent molecule as a 
primary biotransformation pathway or to the Phase I metabo-
lites as a secondary biotransformation pathway. In general, 
glucuronidation is used more as a secondary step than as a 
primary elimination pathway for xenobiotics.135 Substrates of 
UGTs are usually small, lipid soluble, and contain nucleophilic 
functional groups including alcohols, phenols, carboxylic acid, 
primary and secondary amines and amides, sulfonamides, ter-
tiary amines, pyridines, thiols, and acidic enols. Thus, it is not 
surprising that many Phase I metabolites undergo subsequent 
glucuronidation. Most of the Phase II conjugate metabolites 
are considered to be the final metabolic products and phar-
macologically inactive. But in some rare cases, glucuronide 
metabolites (e.g., morphine-6-glucuronide)204 and ezetimibe-
glucuronide205 retain significant pharmacological activities.
The co-substrate, UDP-glucuronic acid is formed in the 
cytoplasm in a two-step reaction. The first step is a conjugation 
reaction between glucose-1 phosphate and UDP to form UDP-
glucose by UDP-glucose pyrophosphorylase. The second step 
is an oxidation reaction of UDP-glucose to UDP-glucuronic 
acids by UDP-glucose dehydrogenase. UGT then conjugates 
UDP-glucuronic acid to a substrate structure and transfer a 
glucuronosyl group from UDP-glucuronic acid to the substrate 
molecule. The most common glucuronidation reaction are 
O-and N-glucuronidation, and on rare occasions through S- 
and C-glucuronidation, depending on the substituent function 
group in the substrate structures. For O-glucuronidation, the 
glucuronic acid moiety is attached to alcohols, phenols, or car-
boxylic acids, whereas in the case of N-glucuronidation amines 
and amides are conjugated. Some examples of glucuronida-
tion are displayed in Figure 4.23. Substrates that can form 
N-glucuronides include primary aromatic amines, hydrox-
ylamines, amides, tertiary aliphatic amines, and aromatic 
N-heterocycles. The N-glucuronides are of particular interest 
for toxicity implication and application in cancer research. 
Carcinogenic arylamines can be oxidized by CYP450s and the 
metabolites are subsequently glucuronidated. The glucuroni-
dation step plays an important role in arylamines disposition 
and may critically determine the toxic potential of these com-
pounds. N-glucuronide of arylamines can act as a transport 
vehicle to bring these xenobiotics to the bladder and kidney. 
Since N-glucuronides are acid labile, these N-glucuronides can 
regenerate parent acryl amine under acidic conditions such as 
in urine. Such instability may contribute to the carcinogenicity 
of some primary arylamines. Bioactivation of the arylamines 
could generate toxic N-hydroxy-amines in the bladder, and the 
toxic metabolite can bind to DNA to form DNA adducts.206–208
Phase II metabolism is mostly considered an inactivation 
reaction prior to biliary or urinary excretion of conjugated 
metabolites. Toxic metabolites, such as acyl glucuronides 
(ester glucuronides), have been identified from the bioactiva-
tion of substances with a carboxylic acid group.209–210 Human 
UGT2B7 and rat UGT2B1 are involved in acyl glucuronida-
tion.211 In contrast to ether glucuronides, acyl glucuronides

167
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
are prone to undergo hydrolysis and, more importantly, intra-
molecular rearrangement (also known as acyl migration). The 
resulting free aldehyde moiety potentially poses a toxicity risk 
by forming covalent adducts between protein and the gluc-
uronide conjugate. Besides acyl migration, the acyl carbon 
can also react directly with protein amino acid side chains 
to form protein adducts. For example, diclofenac is metabo-
lized by UGTs to diclofenac acyl-β-d-glucuronide (DIC-AG) 
(Figure 4.24). As depicted in Figure 4.25, the acyl glucuronide 
(DIC-AG) is a reactive intermediate that could form covalent 
protein adducts through transacylation or glycation, leading to 
cellular damage and immune responses against the covalent 
protein adducts.212–214 UGTs are also involved in the forma-
tion of acyl glucoside. As a sugar conjugate, acyl glucoside 
may also potentially have toxicological significance. Acyl 
glucosides have received much less attention than acyl gluc-
uronides. Similar to acyl glucuronide, acyl glucoside has also 
been shown to undergo transacylation and hydrolysis. Acyl 
glucosides can be equally electrophilic as their glucuronide 
counterparts.215–219
Glucuronidation is a reversible reaction, particularly with 
the presence of bacterial β-glucuronidases in slightly acidic 
conditions, such as in the small intestine. Biliary excreted 
glucuronide metabolites can be enzymatically converted back 
to their parent drug after deconjugation catalyzed by bacte-
rial β-glucuronidases in the duodenum. The released parent 
drug can be further reabsorbed through enterocytes reaching 
back into system circulation. The net outcome is equivalent to 
enterohepatic recirculation that essentially results in prolong 
drug exposure in vivo.
Probenecid inhibits glucuronidation both as a competitive 
inhibitor of UGT and an inhibitor of UDP-glucuronic acid in 
the endoplasmic reticulum.220,221 7,7,7-Triphenylheptyl-UDP 
has been utilized as an irreversible UGT inhibitor.222 However, 
potent and selective UGT inhibitors that are relevant to clinical 
drug-drug interaction are rare. Exposure increases of glucuro-
nide metabolites are rarely greater than two-fold in the pres-
ence of a UGT inhibitor compared to the baseline level. This 
magnitude of exposure change contrasts with the drug-drug 
interaction scenarios observed from CYP450 involvement 
where the increase of exposures can be up to 35-fold.223 The 
primary reasons for low drug-drug interaction risk from UGT 
inhibition include (1) low affinity of drug substrate to UGTs, 
(2) low affinity of the inhibitor to UGTs, (3) multiple enzyme 
FIGURE 4.23  Example of glucuronidation reactions.

168
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
superfamilies (e.g., both P450s and UGTs) contributing to 
the metabolism of the drug, and (4) the potential for multiple 
UGTs to metabolize the drug.29
4.4.1.2  Species, Gender, and Genetic 
Differences in UGT Activity
Although UGT1A gene structure is largely conserved from 
animals to humans, different species have different UGT1A 
isoforms that are functional or deficient. For example, 
UGT1A6 is a pseudogene in domestic cats, and cats are more 
susceptible to acetaminophen toxicity because of the deficient 
glucuronidation of this drug.224 Similarly, UGT1A9 is a pseu-
dogene in rats and dogs. As a result, glucuronidation of propo-
fol (a selective substrate of UGT1A9) was low in rats and dogs, 
while propofol is efficiently glucuronidated in humans, mar-
mosets, and mice. UGT1A1, the Phase II enzyme responsible 
for bilirubin clearance, is functionally similar in many spe-
cies except in Gunn rats that lack UGT1A enzymes. Therefore, 
high serum bilirubin concentration is observed in Gunn rats. 
A similar defect was observed in patients with Gilbert’s 
syndrome, a UGT1A deficiency in humans with impaired 
metabolic capability toward bilirubin conjugation. UGT2B10, 
the “orphan enzyme”, has been identified to play an important 
role in N-glucuronidation of many aromatic N-heterocycles 
and tertiary amines in humans. For example, nicotine and 
medetomidine are predominantly conjugated by UGT2B10 in 
the human liver. An ortholog of UGT2B10 has not been found 
in laboratory animals.
Conjugation of amine-containing xenobiotics with UDP-
glucuronic acid also showed important species differences. 
For primary and secondary amines, N-glucuronidation path-
ways are similar across species. The metabolism of tertiary 
amine-containing xenobiotics to quaternary ammonium-
linked glucuronides is highly species-dependent. For tertiary 
aliphatic amines (e.g., antihistamines), N-glucuronidation 
is a major metabolism pathway.62,225 However, glucuroni-
dation of these substrates is generally undetectable in some 
animal species such as rats, dogs, and cynomolgus monkeys. 
Marmoset monkeys and rabbits are reported to have efficient 
N-glucuronidation of drugs (e.g., amitriptyline), and these 
species are therefore more predictive of human metabolism 
via this route. Only human UGTs can catalyze quaternary 
N-glucuronide formation. The inability of animal species 
FIGURE 4.25  Intramolecular acyl migration, transacylation, and glycation.
FIGURE 4.24  Glucuronidation of diclofenac.

169
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
to form quaternary N-glucuronides could be related to spe-
cies differences in UGT1A3, UGT1A4, and UGT2B10 speci-
ficity, expression level, and activity.226 Glucuronides are 
excreted through different pathways in different species. An 
N-glucuronide of MaxiPost (a calcium-activated potassium 
channel opener developed for acute ischemic stroke) is mainly 
excreted through urine in humans, while in dogs the metab-
olite was mainly found in the bile. Another example is zid-
ovudine for the treatment of human immunodeficiency virus 
(HIV). The glucuronide metabolite of zidovudine is the major 
metabolite in monkeys and humans, while the drug is elimi-
nated primarily unchanged in rats and dogs.
Gender difference in glucuronidation activity has been 
observed, however, the exact underlying mechanism remains 
to be determined. It has been reported that men have approxi-
mately four-fold higher expression of UGT2B17 and conse-
quently higher UGT2B17-mediated glucuronidation activities 
than women.227 UGT2B15-mediated oxazepam glucuroni-
dation was also found to be faster in men than in women.217 
Plasma sex hormone levels in female species are thought to be 
the driving force for any observed gender-dependent differ-
ence in UGT metabolism.228
4.4.1.3  Induction of UGT
UGTs can be induced by xenobiotics. UGTs are responsive to 
ligands of PXR, CAR, glucocorticoid receptors, and PPAR. 
As a result, the induction of UGTs is commonly coordinated 
with the induction of CYP450s, although increases in protein 
levels are much less than those of CYP450s. For example, the 
response of human UGTs to classic inducers such as dexa-
methasone, 3-methylcholanthrene, phenobarbital, rifampicin, 
and omeprazole was studied by monitoring the glucuronida-
tion of estradiol, naphthol, propofol, and morphine in primary 
human hepatocyte culture. Only 1.7- to 2.5-fold increases in 
the levels of glucuronidation were observed after treatments 
with different inducers. Induction of UGT activity can have 
toxicological implications due to altered Phase II glucuroni-
dation activity, particularly for xenobiotics that form reactive 
acyl glucuronide. For example, nonsteroidal anti-inflamma-
tory drugs (NSAIDs) undergo Phase II conjugation on the 
carboxylic acid group in their structures to form acyl gluc-
uronides, which are subsequently excreted through bile and 
finally reach the intestine.229 Induction of UGT isoforms can 
increase the biliary output of the acyl glucuronide conjugates, 
which is considered to be the cause for increased severity of 
small intestinal ulceration.230
4.4.2  Sulfotransferase (SULT)
SULTs belong to another important superfamily of conjugat-
ing enzymes involved in Phase II metabolism. In general, the 
addition of a sulfonate group to a compound increases its water 
solubility and decreases its biological activity. However, some 
SULTs can bioactivate xenobiotics to reactive metabolites. A 
survey in 2016 indicated that sulfonation accounted for 17% of 
non-CYP450-mediated metabolism of the top 200 most pre-
scribed drugs.30 SULTs are mostly involved in the posttrans-
lational modification of macromolecules such as secretory 
proteins and glycosaminoglycans. Membrane-bound SULTs 
are less metabolically significant than SULTs present in the 
cytosol. Cytosolic SULTs play an important role in Phase II 
metabolic conjugation of xenobiotics and their metabolites, as 
well as endogenous hormones, neurotransmitters, bile acids, 
proteins, and carbohydrates. For example, 90% of dopamine in 
the blood circulation and cerebrospinal fluid is metabolized by 
SULTs (SULT1A3) to inactive dopamine sulfate.231 Cytosolic 
SULTs are mostly found in the liver but are also expressed in 
almost all organs such as the intestine, breast, lung, adrenal 
glands, kidney, and brain. The cytosolic SULTs are grouped 
into four different families, SULT1, SULT2, SULT4, and 
SULT6 with different substrate specificity and tissue distri-
bution.232,233 The primary human SULT enzymes involved in 
the metabolism of xenobiotics are SULT1A1 and SULT1B1, 
which account for approximately 70% of hepatic SULTs.233 
The enzymes in SULT2 family are primarily responsible for 
the metabolism of steroids, and the physiological significance 
of SULT4 and SULT6 remains elusive (Table 4.7).234
Similar to UGT glucuronidation, SULT-mediated sulfate 
conjugation is typically considered a detoxification reac-
tion for many xenobiotics with sulfated metabolites readily 
excreted in the urine. Sulfonation can act directly on xeno-
biotics or their Phase I metabolites. Most of the sulfonation 
conjugates are considered pharmacologically inactive. One 
exception is the sulfonate conjugate of the prodrug, minoxi-
dil (Figure 4.26). The sulfate conjugate of minoxidil is the 
active form that has therapeutic benefit as an antihypertensive 
agent and in hair regrowth.242 The co-substrate for sulfonation

is 3-phosphoadenosine-5′-phosphosulfate (PAPS), which is 
formed by a two-step process in the cytosol. Catalyzed by 
adenosine-5′-triphosphate (ATP) sulfurylase, sulfate (SO4
−2) 
is conjugated with adenosine monophosphate moiety of ATP 
to form adenosine 5′-phosphosulfate (APS). APS kinase sub-
sequently catalyzes the phosphorylation reaction of APS to 
form PAPS. The two-step reaction of PAPS synthesis is lim-
ited by hepatic as well as circulating sulfate concentrations. 
Compared to hepatic UDP-glucuronic acids concentration 
(300 nmol/g liver), PAPS concertation in the liver is approxi-
mately 23 nmol/g liver, which can be rapidly depleted during 
the enzymatic reaction. As a result, hepatic sulfonation is often 
a cofactor-limited conjugation reaction. Sulfonation of xenobi-
otics commonly occurs on function groups such as hydroxyl, 
amino, sulfhydryl, and N-oxide groups in substrate structures 
(Figure 4.27). Typical reactions include O-sulfonation (ester) 
and N-sulfonation (amide). The predominant sulfonation is 
O-sulfonation.
Both sulfonation and glucuronidation are major Phase II 
conjugation pathways for detoxification and excretion of xeno-
biotics. They are competing Phase II pathways with overlap-
ping substrates specificities. Interestingly, SULTs and UGTs 
can also conjugate the same xenobiotic substrates in a coopera-
tive way. In human liver, expression levels of UGTs and SULTs 
vary among different regions. Most SULTs were found to have 
higher activity in the periportal region except SULT1A,243 
whereas UGTs have relatively higher activity in the perivenous 
region. Sulfonation is a low-capacity, high-affinity system, 
and glucuronidation is a high-capacity, low-affinity system. 
Sulfonation can be the major pathway at low substrate con-
centrations until saturation of SULTs occurs at high substrate 
concentrations, at which point glucuronidation becomes the 
predominant conjugation pathway.244,245 The expression level 
of SULTs and UGTs also vary at different development stages 
in humans. In the fetus liver, SULTs (e.g., SULT1A3) are pres-
ent at much higher levels than UGTs. A high level of hepatic 
SULT1A3 is expressed early in development and decreases 
in the late fetal/early neonatal stage. A similar trend is also 
observed in the pulmonary SULT1A3 levels with the fetus 
having a much higher level of the enzyme than that in the 
neonatal stage. In contrast, other conjugating enzymes (e.g., 
UGTs) are not expressed at significant levels until the neonatal 
period. As a result, SULTs are considered the major enzyme 
system for detoxification and modulation of homeostasis of 
endogenous hormones and neurotransmitters in the fetus.246,247
Similar to glucuronidation, sulfated metabolites are prone to 
the conjugation-deconjugation cycles. The sulfate ester bond 
can be hydrolyzed to release the parent substrate, and then 
followed by resulfonation cycle. Sulfonation is also involved 
in bioactivation of the parent substrates, leading to potential 
toxicity significance, as seen for tamoxifen (Figure 4.28), saf-
role, phenacetin, and troglitazone.248–250 The reactive interme-
diate is formed through electron withdrawing potential of the 
sulfate group that can produce a strong electrophilic cationic 
carbon center. For example, the oxidative metabolite of tamox-
ifen undergoes sulfonation to form tamoxifen O-sulfonate 
FIGURE 4.27  Enzymatic reactions catalyzed by SULTs.
FIGURE 4.26  Sulfonation of minoxidil.

171
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
metabolite. Subsequent hydrolysis of tamoxifen O-sulfonate 
yields an allylic carbocation that is a reactive electrophilic 
intermediate with the potential to form DNA adducts.
In general, SULTs are resistant to induction when exposed 
to classic inducers such as phenobarbital. Glucocorticoids are 
capable of inducing SULT1A in rats and humans. SULT1C 
and 2A are also inducible by growth hormones through acti-
vation of the PXR. SULT1A3 is highly inducible by dopa-
mine through activation of the D1 and N-methyl-D-aspartate 
(NMDA) receptors.251 Since SULT1A3 in nonhuman primates 
rapidly converts dopamine to dopamine sulfate, the auto-
induction (auto-induction means that the molecule induces 
enzymes that are responsible for its own metabolism) could 
have toxicological implications for neurodegenerative disease 
that result from dopamine depletion. Sulfonation is mostly 
considered a cofactor-limited reaction, thus induction of 
SULTs likely will not have a profound impact on the maximal 
velocity of sulfonation.
Species difference in tamoxifen hepatotoxicity has been 
observed. Tamoxifen is not a liver carcinogen in humans. 
In sharp contrast, the risk in the rodent is much higher. The 
competing kinetic rates of different metabolic pathways for 
tamoxifen in different species contribute to the observed 
species-specific toxicity differences (Figure 4.29). In rats, the 
formation rates of tamoxifen oxidative metabolites are two- to 
five-fold higher than that in humans, and the glucuronidation 
of the Phase I oxidative metabolites in the rat is much less than 
that in humans. Additionally, humans have a three-fold lower 
rate of sulfonation than the rat. As a result, a lower level of 
sulfate ester conjugate metabolites is formed in humans. At 
the standard therapeutic dose of tamoxifen, the safety factor 
of tamoxifen is approximately 60,000 larger in humans than 
in rodents.252–255
In rodents, there are marked gender differences in SULT-
mediated sulfonation due to suppressive or stimulatory effects 
from sex and/or growth hormone. Hepatic Sult1a1, Sult1d1, 
Sult2a1/a2, and Sult3a1 are female-predominant enzymes in 
mice, whereas expression of Sult1c1 is male-predominant in 
mouse liver and kidney. Sult4a1 expression is female-predom-
inant in the mouse brain.256 These gender-based differences in 
the expression levels of SULTs are translated to gender-spe-
cific sulfonation. For example, sulfonations of glucocorticoids, 
androsterone, and 4-nitrophenol in male rats are much less 
than that in female rats. A single dose of tamoxifen in female 
rats produced a much higher level of DNA adduct formation 
than in male rats, likely due to the higher level of SULT2A in 
female rats. However, after repeated dosing of tamoxifen for 
14 days, it was found that SULT2A was induced by tamoxi-
fen. Eventually, DNA adduct formation was similar in both 
male and female rats, providing a possible explanation for 
the susceptibility of both male and female rats to tamoxifen 
carcinogenesis.257

metabolic rate in rats versus in humans.

4.4.3  Glutathione S-Transferase
Since first detected in the earlier 1960s,258 GSTs have been 
studied in great detail as part of a Phase II enzyme family. 
GSTs play an important role in the xenobiotic metabolism and 
detoxification process. GSTs catalyze the conjugation reac-
tions of glutathione with a variety of electrophilic compounds 
such as carcinogens, xenobiotic drugs, and pesticides. The 
knowledge of the GST family has grown substantially over 
the last two to three decades. Besides detoxification, its non-
catalytic activity has been shown to regulate kinases that are 
involved in the development of drug resistance toward anti-
cancer agents.259 Additionally, GSTs constitute an important 
defense mechanism in reducing intracellular oxidative stress 
from reactive species such as α,β-unsaturated carbonyls, qui-
nones, and hydroperoxide.259,260
Similar to UGTs and SULTs, GSTs catalyze the conjugation 
reactions of parent drugs or electrophilic metabolites formed 
from Phase I or Phase II metabolism. The cofactor glutathione 
is a tripeptide composed of glutamate, cysteine, and glycine. 
The low molecule-weight tripeptide is the most abundant solu-
ble thiol antioxidant in cells.261 Glutathione conjugation effec-
tively reduces substrate electrophilicity and their likelihood to 
form covalent adducts to proteins. Glutathione conjugation can 
occur spontaneously due to the high intracellular concentra-
tion of glutathione (in mM range) and is mediated by GSTs. 
In general, glutathione conjugation metabolites are neither 
pharmacologically nor toxicologically active. However, there 
is evidence that GSTs bioactivate chemicals, such as dihaloal-
kanes, to form toxic glutathione conjugates.262,263 Xenobiotic-
induced hepatoxicity can occur when intracellular glutathione 
is depleted. Glutathione conjugation has been widely used in 
assays to identify new chemical entities that have the poten-
tial to form reactive metabolites and subsequent adducts with 
intracellular macromolecules. There is considerable overlap 
in substrate specificity of GST family enzymes. For example, 
1-chloro-2, 4-dinitrobenzene is a common probe substrate to 
study GST activity, and it can be catalyzed by most human 
GSTs (except GST T1-1 or GST T2-2).264,265
4.4.3.1  Organization, Structure, and 
Localization of GST
GSTs are expressed mostly in the liver and at lower levels 
in other tissues such as the kidney, intestine, and lung. The 
GST family consists of three families: the cytosolic GSTs, 
mitochondrial GSTs, and microsomal GSTs. The micro-
somal GSTs are membrane bound with a predominant role in 
eicosanoid synthesis. The cytosolic and mitochondrial GSTs 
are soluble GSTs, which are most important for glutathione 
conjugation with endogenous and exogenous substrates. In 
humans and rodents, soluble GSTs constitute approximately 
4% of total soluble hepatic proteins.266 The cytosolic GSTs 
can be classified into different classes: alpha, mu, pi, theta, 
zeta, omega, and sigma.267 The Kappa class of GST is identi-
fied in mitochondria. The microsomal GSTs are designated as 
the membrane-associated proteins in eicosanoid and glutathi-
one metabolism to reflect their predominant role in eicosanoid 
synthesis.268
The cytosolic GSTs are closely associated with xenobiotics 
conjugation, partly due to the fact that almost 85%–90% of 
cellular glutathione is present in the cytosol.269,270 The half-life 
of GSH is different in the cytosol (2–3 hours) vs. mitochondria 
(30 hours). The abbreviation of GST is preceded with a lower-
case initial to identify the species that isoform belong to, for 
example, “hGST” for human GSTs. An upper-case letter fol-
lowed by a numerical value is used to identify the class subfam-
ily. Since GSTs in mitochondrial and cytosol exist as dimers, 
another numerical value denoted with a hyphen is at the end of 
each GST name to represent the second subfamily component 
of the enzyme dimer. For example, “hGSTA1-1” represents 
human GST homodimer in the alpha-class subfamily. The bio-
logical importance of the dimeric structure is not definitively 
known. There is evidence to suggest that the dimeric configu-
ration enhances GST protein stability and aligns the binding 
site optimally for efficient enzymatic catalysis.271 GSTs in the 
cytosol consist of approximately 20 isoforms, alpha (GSTA1-1, 
GSTA2-2, GSTA3-2, GSTA4-4, and GSTA5-5), mu (GSTM1-
1, GSTM2-2, GSTM3-3, GSTM4-4, and GSTM5-5), pi 
(GSTP1-1), sigma (GSTS1-1), theta (GSTT1-1 and GSTT2-2), 
zeta (GSTZ1-1), and omega (GSTO1-1 and GSTO2-2) classes. 
They are either homodimers or hetero- (alpha and mu) dimers, 
a structural feature necessary to catalyze glutathione conjuga-
tion. Among all subclasses, alpha, mu, and pi classes of GSTs 
have relatively higher levels of expression and ease of puri-
fication, thus they are more commonly seen in the literature 
publications.
Some members of GSTs are associated with drug resistance 
toward anticancer agents. Elevated levels of GST in different 
cancer cells can reduce exposure to chemotherapeutic drugs. 
Thus, GSTs have been one of the therapeutic targets in anti-
cancer drug discovery. Suppression of GST activity likely 
could improve tumor sensitivity to chemotherapeutic drugs. 
However, it has been changeling to develop selective and potent 
GST inhibitors. Examples of GST inhibitors include ethac-
rynic acid and analogs, ethacraplatin, 6-(7-nitro-2,1,3-ben-
zoxadiazol-4-ylthio)hexanol, auranofin, and piperlongumine. 
While the overexpression of GST in cancer cells has posed 
challenges for anticancer drug resistance, GST-mediated glu-
tathione conjugation has been utilized in prodrug design for 
targeted tissue delivery of pharmacologically active drugs.272 
The general prodrug strategy includes molecules containing 
glutathione-like moiety; or molecules whose enzymatic acti-
vation occurs through GST-mediated glutathione conjugation 
pathways.273 Canfosfamide, metformin analogs, nitric oxide 
prodrugs, and doxorubicin analogs have been explored as 
prodrugs.267
4.4.3.2  Reactions of GST
The reaction catalyzed by GST involves a nucleophilic attack 
by reduced glutathione on electrophilic centers of nonpo-
lar xenobiotic substrates. The formed glutathione conjugates 
are much more polar and are exported out of the cell with 
the assistance of transporters (e.g., multidrug resistance pro-
tein).274 Unlike glucuronidation and sulfonation, glutathione 
conjugates typically do not undergo deconjugation. As shown 
in Figure 4.30, the amide bonds in glutathione conjugates can

173
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
be further hydrolyzed to form cysteinyl glycine and cysteine 
metabolites. The cysteine S-conjugates either undergo elimi-
nation through bile or are further metabolized to mercapturic 
acids (N-acetylcysteine conjugates) that are readily removed 
from the body through renal excretion.275,276 Many of the sub-
strates of glutathione conjugation are highly electrophilic in 
nature and can readily form covalent adducts with intracel-
lular macromolecules. This includes therapeutic drugs and 
their electrophilic metabolites formed during bioactivation by 
Phase I metabolizing enzymes.277–280 GST-catalyzed conjuga-
tion reactions are important pathways for inactivating reactive 
metabolites and reducing the formation of covalent macro-
molecule adducts. Aside from the glutathione conjugation, 
GSTs also catalyze isomerization and reduction reactions.281 
Functional groups that are prone to glutathione conjugation 
include electrophilic carbon, nitrogen, oxygen, and sulfur cen-
ters commonly found in quinones and epoxides in both exog-
enous and endogenous compounds. For example, acrylamides 
have been a popular electrophilic moiety for covalent inhibi-
tors. Acrolein, a metabolite of the chemotherapeutic agent 
cyclophosphamide, is an α, β-unsaturated aldehyde that can be 
readily conjugated by GST.282,283 Figure 4.31 lists a few struc-
ture examples of typical glutathione substrates.
4.4.3.3  Regulation of GST
St. John’s wort and garlic can induce GST expression.284,285 
Xenobiotics such as rifampin, NSAIDs, quinolones, and phe-
nobarbital have also been shown to upregulate the expression 
of GSTs.286–289 Increased GSTs levels are often detected in can-
cer cells. Definitive understandings of GST induction mecha-
nisms remain elusive. Regulation through Nrf-2 mediated 
pathways due to endogenous electrophiles was hypothesized 
as one of the causes.290 Other studies have shown that upregu-
lation of GST expression is controlled through receptors such 
as CAR, PXR, and AhR. Among different mechanisms, the 
Nrf-2 activators induce most of the GSTs, followed by CAR, 
PXR, and AhR.291
4.4.3.4  Species and Gender Differences in GST
Species difference in carcinogenic response is sometimes 
associated with different expression levels of hepatic GSTs. 
Mice and rats have significant differences in cytosolic GST 
activity in the liver. Rats are susceptible to the hepatocarcino-
genic effect of the fungal toxin aflatoxin B1 at a dose as low 
as 15 parts per billion (ppb). This is in sharp contrast to mice 
FIGURE 4.30  GSH conjugation and subsequent reactions leading to the formation of mercapturic acid.

174
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
that can tolerate a dietary dose of aflatoxin B1 at up to 10,000 
ppb without signs of liver cancer. This huge inter-species dif-
ference in carcinogenic response is considered mainly due to 
the different expression levels of GSTA3-3, the main detoxify-
ing enzyme of the carcinogenic epoxide of aflatoxin B1. The 
catalytic activity of GSTA3-3 is much lower in rats compared 
to that in mice. As a result, mice can tolerate aflatoxin B1 at a 
much higher dose without hepatocarcinogenic effects.292,293,294 
Rodents have much greater capacity in GST-mediated gluta-
thione conjugation to dichloromethane and 1,2-epoxy-3-(4′-
nitrophenoxy) propane compared to other species. The species 
difference can be substrate- and reaction-dependent. Human 
GSTT1 has a 4.8-fold higher capacity than mouse GSTT1 
to catalyze the reduction of cymene hydroperoxide.295 Using 
1,2-dichloro-4-nitrobenzene as a substrate, GST activity is 
greater in hamsters than that in rabbits, guinea pigs, mice, and 
rats.296 Preclinical and clinical studies of efavirenz showed 
renal tubule epithelial cell toxicity in rats, but not in monkeys 
and humans. The higher susceptibility to renal toxicity in rats 
is likely due to an additional glutathione conjugation step of 
efavirenz. Efavirenz is metabolized to several metabolites 
among species, but only in rats is there an additional meta-
bolic step leading to glutathione conjugate (M9) and subse-
quent cysteinylglycine conjugates (M10) (Figure 4.32). It was 
proposed that the cysteinylglycine conjugates (M10) formed 
in the kidney by γ-glutamyl-transpeptidase could be further 
metabolized by dipeptidase to yield toxic species (cysteine 
conjugate), leading to the observed kidney toxicity in rats. 
Pretreatment of acivicin, a GST inhibitor, protected rats from 
efavirenz-mediated renal toxicity.3
Brain GST activity in female rats and mice is higher than 
in males.297 However, male birds, such as pigeons and kites, 
exhibit relatively higher brain GST activities than females. 
Different enzymatic activities have also been observed 
between chickens and quails.298 In the liver, male rodents were 
found to have higher GST activity than females.299 This was 
hypothesized as one of the reasons for the higher susceptibil-
ity of female rodents to the carcinogenicity of benzo(a)pyrene 
compared to males.300 In rodent kidneys, female GSTA, 
GSTM2, and GSTO1 levels are higher than males.301,302 GSTA 
FIGURE 4.31  Examples of the reaction types catalyzed by GSTs.

175
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
has been suggested as a potential biomarker of renal injury and 
acute liver injury.303,304 Repeated treatment with gentamicin in 
female Sprague-Dawley rats increased GSTA activity 33-fold 
compared to only a 21-fold increase in male rats. This find-
ing is consistent with more severe kidney damage in female 
rats.305 In healthy human subjects, no major gender differences 
in GST activities have been observed. Aging has been con-
sidered a factor in reduced GST detoxification potential.306 In 
patients with multiple sclerosis, male patients were found to 
have lower GSTP1 activity and more severe disease compared 
to female patients. The different disease responses in genders 
could suggest a role of GST detoxification in patients with 
multiple sclerosis.307
4.4.4  N-Acetyltransferase
A few other Phase II conjugation reactions account for less 
than 3% of the metabolism of marketed drugs. These include 
acetylation, methylation, and amino acid conjugation. There 
are two NAT isoforms in humans, NAT1 and NAT2. NAT1 can 
be found in almost all tissues, while NAT2 is mainly expressed 
in the liver and small intestines. They are cytosolic enzymes. 
NATs catalyze the acetylation reaction by transferring an ace-
tyl group from the cofactor acetyl-CoA to an aromatic amine 
or a terminal nitrogen of hydrazine. Genetic polymorphism 
of NATs is well known with over 100-fold inter-subject varia-
tions in NAT activities. Phenotypically, “slow acetylators” 
FIGURE 4.32  Biotransformation pathways of efavirenz leading to the formation of M9 and M10 in rat.

176
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
and “rapid acetylators” have been associated with the risk of 
developing environmentally related cancers.26 NAT-catalyzed 
acetylation is an important metabolic detoxification and bio-
activation reaction for many nitrogen-containing endogenous 
and exogenous substances, such as hydrazines, aromatic and 
aliphatic amines, amino acids, and sulfonamides. Aromatic 
amines are known human carcinogens that are found in many 
industrial and environmental chemicals. It is interesting to 
know that dogs do not express NATs, which could be related 
to the dog being relatively more susceptible to amine-induced 
carcinogenesis. Reactive intermediates can be formed by NAT-
mediated bioactivation. Some reactive intermediates can form 
DNA adducts, leading to mutations or organ toxicities.308,309 
Unlike other conjugations, acetylation generally produces 
metabolites with much less solubility than parent molecules, 
and such metabolites sometimes might induce organ toxicity. 
For example, sulfonamide is catalyzed by NAT-mediated acet-
ylation followed by renal excretion. The acetylated metabolites 
are less watersoluble and can precipitate in the kidney, leading 
to crystalluria and proximal tubular damage.
4.4.5  Methylation
Methylation is another conjugation pathway that tends to 
form metabolites with decreased solubility unless the reac-
tion produces metabolites with positively charged ions.310 A 
number of enzymes in the methyltransferase family catalyze 
metabolic biotransformation of substrates through O-, N-, or 
S-methylation (Table 4.8). These enzymes use S-adenosyl-L-
methionine (SAM) as the methyl donor, and SAM is converted 
to S-adenosyl-L-homocysteine after donating the methyl 
group to the substrates.
Catechol O-methyltransferases (COMTs), as its name sug-
gested, catalyze the methylation reaction of catechols. It is an 
important metabolic regulation pathway for catecholamines 
and catechol estrogens.311 Two COMT isoforms are avail-
able, a soluble form (S-COMT) and a membrane-bound form 
(MB-COMT). S-COMT is virtually found in all tissues with 
the highest concentration in the liver. MB-COMT is highly 
expressed in the brain. Although both isoforms share almost 
identical sequences, they have little overlap in substrate speci-
ficity. Inhibition of COMT by entacapone and flavonoids has 
shown therapeutic benefit for Parkinson’s disease. Genetically, 
Asians and African Americans tend to have higher COMT 
activity. The N-methylation of nitrogen-containing compounds 
can be catalyzed by several enzymes in humans and other spe-
cies.310–313 Among them, histamine N-methyltransferase, and 
nicotine N-methyltransferase have been reported as poly-
morphic.314,315 Nicotine N-methyltransferase is expressed in 
the brain and has been linked to reactive intermediates for-
mation that has implications in Parkinson’s disease develop-
ment.316 Thiopurine S-methyltransferases are best known for 
their role in catalyzing S-methylation of thiopurine drugs. 
Example substrates include aromatic heterocyclic sulfhydryl 
compounds, such as 6-mercaptopurine, captopril, and azathio-
prine. Thiopurine S-methyltransferases is a cytosolic enzyme 
mostly found in the liver, kidney, lung, and human red blood 
cells. Thiopurine S-methyltransferases can be inhibited by 
naproxen, mefenamic, and tolfenamic acid, leading to excess 
formation of thioguanine nucleotides from thiopurine drugs 
and subsequent adverse effects.317
4.4.6  Amino Acid Conjugation
Amino acid conjugation can occur on xenobiotics containing 
a carboxylic acid group (–COOH), which are common in mar-
keted drugs (e.g., simvastatin and valproic acid), herbicides, 
insecticides, and food preservatives. Among different amino 
acids, glycine is most commonly involved in conjugation. 
Amino acid conjugation plays an important role in regulating 
endogenous substrates such as bile acids. The liver and kidney 
are the primary organs where amino acid conjugation occurs. 
In general, amino acid conjugation is considered a nontoxic 
biotransformation pathway. However, there are exceptions. For 
example, conjugation of N-hydroxy arylamines forms reactive 
intermediates that are toxic.318
4.5  Pharmacogenomics of Xenobiotic 
and Drug Metabolism
Pharmacogenomics is the study of how an individual’s genome 
as a whole affects their response to drugs. Although the term 
pharmacogenetics is sometimes used interchangeably with 
pharmacogenomics, pharmacogenetics refers to the study of 
single gene-drug interactions. By contrast, pharmacogenom-
ics is a much broader concept and is based on a genome-wide 
association approach that deals with the effects of multiple 
genes on drug responses. Pharmacogenomics includes not only 
genetic variation but integrates these with gene expression pro-
filing, and epigenetics. Despite their difference in scope, the 
goal of both approaches is to understand how an individual’s 
genetic makeup affects his or her response to medications. 
Their long-term goal is to help doctors select the drugs and 
dosages best suited for each person by maximizing efficacy 
while avoiding adverse effects. Both approaches are a part of 
precision medicine, which seeks to replace the “one-size-fits-
all” approach to medicine with evidence-based, individualized 
treatment. Many FDA-approved drugs now contain pharma-
cogenomic information within their labels. Implementation of 
pharmacogenomics in the clinic has led to more specific rec-
ommendations and warnings about which genetic tests should 
be performed under what circumstances before a specific drug

Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
is administered. Pharmacogenomic analysis of drug candi-
dates early in the drug development process can help to pre-
dict and avoid unnecessary adverse effects in later stages of 
development and after marketing.319
The first description of individual variability in response 
to toxicants was by the Greek philosopher and mathemati-
cian, Pythagoras. In 510 BCE, he noted that a fraction of 
individuals became ill or died as a result of consuming fava 
beans, while others showed no adverse effects.320 Ironically, 
not only did his crippling fear of fava beans lead to his 
untimely demise when he refused to enter a fava bean field 
to escape attackers, but it also laid the foundation for a new 
field of medical research. This prompted Janan Arslan, blog-
ger of “Gene Dosage”, to coin the tongue-in-cheek nickname 
“The Fava of Pharmacogenomics” to describe the math-
ematician well known to all students of geometry (http://
www.lateralmag.com/columns/gene-dosage/pythagoras- 
the-fava-of-pharmacogenomics). While this astute obser-
vation in ancient times was a harbinger of a now-bour-
geoning field, it took more than two thousand years to link 
Pythagoras’ insight with a gene mutation involved in the 
disposition of toxic metabolites produced by the fava bean 
plant.
The recent dramatic expansion of pharmacogenomics was 
empowered by the human genome project that revealed an 
unexpected level of genetic diversity among individuals of our 
species. The genomic sequence of unrelated individuals can 
vary by as much as 2% or approximately one single nucleotide 
polymorphism (SNP) for every 500 of the ~3 billion base pairs 
comprising our haploid genome. In addition to SNPs, there are 
also deletions, insertions, rearrangements, changes in chromo-
some number, variations in gene copy numbers, differences in 
nucleotide repeat copy numbers, and a wide variety of other 
differences in non-coding sequences. The vast knowledge of 
the genome gained in the last few decades has fueled a rapid 
expansion in the number of metabolizing genes and variant 
alleles linked with differential responses to xenobiotics and 
drugs.321–323
Variable responses of patients treated with the same drug 
were recognized as a common phenomenon by the 1950s,324–
326, and the term pharmacogenetics was first used by Friedrich 
Vogel in 1959327 to describe the relationship between inher-
ited, phenotypic differences in drug metabolism and variable 
responses of patients to the same dose of a drug. These early 
studies predicted that mutations affecting the activity, stabil-
ity, or expression of proteins involved in the metabolism of 
xenobiotic molecules were the basis for variation in efficacy, 
susceptibility, and toxicity of environmental chemicals and 
drugs. These predictions were borne out by the 1980s when 
multiple CYP450 genes were cloned and sequenced.328 In the 
1980s, several groups linked the extensive metabolizer (indi-
viduals with normal metabolic activity) and poor metabolizer 
(individuals with little to no functional metabolic activity) 
phenotypes for the antihypertensive drug debrisoquine to the 
CYP2D6 gene.329 Cloning and cDNA sequencing revealed that 
several poor metabolizer phenotypes are the result of mutant 
genes producing incorrectly spliced pre-mRNA.330 Since then, 
hundreds of variants have been characterized in genes encod-
ing metabolizing enzymes, and those with clinical significance 
are now referred to as pharmacogenes. Functional variants in 
Phase I or Phase II enzymes can result from mutations in cod-
ing sequences (affecting metabolic rates or enzyme stability), 
mutations in regulatory sequences (altering transcriptions), or 
complex rearrangements (e.g., those leading to changes in gene 
copy number). Variations in metabolism can also result from 
mutations in genes that indirectly affect the reaction kinetics 
by reducing the availability of enzyme cofactors, substrates, 
or allosteric regulators. Importantly, both loss-of-function 
and gain-of-function mutations are found among allelic vari-
ants. Collectively, mutations in metabolizing genes can lead to 
adverse effects, or conversely protect against adverse effects 
from exposures to endogenous substances or xenobiotics. 
Identifying and detecting the presence of variant alleles that 
affect an individual’s susceptibility to xenobiotic compounds 
is therefore important in preventing xenobiotics-induced tox-
icity. Toward that end, a number of databases and knowledge 
bases have been created to curate genetic and pharmacoge-
nomic data related to the metabolism of xenobiotics.331
	
1.	The Pharmacogenomics Knowledge base (PharmGKB): 
collection and management of pharmacogenetics eff­
ect; integration of information regarding the impact 
of human genetic variation on drug responses (www. 
pharmgkb.org)
	
2.	Pharmacogenetics of Absorption, Distribution, 
Metabolism, and Excretion genes (PharmaADME): 
development of ADME genes and genetic biomark-
ers that could predict pharmacokinetic variability 
and the subsequent impact on drug safety and effi-
cacy in the drug development process (www.phar-
maadme.org)
	
3.	The Human CYP450 Allele Nomenclature website: 
management of allele designations; summarization 
of genetic information on CYP450 alleles and their 
associated effects (http://www.cypalleles.ki.se/)
	
4.	The Human Arylamine N-Acetyltransferase Gene 
Nomenclature Committee: review and designation of 
NAT alleles (http://nat.mbg.duth.gr/)
	
5.	The UDP-Glucuronosyltransferase Allele Nomen­
clature Committee: review and designation of UGT 
alleles (www.pharmacogenomics.pha.ulaval.ca/sgc/
ugt_alleles)
The PharmGKB knowledge base was initiated in April 2000 
and is continuously updated with genomic, phenotype, and 
clinical information collected from ongoing pharmacogenetic 
studies. PharmGKB collects, curates, integrates, and dis-
seminates available knowledge regarding the effects of human 
genetic variation on responses to drugs. As of January 2021, 
the PharmGKB included annotations on 780 drug labels, 709 
drugs, 151 curated pathways, and 165 clinical guidelines. 
Requirements or recommendations may be general or only for 
a subset of patients and are classified into four categories:
•	 Genetic testing required: genetic testing, functional 
protein assays, or cytogenetic studies must be done 
before the use of the drug.

178
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
•	 Genetic testing recommended: genetic testing, 
functional protein assays, or cytogenetic studies is 
recommended before the use of the drug.
•	 Actionable pharmacogenomic: the label may con-
tain information about changes in efficacy, dosage, 
or toxicity due to genetic variants.
•	 Informative pharmacogenomic: information on 
the label suggests the involvement of metabolism or 
pharmacodynamics of the drugs, but genetic variants 
are mentioned for different drug responses.
4.5.1  Pharmacogenetics of Human 
Polymorphism in Phase I and 
Phase II Metabolizing Enzymes
The earliest investigations of xenobiotic metabolism identi-
fied variations in enzyme activities associated with altered 
drug response in a fraction of the treated populations. These 
findings revealed that variations in a single enzymatic activity 
were often associated with dramatic phenotypic differences. 
The concept that these variations were inherited was recog-
nized as early as the 1940s and led to the hypothesis that the 
metabolism of a specific drug is controlled by genes that show 
a Mendelian pattern of inheritance.332 The term pharmacoge-
netics was coined for the studies that examine the hereditary 
basis for observed differences in the responses of individuals 
and populations to drugs. Cohort and family studies examin-
ing the distribution of impaired metabolism and the resulting 
side effects of the anti-tuberculosis drug, isoniazid, provided 
the first evidence for a Mendelian autosomal recessive pattern 
of inheritance.333 Similar studies that compared the response 
of maternal versus fraternal twins to non-narcotic analgesic, 
antipyrine, further validated the genetic basis for differential 
drug metabolism in humans.334
Advances in pharmacogenetics were driven by the develop-
ment of molecular biology and genetic tools such as higher 
throughput DNA sequencing, genetic mapping, and associa-
tion studies in human cohorts. Application of these tools cul-
minated in cloning and sequencing of the first polymorphic 
human gene CYP2D6, which encodes an important metabo-
lizing enzyme CYP2D6.330 In 1991, more than 30 years after 
first being reported, inter-individual differences in the metabo-
lism of isoniazid were found to be due to inherited variants 
in the NAT2 gene, which encodes the NAT 2 enzyme.335,336 
Genotypes of individuals are in general classified into the 
following four types: (1) poor metabolizer – individuals with 
little to no functional metabolic activity; (2) intermediate 
metabolizer – individuals with decreased metabolic activity; 
(3) extensive metabolizer – individuals with normal metabolic 
activity; and (4) ultrarapid metabolizer – individuals with sub-
stantially enhanced metabolic activity.
Over the next twenty years, the pharmacogenomics of xeno-
biotic/drug metabolism was expanded to include essentially all 
SNPs in Phase I and Phase II enzymes, especially CYP450 
genes given their central role in drug metabolism.337 While a 
preponderance of past investigations focused on SNPs, more 
recent studies using high throughput whole genome or tar-
geted sequencing have revealed the importance of other types 
of mutations. For example, several alleles in the CYP2D6 are 
known to contain nucleotide deletions that result in a failure to 
produce CYP2D6 protein. Additional analyses identified and 
experimentally confirmed population-specific copy-number 
variants in 208 relevant pharmacogenes, including duplica-
tions in 201 (97%) pharmacogenes and several exonic dele-
tions.338 The frequency of copy-number variants in humans 
ranges from singletons up to 1% of the population. Copy-
number variants account for >5% of all loss-of-function alleles 
in ~40% of the genes evaluated, including novel deletions in 
CYP2C19 and CYP4F2. Individuals homozygous for these 
gene variations will be poor metabolizers of their substrates. 
In contrast, some individuals have multiple copies of the 
CYP2D6 gene, possibly due to gene duplication. These indi-
viduals have substantially enhanced capability to metabolize 
CYP2D6 substrates and are ultrarapid metabolizers.339
The CYD2D6 gene illustrates how variations in pharmaco-
genes can produce a spectrum in the efficiency of drug metab-
olism. A chemical that is metabolically activated by CYP2D6 
could be more toxic in ultrarapid metabolizers and less toxic 
in poor metabolizers. In contrast, a chemical whose detoxi-
fication and clearance depends on CYP2D6 would be more 
toxic than expected in poor metabolizers, but less toxic than 
expected in ultrarapid metabolizers. Drugs that are metabo-
lized by more than one polymorphic enzyme and in more 
than one organ would yield even more complex phenotypes 
that depend on the levels, activities, and tissue distribution 
of the enzymes they encode. To predict toxicity in exposed 
individuals, understanding the role and relative contributions 
of different metabolizing enzymes for a given compound is 
not enough. Toxicity is also affected by genotypes of all phar-
macogenes and phenotypes of drug-metabolizing enzymes 
that are involved, as well as enzyme inducers and inhibitors. 
Integration of all factors into risk assessment is the goal of 
pharmacogenomics.
Polymorphisms in CYP450 enzymes play an important role 
in the reduced efficacy and adverse effects of drugs among 
individuals and populations. Among the polymorphic CYP450 
isoforms, CYP2C9, CYP2C19, CYP2D6, and CYP3A5 play a 
major role in the disposition of drugs used clinically. Variants 
in CYP2B6, CYP2C8, and CYP3A4 also affect the pharmaco-
kinetics of drugs, but evidence regarding their clinical impli-
cations remains sparse. Polymorphisms are most important 
in pharmacogenes and have been the subject of many recent 
reviews.321,340 Here, we provide an up-to-date review of the 
major enzymes involved in xenobiotic and drug metabolism.
4.5.2  Pharmacogenetics of CYP450 Genes
The nomenclature of CYP450 genes and their variant alleles 
has been standardized by the Human Genome Nomenclature 
Committee.341 As shown in Figure 4.33, genes encoding 
CYP450 enzymes are designated with the root symbol CYP 
for the gene superfamily (CYP for human, and Cyp for mouse), 
followed by an Arabic number indicating the gene family, a 
capital letter designating the subfamily, and then by another 
Arabic number differentiating individual gene isoforms. 
Allelic variants of each isoform are indicated by an asterisk 
and an Arabic number. The *1 allele is usually considered to

179
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
be the normal or wild-type allele. The human gene name is 
always in italic font. For the nomenclature of CYP450 pro-
teins, the asterisk for each allele is replaced by a period and the 
names are in regular font. A complete list of CYP450 alleles 
is maintained by the Pharmacogene Variation Consortium 
(https://www.pharmvar.org).
4.5.2.1  CYP2A6
CYP2A6 is expressed primarily in the liver and is also present 
at lower levels in the nasal mucosa, sinuses. trachea, bronchi, 
and specific brain regions.342 The enzyme plays a significant 
role in the metabolism of clinically important drugs, environ-
mental toxicants, carcinogens, and endogenous substances 
(Table 4.9).343–345 The CYP2A6 gene is polymorphic with at 
least 40 alleles identified to date. Since several mutagens and 
carcinogens (e.g., N-nitrosamines in cigarette smoke) are acti-
vated by CYP2A6, researchers investigated the possibility that 
alleles with low catalytic capacity might decrease the risk of 
lung cancer in smokers. Indeed several, but not all studies sug-
gested a decreased incidence in lung cancer in poor metabo-
lizers. A meta-analysis indicated a reduced odds ratio of 0.25 
(95% confidence interval 0.16–0.39) for the null poor metabo-
lizer CYP2A6*4 compared with the wild-type CYP2A6*1 
homozygotes for tobacco-related cancers.344,345 Therealso 
appears to be a confluence of CYP2A6 activity and nicotinic 
acetylcholine receptors that bind the neurotransmitter and 
are responsible for the addictive sensation in smokers.342,346 
Nicotine is rapidly metabolized and has a plasma half-life of 
around 2 hours. Approximately 80% of nicotine is converted to 
cotinine in a two-step process in which the CYP2A6 enzyme 
plays a predominant role. Inter-individual variation in nicotine 
metabolism is largely explained by CYP2A6 polymorphism. 
Slow metabolizers (CYP2A6*2, *4, *9, and *12 alleles) are 
less likely to be smokers, and they smoke fewer cigarettes per 
day, have lower dependence, and are more able to quit smok-
ing compared to normal metabolizers. Recent genome-wide 
association studies also linked variant alleles of the nicotine 
receptor to tobacco addiction and lung cancer.346–348 This is 
a good example of how having two different alleles can over-
come an adverse outcome from exposure to a single substance. 
In addition to nicotine, CYP2A6 also affects the metabolism 
of numerous clinically used drugs. As a result, therapeutic rec-
ommendations based on phenotype have been developed for 
over 48 drugs metabolized by the enzyme. However, CYP2A6 
genotyping has not yet been implemented broadly into clinical 
practice (https://www.pharmgkb.org).
4.5.2.2  CYP2C9
CYP2C9 is the most abundantly expressed compared to other 
enzymes in the CYP2C subfamily and accounts for approxi-
mately 18% of hepatic CYP450 proteins. CYP2C9 is also 
expressed at high levels in the gastrointestinal tract. CYP2C9 is 
a major enzyme responsible for the disposition of many widely 
used drugs, including several that have a very narrow thera-
peutic index (Table 4.10) (FDA Table of Pharmacogenomic 
Biomarkers in Drug Labeling, https://www.fda.gov/drugs/
science-and-research-drugs/table-pharmacogenomic-bio-
markers-drug-labeling).349 The CYP2C9 gene is highly poly-
morphic.350 Genomic sequencing has identified numerous SNP 
and frameshift mutations, many of which are rare alleles. The 
most common alleles with decreased enzyme function are 
CYP2C9*2 and CYP2C9*3. Other alleles such as CYP2C9*8, 
CYP2C9*9, CYP2C9*11, and CYP2C9*14 that are less 
FIGURE 4.33  Nomenclature rules for CYP450 genes.

frequent in the populations can still have significant impacts 
on drug metabolism in carriers. Inter-individual variability of 
CYP2C9 enzyme activity is very broad with variant alleles 
decreasing activity levels by as much as ten-fold. As a result, 
CYP2C9 variants can have dramatic impacts on the efficacy 
and safety of drugs. For example, CYP2C9 substrates include 
coumarin anticoagulants that are used to prevent thromboem-
bolism in individuals at risk. Warfarin and structurally related 
acenocoumarol and phenprocoumon are the most widely used 
member of this drug class. These drugs target the enzyme 
vitamin K epoxide reductase and require individualized, 
genetically guided dosing to optimize coagulation. CYP2C9 
mutations that reduce the expression or activity of the enzyme 
can lead to excessive plasma concentrations and increased risk 
of bleeding episodes in affected individuals. The major allelic 
variants, CYP2C9*2 and CYP2C9*3, are both due to missense 
mutations. CYP2C9*2 decreases enzymatic activity by up to 
100%, depending on the substrates. Similarly, carriers of the 
CYP2C9*3 allele express significantly lower catalytic activity 
compared to the wild-type CYP2C9*1 genotype. As a result, 
warfarin was among the first drugs to include pharmacoge-
netic information in its label, including a dosing table based on 
the genotype of CYP2C9.
CYP2C9 also metabolizes a number of endogenous com-
pounds including arachidonic acid, linoleic acid, steroids, 
melatonin, and retinoids, as well as non-drug xenobiotics (e.g., 
tobacco products, galangin, methiocarb, pyrene, safrole, sul-
profos, and Δ-9-tetrahydrocannabinol). Several studies have 
associated the altered metabolism of endogenous or exogenous 
substances by variant alleles of CYP2C9 with cancer. For exam-
ple, decreased oxidation of arachidonic acid by CYP2C9*2 
and CYP2C9*3 impairs the biosynthesis of EETs, providing a 
direct protective role against tumorigenesis.351 Tobacco intake 
increases the risk of head and neck squamous cell carcinoma 
several-fold in individuals carrying CYP2C9*2 variant allele, 
suggesting that the normal allele plays an important role in the 
detoxification of carcinogens in tobacco.352 At least a dozen 
drugs currently in clinical use include recommendations or 
drug label warnings regarding the need for pharmacogenomic 
assessment of CYP2C9 prior to use in patients (https://www.
pharmgkb.org).353
4.5.2.3  CYP2C19
CYP2C19 is another important polymorphic pharmacogene that 
encodes CYP2C19 responsible for the metabolism of approxi-
mately 10% of marketed drugs (Table 4.11) (FDA Table of 
Pharmacogenomic Biomarkers in Drug Labeling, https://www.
fda.gov/drugs/science-and-research-drugs/table-pharmacoge-
nomic-biomarkers-drug-labeling).354,357 The two most common 
variants of CYP2C19 with low activity, CYP2C19*2 and 
CYP2C19*3, are the result of mutations that lead to a nonfunc-
tional CYP2C19 enzyme. The CYP2C19*2 harbors a mutation 
that affects RNA splicing, leading to a truncated protein, and 
CYP2C19*3 has a premature stop codon in exon 4. Reduced 
metabolism of S-mephenytoin in individuals with poor metabo-
lizer alleles of CYP2C19 leads to excessive sedation by this anti-
convulsant agent. Deficient S-mephenytoin metabolism is found 
in 2%–5% of Caucasians but is much more common in Asian 
populations with a frequency of 18%–23%.
The CYP2C19 enzyme contributes to the metabolism of a 
large number of drug classes such as antidepressants, ben-
zodiazepines, and proton pump inhibitors. In the case of 
tertiary amine tricyclic antidepressants (e.g., imipramine, 
amitriptyline, trimipramine, clomipramine, and nortripty-
line), plasma concentrations of active metabolite levels have 
been reported to be significantly altered in CYP2C19 poor 
metabolizers than in extensive metabolizers.355,356,357 As 
a result, reduced and loss-of-function alleles are likely to 
cause drug-induced adverse effects. CYP2C19 also metabo-
lizes selective serotonin-reuptake inhibitors, such as citalo-
pram, sertraline, fluoxetine, venlafaxine, and the reversible 
monoamine oxidase inhibitor moclobemide. Patients with 
one or two CYP2C19 loss-of-function alleles typically have 
higher sertraline serum concentrations compared to exten-
sive metabolizers.
Proton pump inhibitors are frequently used to treat gas-
troesophageal reflux, gastric and duodenal ulcers with

Helicobacter pylori infection, nonvariceal upper gastrointes-
tinal bleeding, or non-ulcer dyspepsia and mucosal erosion 
caused by NSAIDs. Low-function alleles in intermediate 
metabolizers and poor metabolizers have impaired drug elimi-
nation and increased plasma drug concentrations at standard 
therapeutic doses. As a result, healing rates of peptic ulcers 
and gastroesophageal reflux, and eradication of H. pylori 
infection with omeprazole, lansoprazole, and pantoprazole are 
more efficient in poor metabolizers with reduced enzymatic 
activity compared to extensive metabolizers.357,358
Variant alleles may also lead to impaired activation of pro-
drugs, thereby reducing therapeutic efficacy. Clopidogrel, a 
prodrug that inhibits platelet aggregation, is used extensively 
in patients with cardiovascular disease to treat and prevent 
strokes and infarction in conjunction with stents. It is the most 
specific and effective drug available to prevent blood clot-
ting. Clopidogrel works by binding irreversibly to the platelet 
purinergic receptor to prevent activation by adenosine diphos-
phate. A major advantage of clopidogrel is that it can be used 
in combination with other drugs such as aspirin to prevent 
clotting in patients.359 As shown in Figure 4.33, clopidogrel 
is a prodrug that is metabolized by several hepatic CYP450 
enzymes, including CYP2C19 to form 2-oxo-clopidogrel. 
The intermediate is further converted to the active metabolite 
that is released into the blood where it binds to platelets. As 
a result, poor metabolizers are at higher risk for thrombosis 
and myocardial infarction during clopidogrel therapy, whereas 
ultrarapid metabolizers have an increased risk of bleeding 
(Figure 4.34).
Gain-of-function mutations in CYP2C19 alleles are also 
found in individuals and specific populations. Sim et al. dis-
covered a novel gain-of-function allele (CYP2C19*17) with a 
frequency of 18% in both Swedes and Ethiopians and 4% in 
Chinese subjects.360 The gain-of-function CYP2C19*17 allele

harbors two transversions at positions C806T and C3402T in
