Variants that are heterozygous (CYP2C19*1/*17) and homo-
zygous for the gain-of-function allele *17/*17 double copy 
genotypes are both considered to have an ultrarapid metabo-
lizer phenotype. In Swedes homozygous for CYP2C19*17 
allele, metabolism of omeprazole by CYP2C19 is about 50% 
higher than in individuals with wild-type CYP2C19*1 allele 
(P = 0.010). In Ethiopians, a similar difference was observed 
between individuals homozygous for CYP2C19*1 and those 
homozygous for CYP2C19*17 (P = 0.013). Most, but not all 
studies have found that the ultrarapid metabolizer genotype 
alters omeprazole pharmacokinetics, resulting in decreased 
drug exposures at standard dosages.361–363 At least 21 drugs 
currently in clinical use include recommendations or drug 
label warnings regarding the need for pharmacogenomic 
assessment of CYP2C19 before use in patients (https://www.
pharmgkb.org).353
4.5.2.4  CYP2D6
Genetic analysis of the inter-individual variability in debriso-
quine metabolism led to the discovery of CYP2D6 as the first 
polymorphic CYP450 gene to be cloned. CYP2D6 accounts 
for about 2% of the total hepatic CYP450 protein and plays 
a major role in the metabolism of ~20% of all drugs in clini-
cal use.337 Substrates include major classes of drugs, such as 
selective serotonin–reuptake inhibitors, tricyclic antidepres-
sants, opioids, cardiovascular drugs, antiemetics, antimalarial 
drugs, and selective noradrenaline transport blockers used to 
treat attention deficit hyperactivity disorder (Table 4.12). As 
a result, variant alleles have a significant impact on altered 
drug response in patients (FDA Table of Pharmacogenomic 
Biomarkers in Drug Labeling, https://www.fda.gov/drugs/
science-and-research-drugs/table-pharmacogenomic-bio-
markers-drug-labeling).345 At least 100 known variants of 
CYP2D6 (including copy-number variants) are associated 
with altered enzyme activities or expressions (https://www.
pharmvar.org/gene/CYP2D6). The enzymatic function of 
these allelic variants ranges between nonfunctional and ultra-
rapid metabolizers. The latter usually harbors duplicated two 
functional CYP2D6 alleles. CYP2D6 allele frequencies vary 
substantially among different populations and ethnicities. 
The normal CYP2D6 allele and the non-functioning variant 
defining CYP2D6*4 are most frequent in European, African, 
South Asian, and admixed American populations with allele 
frequencies of 27%–36% and 12%–16%, respectively. Other 
alleles tend to show more restricted population distributions.
The tremendous variability of CYP2D6 leads to adverse drug 
effects via different mechanisms. For example, the commonly 
used opioid analgesic, codeine, is a prodrug that is bioactivated 
to morphine by CYP2D6. As a result, poor metabolizers will 
have reduced pain relief at normal doses. Ultrarapid metabo-
lizers will have a higher rate of biotransformation, sometimes 
resulting in severe or life-threatening adverse effects from 
morphine even at small doses of codeine.364 Several studies 
have demonstrated that in addition to monoamine oxidase, 
CYP2D6 can metabolize 1-methyl-4-phenyl-1,2,3,6-tetrahy-
dropyridine (MPTP) to its toxic metabolite. The toxic metab-
olite rapidly induces Parkinson-like syndrome in humans, 
nonhuman primates, and mice. Inhibitor studies showed that 
CYP2D6 activity in dopaminergic neurons was required for 
the formation of the toxic metabolite, suggesting that null or 
low activity alleles may protect against this Parkinson-like 
syndrome.365,366,367 The antiestrogenic agent Tamoxifen, used 
in the treatment of breast cancer is bioactivated by CYP2D6. 
As a result, poor metabolizers will exhibit lower concentra-
tions of the active metabolite and will have a higher risk of 
cancer recurrence. Conversely, if a drug is inactivated by 
CYP2D6 before its clearance, ultrarapid metabolizers with 
higher CYP2D6 enzymatic activity can quickly inactivate the 
drug, leading to a failure to attain therapeutic drug exposures. 
At least 60 drugs currently in clinical use include recommen-
dations or drug label warnings regarding the need for phar-
macogenomic assessment of CYP2D6 prior to use in patients 
(https://www.pharmgkb.org).353

4.5.2.5  CYP3A4
CYP3A4, which encodes the most abundant CYP450 enzyme 
in the human liver, is also prevalent in the intestinal mucosa. 
CYP3A4 also has the most promiscuous substrate specific-
ity, accounting for the metabolism of ~50%–60% of drugs 
used clinically as well as many other xenobiotics. It, there-
fore, plays a major role in first-pass metabolism in the small 
intestine and liver, especially in the case of oral exposures.328 
CYP3A4 has been implicated in delayed diagnosis of injury 
(DDI) as well as drug interactions with compounds in food 
and herbal supplements including grapefruit, black pepper, 
St. John’s wort, and goldenseal root.368,369 The prevalent 
pharmacogene has approximately 40 alleles that alter the 
expression or activity of the enzyme. Variant alleles span 
the typical range of phenotypes, ranging from poor to ultra-
rapid metabolizers that are differentially distributed among 
ethnicities.
A recent study370 analyzed a large database of 141,456 
individuals and found 856 SNPs, 312 of which are missense 
mutations in CYP3A4. Thus, the frequency of polymor-
phisms vastly exceeds the 40 alleles reported previously. The 
distribution of these mutations differs among ethnic groups. 
Although the clinical utility of these new variants remains to 
be determined, those confirmed to alter enzymatic functions 
have been shown to affect the metabolism of a wide variety of 
drugs, foods, herbal supplements, and toxicants. For example, 
cabozantinib, a multi-tyrosine kinase inhibitor, is an impor-
tant anticancer drug with a wide range of applications in the 
clinic. Recent in vitro findings indicated that different CYP3A4 
variants exhibited significantly different clearance rates of 
cabozantinib.370 These studies predict significant potential 
for drug-induced adverse effects, ranging from increased 
drug exposure and associated toxicity in poor metabolizers to 
therapeutic failure in ultrarapid metabolizers when receiving 
standard dosages. Human CYP3A4 plays a major role in the 
bioactivation of environmental chemicals, such as aflatoxin 
B1, the flame retardant tris(2,3-dibromopropyl)phosphate, and 
several PAHs to highly mutagenic metabolites. The enzyme 
can also inactivate certain substrates, including alternate oxi-
dative detoxification pathways for aflatoxin B1.371 CYP3A4 
polymorphisms can impact the bioactivation or detoxification 
process, leading to altered efficacy or toxicity.
4.5.3  Genetic Differences in UGT Activity
UGTs are membrane-bound Phase II conjugating enzymes 
that catalyze the transfer of the glucuronic acid group of UDP-
glucuronic acid to specific hydroxyl, carboxyl, amino, and 
sulfur groups on hydrophobic substrates. UGTs catalyze the 
glucuronidation of a variety of endogenous compounds, such 
as bilirubin, bile acids, and steroid hormones. These enzymes 
also metabolize xenobiotics including drugs from almost all 
therapeutic classes, toxicants, carcinogens, and their Phase I 
metabolites. Mutations in UGT1A were first associated with 
specific heritable diseases related to the metabolism of bili-
rubin. Variant alleles of these conjugating enzymes were also 
later associated with adverse responses to many drugs in clini-
cal use.372–375
Deficiencies in UGT activity result in excess unconjugated 
hyperbilirubinemias. Gilbert’s syndrome is a mild form of 
UGT deficiency that occurs in 2%–5% of the population. 
These patients are characterized by having mild, chronic, 
unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia that produces jaundice and 
an impaired ability to metabolize menthol. The prevalence of 
this disease in the population makes the associated UGT1A 
deficiency an important pharmacogene to evaluate for many 
drugs and other xenobiotic exposures. Gilbert’s patients are 
usually homozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele that produces 
reduced amounts of the enzyme due to mutations in the gene 
promoter.374 Patients with Gilbert’s syndrome are at increased 
risk from exposure to drugs that are cleared by UGT1A includ-
ing tolbutamide, rifamycin, josamycin, and acetaminophen.
From the perspective of drug metabolism, pharmacogenomic 
analyses have identified the association of many UGTs with 
altered drug metabolisms. The important anticancer prodrug, 
irinotecan, is converted to the active SN-38 by carboxylester-
ases. Subsequent inactivation of SN-38 is mainly mediated by 
UGT1A1 and UGT1A7 (detoxification). However, the effect of 
UGT1A polymorphism on the metabolism of irinotecan was 
inconsistent among studies including Gilbert patients. This 
may not be surprising since the UGT1A1*28 allele associated 
with Gilbert’s syndrome only reduces transcription of wild-
type UGT1A1* by ~30%. As a result, patients carrying other 
low-function UGT1A genes may have significantly decreased 
detoxification capacity and consequently may be more suscep-
tible to irinotecan-induced toxicity. This possibility was con-
firmed by a recent study that examined allelic variants in 105 
cancer patients treated with irinotecan.376 This pharmacoge-
nomic study found that toxicity was more likely in patients 
with Gilbert’s syndrome carrying the UGT1A1*28 allele when 
combined with reduced function UGT1A7 alleles carrying 
N129K/R131K and UGT1A7–57T/G SNPs. These two alleles 
were previously shown to reduce the function of UGT1A7 pro-
tein, or a 70% reduction of gene transcription. This analysis 
explained why patients with Gilbert’s syndrome carrying these 
poor metabolizing alleles of UGT1A7 are therefore at higher 
risk of irinotecan-induced leukopenia and thrombocytope-
nia than those carrying only variant UGT1A1*28 alleles. The 
analysis of two pharmacogenes illustrates the fact that pharma-
cogenetic analysis of single metabolic genes can be mislead-
ing, as few xenobiotics are metabolized by a single enzyme. 
Rather, there is significant redundancy among the different 
families of metabolic enzymes and usually involves sequential 
steps by Phase I and Phase II enzymes, most of which can be 
polymorphic. Pathways can also be dependent on polymorphic 
genes that affect metabolism indirectly through effects on the 
metabolism of cofactors, reaction substrates, and others.
4.5.4  Polymorphism of GST
GST polymorphism has been reported in the GSTA1, GSTM1, 
GSTP1, GSTT1, GSTO1, and GSTZ1.377,378 Polymorphisms of 
GSTM1 and GSTT1 have been extensively studied with respect 
to DNA adducts and increased risk of cancer in epidemiologic 
studies.379–381 The homozygous deletions or null genotypes 
of GST1*0 and GSTM1*0 exist in a high percentage of the 
human population with major ethnic differences.381,382 Studies

184
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
of ethnic differences in the frequency of nonfunctional GSTT1 
revealed that East Asians have the highest percentage of indi-
viduals with the homozygous GSTT1*0 trait. The lowest per-
centage was reported in Scandinavia and Mexican American 
regions. In South India, approximately 30% of the population 
lacks the GSTM1 gene, 17% lacks the GSTT1 gene, and 5% 
lack both GSTM1 and GSTT1 genes. Approximately 50% of 
the Caucasian population carries homozygous GSTM1*0, and 
thus lacks functional GSTM1-1 enzyme.383
Both GSTM1 and GSTT1 isoenzymes play an important role 
in the detoxification of environmental chemicals and therapeu-
tic drugs, particularly in the deactivation of reactive oxygen 
species to reduce the risk of inflammation and degenerative 
diseases. GSTM1-1 is an essential enzyme in the deactivation 
of genotoxic epoxides generated during the Phase I metabo-
lism of PAHs. GSTT1-1 is important for the Phase II metabo-
lism of halogenated hydrocarbons and ethylene oxides.384 
Individuals with the null genotype of GSTT1*0 have a 1.5–3 
times higher likelihood of forming protein adducts in both 
albumin and globin after exposure to ethylene oxide.385 Loss of 
GSTM1-1 and GSTT1-1 isozyme function in humans is associ-
ated with a higher risk of lung and bladder cancers following 
chemical inactivated by these enzymes.381 Polymorphisms of 
GSTs can also reduce the capability to modulate free radicals 
produced during normal physiological processes and may 
be a contributing factor for Alzheimer’s disease and glioma. 
Consistent with this hypothesis, GSTM1 and GSTT1 activities 
are significantly decreased in all brain regions of patients with 
Alzheimer’s disease. The decreased GST levels could lead to 
the imbalance of reactive oxygen species and oxidative stress 
in neurons.
4.5.5  Pharmacogenomics of Glucose-6-
Phosphate Dehydrogenase (G6PD)
In 510 BCE, the Greek philosopher and mathematician, 
Pythagoras, noticed that only a fraction of individuals become 
ill or died as a result of consuming fava beans, while others 
showed no adverse effects.320 Modern science has discovered 
that favism is caused by inherited mutations that significantly 
reduce the activity of G6PD. This enzyme catalyzes the first 
step of the pentose phosphate pathway which is the major mech-
anism for the generation of cytoplasmic NADPH (Figure 4.35). 
NADPH:P450 oxidoreductase is essential for CYP450 cataly-
sis. Most eukaryotic CYP450s are coupled with NADPH:P450 
oxidoreductase that requires NADPH as a cofactor. As a result, 
G6PD is essential for CYP450-mediated metabolism of xeno-
biotics and drugs.386 G6PD also plays a major role in inactivat-
ing drugs, chemicals, and reactive metabolites by regenerating 
reduced glutathione which is also used as a substrate for GST-
mediated conjugation reactions. The conjugated substrates are 
then transported out of cells for excretion. Exposure to drugs 
and toxicants, including isouramil and divicine present in 
fava beans can lead to the formation of reactive oxygen spe-
cies that rapidly deplete NADPH in individuals with reduced 
function G6PD alleles.322 By contrast, the pentose phosphate 
pathway in normal individuals generates a sufficient amount 
of NADPH to overcome the induced oxidative stress and plays 
a critical role in preventing oxidative stress.
The pentose phosphate pathway is especially important 
in red blood cells. G6PD deficiency is an inherited error of 
metabolism that predisposes to red blood cell breakdown. 
Mature erythrocytes lack a nucleus, mitochondria, and 
FIGURE 4.35  Role of pentose phosphate pathway in the production of NADPH and reduced glutathione. Abbreviations: GSH, glutathione (reduced 
glutathione); GSSG, glutathione disulfide (oxidized glutathione); NADP+, nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate; NADPH, nicotinamide ade-
nine dinucleotide phosphate hydrogen (reduced NADP+).

185
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
ribosomes and hence are unable to synthesize new proteins. In 
red blood cells, NADPH produced by the pentose phosphate 
pathway is required to maintain an adequate pool of reduced 
glutathione, which plays a crucial role in removing hydrogen 
peroxide and preventing the oxidation of hemoglobin and 
erythrocyte lysis. The pentose phosphate pathway is the only 
source of reduced glutathione in mature red blood cells. As a 
result, red blood cells are exquisitely susceptible to oxidative 
stress. In individuals with mutant alleles of G6PD, especially 
the severe Mediterranean variants, NADPH is rapidly depleted 
by oxidative stress, especially in erythrocytes. As a result, 
exposure of individuals with reduced levels of G6PD to stress-
ors (e.g., drugs, toxicants, or infections) can therefore trigger 
a variety of conditions including acute hemolytic anemia, 
neonatal hyperbilirubinemia leading to kernicterus, chronic 
non-spherocytic hemolysis, spontaneous abortions, jaundice, 
favism, and even death.387
Adding to its importance, G6PD deficiencies comprise the 
most common inherited human enzyme defects, affecting an 
estimated 400 million people worldwide. The G6PD gene is 
located in the human X chromosome (Xq28). G6PD deficiency 
results primarily from a single point mutation in the coding 
region of the gene that encodes the 515 amino acid protein 
(https://www.genecards.org).388 More than 300 variants have 
been identified within the base coding region, including three 
splice variants. These variants are classified into five categories 
by the World Health Organization based on the severity of the 
clinical sequelae. Type 1 mutations include those that induce 
the most severe clinical sequelae, while Type 5 comprises 60% 
of these mutations that do not impart a clinical phenotype. 
More than 140 variants that affect the function or stability of 
the enzyme have been identified and associated with varying 
degrees of enzyme activity. Enzyme deficiency leads to chronic 
non-spherocytic hemolytic anemia (Class I) to mild or almost 
normal enzyme activity (> 60% normal activity and no obvi-
ous clinical sequelae, Class IV). The variants associated with 
the most severe clinical outcomes include the G6PD-A variant 
which affects 10% of Africans389 and the G6PHD-Mediterranean 
variant which is most prevalent in Mediterranean countries.390 
G6PD deficiency accounts for thousands to tens of thousands 
of deaths annually, primarily among people of African, Middle 
Eastern, and South Asian ancestries.
Although G6PDH enzyme activity is not directly involved 
in the metabolism of drugs or toxicants, it is nonetheless an 
important pharmacogene. G6PD plays a central albeit indirect 
role in the metabolism of numerous xenobiotic compounds or 
drugs. NADPH is an essential cofactor (reducing agent) for 
many enzymes comprising Phase I and Phase II metabolism. 
As such, G6PD deficiencies are a major contributor to the list 
of more than 30 drugs that have been discontinued or come 
with black box warnings regarding the need for pharmacoge-
nomics assessment of G6PD (https://www.pharmgkb.org).353
4.6  Metabolism-Induced Organ-
Specific Toxicity
Metabolism plays a central role in chemical-induced toxic-
ity. Xenobiotics including drugs entering the body undergo 
biotransformation via Phase I and Phase II metabolic path-
ways. Some metabolites are unstable and can react with cel-
lular components. Relatively innocuous substances can be 
converted to highly toxic metabolites. The liver is a promi-
nent site for toxic injury by agents requiring metabolic acti-
vation. However, the liver can also be the source of reactive 
metabolites that cause toxicities in extrahepatic organs. Organ 
selective toxicity can also result from the in situ metabolic 
activation of foreign chemicals in extrahepatic target tissues 
such as the lungs and the kidneys. In this section, we will 
explore how metabolites can lead to organ toxicity in several 
major organ systems.
4.6.1  Liver Toxicity
Strategically located between the digestive system and the 
systemic circulation (Figure 4.36), the liver is vital for nor-
mal body function and metabolism. The liver plays a central 
role in maintaining energy homeostasis and gluconeogenesis, 
synthesis of plasma proteins (e.g., albumin and clotting fac-
tors), storage of glycogen, vitamins, and minerals, as well as 
production and excretion of bile. The liver is also the princi-
pal “metabolic powerhouse” for both endogenous chemicals 
FIGURE 4.36  Liver: strategical location between the digestive system 
and the systemic circulation. Strategic placement of portal circulation 
and the liver between the digestive system and the systemic circulation. 
Blood flows from the gastrointestinal tract and other abdominal organs 
and passes through the portal vein, hepatic sinusoids (special vascular 
structures in the liver) and then enters the hepatic vein before flowing 
to the heart from the inferior vena cava. This pathway allows the liver 
to process xenobiotics entering the body from the gastrointestinal tract.

(e.g., cholesterol, steroid hormones, fatty acids, and proteins) 
and xenobiotics (e.g., drugs, industrial agents, alcohol, and 
herbal supplements). The central role played by the liver in the 
biotransformation and clearance of chemicals makes hepato-
cytes particularly vulnerable to xenobiotics-induced injury. In 
the liver, CYP450s show preferential localization in the centri-
lobular hepatocytes. Among all CYP450 enzymes in the human 
liver, the CYP3A4 protein content is the most abundant, fol-
lowed by CYP2C9 and CYP2E1, representing approximately 
40%, 18%, and 15% of the total CYP450s, respectively.391 In 
addition to CYP450s, other Phase I enzymes contribute to the 
clearance of a wider variety of drugs, xenobiotics, and endog-
enous substrates, including FMOs, monoamine oxidases, 
molybdenum hydroxylases, ADHs, ALDHs, aldo-keto reduc-
tase, NADPH:quinone reductases, and hydrolytic enzymes. 
The most common Phase II drug-metabolizing enzymes in the 
liver are UDP-glucuronosyltransferases, SULTs, NATs, GSTs, 
thiopurine S-methyltransferases, and COMTs. A few classic 
examples of hepatic metabolism-associated toxic events are 
described below.
Ethanol: The prototypical hepatotoxicant consumed by a 
large fraction of the human population is alcohol. Ethanol is 
absorbed primarily from the gastrointestinal tract and quickly 
reaches the liver, where it is subject to first-pass metabolism. 
The major metabolic pathway for processing alcohol in the liver 
is its conversion to acetaldehyde by ADH in the hepatocytes. 
The intermediate metabolite, acetaldehyde, is then oxidized to 
the acetate by ALDH2 in mitochondria. Accumulation of acet-
aldehyde is toxic and can result in flushes on the face, neck, and 
shoulders. Both ADH and ALDH genes are polymorphic among 
ethnicities and have effects on alcohol consumption and addic-
tion. The best-known variation of the alcohol-metabolizing 
enzyme is encoded by the ALDH2*2 allele which results in a 
nearly inactive ALDH2 enzyme. People carrying an ALDH2*2 
allele show an alcohol flush reaction, even when they consume 
a small amount of alcohol.392 Chronic alcohol consumption 
results in hepatic accumulation of acetaldehyde that is highly 
toxic due to multiple mechanisms including oxidative stress, 
glutathione depletion, lipid peroxidation, and formation of pro-
teins adducts. Chronic alcohol consumption induces CYP2E1 
activity, which can also metabolize alcohol. The CYP2E1-
mediated alcohol metabolism leads to the generation of reac-
tive oxygen species that induce various deleterious effects on 
hepatocytes (e.g., dysregulation of fatty acid synthesis and oxi-
dation), leading to severe liver toxicity. Alcoholic liver disease 
involves a complex progression of sequelae. Histopathologic 
changes include accumulation of fat, development of steato-
hepatitis, and progressive fibrosis leading to cirrhosis.
Acetaminophen: Tylenol®/Acetaminophen (Paracetamol or 
APAP) remains one of the most widely used analgesic/anti-
pyretic agents worldwide due to its effectiveness and lack of 
side effects at therapeutic doses. However, acetaminophen 
overdose leads to irreversible liver failure. Tragically, over-
doses from both unintentional (e.g., parents inadvertently giv-
ing multiple doses to a child) or intentional overdoses (used 
in attempted suicides) remain a significant problem, with an 
estimated 56,000 emergency room visits, 26,000 hospitaliza-
tions, and as many as 500 deaths annually in the United States. 
Acetaminophen-induced liver injury is a classic example of 
how metabolic activation and detoxification can affect toxic-
ity (Figure 4.36).393,394 Fortunately, the potent antioxidant, 
N-acetylcysteine is a highly effective antidote when admin-
istered early after an overdose. When taken at therapeutic 
doses, acetaminophen is rapidly absorbed from the intestine 
and transported to the liver. Acetaminophen is eliminated 
metabolically in the liver primarily by sulfate conjugation 
of the phenyl hydroxyl group; however, competing with this 
high-affinity, low-capacity pathway is elimination via gluc-
uronidation also of the hydroxyl group. At high doses of acet-
aminophen, PAPS pools become depleted and rate limiting, 
and the secondary pathway of glucuronidation predominates. 
Glucuronidation thus provides a backup route of elimination. 
The saturation of these two elimination pathways allows more 
acetaminophen to flow through the oxidation pathways. In 
liver, acetaminophen is oxidized predominantly by CYP2E1 
to yield the NAPQI. This pathway of metabolic activation 
forms reactive species that bind to critical macromolecules 
in the liver. Under acetaminophen overdose, sulfonation and 
glucuronidation pathways are saturated, resulting in accumu-
lated acetaminophen being bioactivated to excessive and toxic 
NAPQI. The formation of excessive NAPQI subsequently 
depletes hepatic glutathione and covalently binds to sulfhy-
dryl groups in cellular proteins. This results in mitochondrial 
oxidative stress and dysfunction, ultimately leading to hepato-
cyte necrosis. Inducers of CYP2E1, such as ethanol, increase 
hepatic capacity for CYP2E1-mediated oxidation and may 
enhance hepatotoxicity (Figure 4.37).
Non-Nucleoside 
Reverse 
Transcriptase 
Inhibitors 
(NNTRIs): Liver toxicity is one of the most common adverse 
effects of antiretroviral therapy in HIV-infected patients. 
Among the NNTRIs, the frequency of severely increased 
transaminases (indication of impaired liver function) in 
patients receiving efavirenz ranges from 1% to 8%, whereas 
in patients treated with nevirapine (NVP), it ranges from 4% 
to 18%. Clinical data have shown that NVP usually causes 
severe liver toxicity starting in the first six weeks of treatment. 
In 2000, the U.S. FDA issued a black box warning on NVP-
induced life-threatening liver toxicity and skin reactions. 
Due to the unacceptably high risk of serious liver damage in 
patients, the U.S. Department of Health and Human Services 
released a guideline to restrict the use of NVP to patients at 
lowest risk for adverse effects, unless the benefit to the patient 
clearly outweighs the risk. Studies have shown that the bioacti-
vation of NVP is responsible for liver injury. In humans, NVP 
is differentially metabolized by CYP3A4 and CYP2B6.395 
CYP2B6 catalyzes NVP to its 3- and 8-hydroxy metabolites 
that are primarily eliminated in the urine as glucuronides. In 
contrast to CYP2B6, CYP3A4 bioactivates NVP to 2- and 
12-hydroxy NVP, both of which are further metabolized by 
SULT1A1 or oxidized to form multiple reactive metabolites, 
such as quinone methide, quinoneimine, 2,3-NVP-quinone, 
and NVP-2,3-epoxide. These reactive metabolites have been 
reported to form covalent bindings to hepatic proteins, ulti-
mately causing acute hepatitis.
Ragwort: Tansy ragwort (Senecio jacobea) is a tall daisy-
like plant with yellow flowers that grows in hayfields and pas-
tures. Ingestion of the poisonous Tansy ragwort by grazing 
livestock leads to irreversible liver damage. Ragworts contain

187
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
pyrrolizidine alkaloids, which occur in 3% of the flora world-
wide. Pyrrolizidine alkaloids in plants are not inherently toxic. 
However, following ingestion of ragwort, the pyrrolizidine 
alkaloids are absorbed from the intestine and pass through 
the liver, where they are rapidly metabolized by CYP3A and 
CYP2B to form reactive and cytotoxic pyrroles. The pyrro-
lic metabolites bind to liver macromolecules, eliciting cell 
injury.396
Aflatoxin B1: A liver toxicant that frequently contaminates 
food consumed by both livestock and humans is aflatoxin 
B1. Produced by Aspergillus fungi that contaminate grains, 
peanuts, and other food sources, aflatoxins B1-induced liver 
injury is another classic example of metabolite-induced toxic-
ity. Aflatoxin B1 has a complex pattern of metabolism in the 
liver that is initiated by CYP450. The major source of hepato-
toxicity is via the highly reactive AFB1–8,9-epoxide formed 
by CYP3A4-mediated oxidation reactions. The AFB1–8,9-
epoxide is one of the most mutagenic compounds known and 
can lead to acute toxicity and death by inducing significant 
DNA damage within the cell. The epoxide is also a potent liver 
carcinogen in animals and humans. Surprisingly, some strains 
of mice are relatively resistant to the carcinogenic effects of 
aflatoxins B1. Studies suggest that these differences are in part 
due to increased activity or expression of GST that conjugate 
and inactivate the epoxide in the mice. There are inter-indi-
vidual variations in the rate of activation between children 
and adults. The rate of AFB1–8,9-epoxide formation and its 
conjugation with glutathione are the key parameters in inter-
species and inter-individual differences in sensitivity to the 
toxic effect of aflatoxins B1.292
4.6.2  Intestinal Toxicity
As one of the largest environmentally facing organs, the epi-
thelium lining the alimentary canal plays a central role in 
absorbing, distributing, metabolizing, and eliminating a vast 
FIGURE 4.37  Metabolism of acetaminophen and pathways lead to liver toxicity. Major detoxification pathways are indicated by open arrows and 
include sulfonation and glucuronidation. Bioactivation of acetaminophen involves oxidation to NAPQI, and it may be catalyzed by several P450 
isozymes as well as PHS. At therapeutic doses, conjugation pathways predominate and bioactivation is negligible. Following overdose, conjugation 
pathways become saturated and oxidation to the reactive intermediate NAPQI becomes significant. NAPQI may be detoxified by reaction with reduced 
GSH, either by a spontaneous or GST-catalyzed reaction, and excreted as the corresponding mercapturate. At high exposure levels, GSH may become 
depleted, allowing NAPQI to react with tissue nucleophiles, resulting in toxicity.

188
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
array of compounds found in foods, water, drugs, and even 
inhaled particulates swallowed after being removed from the 
airways by mucociliary clearance. While the primary function 
of the intestine is digestion and absorption of nutrients, the gut 
and its associated microbiome play a crucial role in protecting 
the body against toxic compounds. Often this involves absorp-
tion and shunting directly to the liver for first-pass metabolism/
detoxification.397 Recent studies indicate that the intestinal epi-
thelium expresses many membrane transporters as well as a 
plethora of Phase I and Phase II enzymes.398,399 Among the 
Phase I enzymes, CYP3A4 and CYP3A5 are expressed at the 
highest levels. CYP3A4/5 are important CYP450 enzymes 
and account for the metabolism of the majority of marketed 
drugs.328 CYP3A5 has lower enzymatic activity321 and is 
involved in the metabolism of drugs used to treat common 
infectious diseases: malaria (artemether, lumefantrine, meflo-
quine, primaquine, chloroquine), HIV/AIDS (efavirenz, saqui-
navir, maraviroc, indinavir, nelfinavir, ritonavir, lopinavir), 
and tuberculosis (rifampicin). In the case of Phase II metabo-
lizing enzymes, the intestine also plays an important role in 
inactivating and conjugating xenobiotics and drugs. Of the 
extrahepatic tissues, the intestinal mucosa contains the highest 
activities of acetyltransferases (50% of the liver) and SULT 
(30% of the liver), and it also contains high levels of UGTs, 
carboxylesterases, and AO.400
First-pass metabolism of drugs and xenobiotics is often con-
sidered to occur primarily in the liver via the portal circula-
tion from the gut. However, the fact that the intestine is the 
first organ where many oral drugs and ingested xenobiotics 
are absorbed, coupled with high-level expressions of Phase I 
and II enzymes, prompted Dr. Thummel to defend the pos-
sibility that first-pass metabolism can also occur in the intes-
tinal mucosa.397 His review was based on the elegant studies 
of van Herwaarden et al. who showed that expressing human 
CYP3A4 in the intestine of Cyp3a null (−/−) mice dramatically 
decreased the absorption of docetaxel into the bloodstream. 
By contrast, the expression of human CYP3A in the liver 
aided systemic docetaxel clearance. These observations sug-
gested that the expression of CYP3A in the intestinal mucosa 
impaired drug absorption and increased in situ clearance.401
CYP3A4 has been implicated in adverse drug-drug interac-
tions and drug interactions with food and herbal supplements 
including grapefruit, black pepper, St. John’s wort, and gold-
enseal root.368,402 The unexpected finding that grapefruit juice 
can alter oral drug bioavailability was demonstrated from sev-
eral interaction studies including a study between ethanol and 
felodipine (a calcium channel antagonist). Felodipine is pri-
marily metabolized by CYP3A4 in apical enterocytes of the 
intestine and hepatocytes of the liver. Grapefruit juice used 
to mask the taste of the ethanol in the human study decreased 
felodipine metabolism by inhibiting CYP3A4 expression in the 
intestinal wall. The high inter-individual variation was shown 
to result from inherited differences in enteric CYP3A4 protein 
expression. Since then, many other drugs have been shown to 
be affected by grapefruit juice, and avoidance of this popular 
breakfast drink is in the warning section on many drug labels.
The number of drugs that are associated with toxicity in the 
intestinal mucosa is vast and includes most NSAIDs and many 
chemotherapeutic drugs.403 The mechanisms of toxicity can be 
due to enhanced bioactivation (ultrarapid metabolizers) that 
increase in situ activation of prodrugs. Toxicity can also be 
driven by hepatic metabolites that are further biotransformed 
in the gut by the microbiome. An example of how local, sys-
temic and microbial metabolism of xenobiotics interact to 
induce mucosal toxicity is illustrated by an anticancer drug, 
irinotecan (Figure 4.38). Irinotecan is administered as a pro-
drug by intravenous infusions. It enters hepatocytes by pas-
sive effusion. Hepatic carboxylesterases 1 and 2 bioactivate 
the prodrug to its active and toxic metabolite, SN-38, which is 
then deactivated to SN-38 glucuronide (SN-38G) by UGT1A1. 
FIGURE 4.38  Metabolism of irinotecan and intestinal toxicity. Created with BioRender.com.

189
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
Low-activity alleles of UGT1A1 have been linked to increased 
toxicity, including severe neutropenia and delayed diarrhea.404 
The inactivated SN-38G is exported from the liver to the intes-
tine via bile. Once in the intestine, SN-38G is taken up by bac-
teria in the feces. Bacterial beta-glucuronidases scavenge the 
sugar moiety as an energy source, and in the process reactivate 
SN-38G to its toxic metabolite, leading to severe and dose-
limiting diarrhea in patients.405 Irinotecan is also degraded to 
inactive metabolites by CYP3A4/5. A similar mechanism of 
bacterial-mediated toxicity is seen with major NSAIDs such 
as diclofenac, ketophen, and indomethacin. With any of these 
drugs, the release of the active drug by bacterial enzymes 
results in focal erosion of the intestinal mucosa, leading to 
dose-limiting ulceration and bloody diarrhea. Since crystal 
structures of bacterial beta-glucuronidases vary significantly 
from those of mammalian isoforms, investigators have devel-
oped inhibitors to inhibit only the bacterial enzymes, limit-
ing the drug-induced toxicities.405 Preclinical studies suggest 
that when given in combination with drugs that are released 
from their glucuronidated metabolites by intestinal microbi-
omes, these inhibitors can dramatically reduce in situ toxicity 
in rodents.406
4.6.3  Kidney Toxicity
The kidney is a major clearance organ of the body and is 
responsible for the elimination of many xenobiotics and pre-
scription drugs. Kidneys carry out extensive oxidation, reduc-
tion, hydrolysis, and conjugation reactions.407,408 Given their 
high metabolic biotransformation capability combined with 
high blood flow, glomerular filtration, and a large array of 
membrane transport systems, the kidney is often the major tar-
get organ for a diverse array of toxic chemicals. Nephrotoxicity 
can be induced by metabolites either being formed in the liver 
and then transported to the kidney or formed locally in the 
kidney. Phase I metabolizing enzymes, including CYP450s, 
FMO, alcohol and ALDHss, prostaglandin synthetase, mono-
amine oxidases, and hydroxylases, have been found in the 
kidney. Phase II metabolizing enzymes, such as UGT, NAT, 
GST, methyl transferases, and SULTs, have also been identi-
fied in the kidney. An important difference between the kid-
ney and other tissues is that many of the metabolic enzymes 
are differentially distributed among the nephron cell popula-
tions.409 For example, CYP450 is expressed exclusively in the 
kidney cortex, and prostaglandin synthetase is present in the 
medulla. With its uptake and efflux transporters and metabo-
lizing enzymes, the proximal tubule cell in the nephron is not 
only a major site in the disposition of xenobiotics but also a 
primary site for toxicity. Nephrotoxicity caused by metabolites 
has been well documented. The following are a few examples 
to illustrate metabolite-induced kidney toxicity:
Chloroform: Chloroform is an organic solvent that was 
historically used as an inhaled anesthetic for surgery and is 
still extensively used in the rubber industry, pesticide formu-
lations, and other industrial processes. Chloroform is known 
to cause hepatic and renal toxicity in a number of species 
through CYP450-dependent metabolism. This highly chlo-
rinated chemical is bioactivated by CYP2E1 to form phos-
gene, which can react with cellular macromolecules to initiate 
toxicity.410,411 In vitro studies have indicated that chloroform 
can be metabolized by CYP450 in the kidney to nephrotoxic 
intermediate. To investigate the role of renal P450 in chloro-
form-induced nephrotoxicity, Fang et al. used a mouse model 
with a liver-specific deletion of the NADPH-CYP450 reduc-
tase gene (liver-Cpr-null).412 Their studies showed that loss of 
hepatic CYP450-dependent chloroform metabolism did not 
protect against chloroform-induced renal toxicity, and that 
renal CYP450 enzymes play an essential role in chloroform-
mediated nephrotoxicity. Chloroform-induced nephrotoxicity 
is primarily restricted to cells of the proximal tubules, result-
ing in increased blood urea nitrogen (BUN), proteinuria, and 
glycosuria.
Bromobenzene: Bromobenzene is a chemical reagent used 
to introduce a phenyl group into other compounds. Exposure 
to the compound can cause liver and kidney damage in multi-
ple species. Bromobenzene-induced nephrotoxicity is charac-
terized by glucosuria, enzymuria, proteinuria, elevated BUN, 
and proximal tubular necrosis. Bromobenzene-induced liver 
toxicity is due to CYP450-mediated formation of the 3,4-epox-
ide metabolite that can arylate hepatic tissue. However, the for-
mation of the nephrotoxicant appears to require multiple steps 
of biotransformation. The first of which is hepatic CYP450-
mediated oxidation of bromobenzene to 2-bromophenol. This 
intermediate is further oxidized in the liver to 2-bromohy-
droquinone. Lau and Monks413 showed that administration 
of 2-bromohydroquinone to rats induced a dose-dependent 
decrease in hepatic and renal glutathione levels, an increase in 
BUN levels, and histopathological changes in the kidney with-
out causing any alterations to the liver. The authors suggested 
that 2-bromohydroquinone or a conjugate may be formed in 
the liver and transported to the kidney to cause nephrotoxic-
ity (EPA Toxicological review of bromobenzene, 2009, https://
iris.epa.gov/static/pdfs/1020tr.pdf).413,414
Aristolochic acid: One of the most enigmatic examples of 
environmentally induced kidney failure that baffled clinicians 
and toxicologists for decades is Balkan Endemic Nephropathy 
(BEN).415 It was first recognized in the 1920s among several 
rural villages along the Danube River in Bosnia, Bulgaria, 
Croatia, Romania, and Serbia. The disease did tend to affect 
families in the same household, but there was no clear pat-
tern of inheritance. There was also a strong association with 
upper urinary cancer. Over the next 70 years, scientists investi-
gated the role of heavy metals, PAHs, viruses, trace elements, 
the mycotoxin Ochratoxin A, and even tainted home-distilled 
plum brandy in BEN, but none was identified to be an etio-
logical agent. In October 2006, reports of horses developing 
nephropathy after consuming hay contaminated with seeds 
called Aristolochia clematitis were presented at an interna-
tional symposium held in Croatia. Subsequently, the scien-
tific and medical fields recognized the consumption of wheat 
grains contaminated with A. clematitis as the cause of BEN 
in humans. A. clematitis is a plant native to Europe. It grows 
among wheat plants, and the seeds mingle with the wheat used 
for home-baked bread. Researchers then identified aristolo-
chic acid I (AA-I) in the seeds as the etiological agent associ-
ated with human nephropathy and urothelial cancer.416 Studies 
of AA-I metabolism over the next ten years failed to provide 
a definite explanation of whether the metabolism of AA-I

190
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
occurring in the liver or the kidney was responsible for renal 
tubule toxicity. The mechanism underlying the AA-I-induced 
nephrotoxicity was finally solved by Eaton and a multidisci-
plinary team of investigators.417 These authors used microflu-
idics to connect a kidney-on-a-chip with a liver-on-a-chip to 
investigate the bioactivation and transport of AA-I metabolites 
to the kidney. The results indicated that the primary bioacti-
vation and detoxification of AA-I appear to occur in the liver 
(Figure 4.39). In the liver, AA-I is first bioactivated by nitrore-
ductase NQO1 and other reductases to N-hydroxyaristolactam 
I (AL-I-NOH), which undergoes sulfate conjugation to form 
toxic N-sulfonyloxyaristolactam (AL-I-NOSO3). This sulfate 
conjugate is transported out of the liver via ABC efflux trans-
porters and then actively transported into proximal tubular 
epithelial cells in the kidney via organic anionic transporters, 
leading to renal toxicity via oxidative stress and apoptosis.
4.6.4  Lung Toxicity
The lung is a target organ for the toxic effects of industrial 
chemicals, pesticides, environmental agents, natural products, 
and drugs. Factors that put the lung at high risk include selec-
tive tissue exposure, high tissue oxygenation, and the presence 
of metabolizing enzymes that can generate toxic products 
from initially nontoxic substances. Selective pulmonary expo-
sure most often results from the fact that the airway is exposed 
to the environment and is the major portal of entry for most 
airborne substances. In some cases, selective exposure results 
from the accumulation of agents, such as basic amines, from 
the circulation.418,419
Studies have identified that CYP450s and other Phase I 
enzymes, such as FMO, amine oxidase, EH, and prostaglandin 
synthase, are capable of metabolizing xenobiotics in the lung.37 
Significant levels and activities of Phase II conjugations in the 
lung also contribute to lung toxicity. For example, low GST 
activity in the lung correlates with an increased risk of lung 
cancer in smokers. Although the lung contains metabolizing 
enzymes similar to those found in the liver, the enzymatic sys-
tems in the lung are highly concentrated in specific cell types. 
Phase I metabolizing enzymes are predominantly expressed in 
the bronchial and bronchiolar epithelium, Clara cells, type II 
pneumocytes, and/or alveolar macrophages in the human lung, 
although individual enzymes might have distinct regional 
distributions in the airway. For example, Willey et al. evalu-
ated the expression of metabolic enzymes in primary bron-
chial epithelial cells and alveolar macrophages of nonsmokers 
and smokers.37,420 These authors showed that CYP1A1, 1B1, 
2B7, 2E1, and 4B1 as well as microsomal EH were expressed 
in human bronchial epithelial cells. Alveolar macrophages 
expressed CYP1A1 at low to unmeasurable levels in both 
smokers and nonsmokers. By contrast, expressions of both 
CYP1A1 and CYP1B1 in bronchial epithelial cells of smok-
ers were higher compared with those in nonsmokers. There 
was no significant difference in the expression of microsomal 
EH in smokers compared with nonsmokers. Studies have also 
shown that microsomal GST12, GST mu, phenol SULT, and 
thermolabile phenol SULT are found in both bronchial epi-
thelial cells and alveolar macrophages.37 CYP3A4, as well as 
UGT-1 and -2, are not expressed in either bronchial epithelial 
cells or alveolar macrophages.
Benzo(a)pyrene: Metabolism of benzo(a)pyrene in the 
lung is a classic example of how biotransformation of PAHs 
results in genotoxic metabolites that cause pulmonary can-
cer (Figure 4.9). Benzo(a)pyrene is found in cigarette smok-
ing, diesel exhaust, and combustion of organic materials. Like 
many PAHs, it is initially oxidized by CYP1A1 and 1B1 to 
the 7,8-oxide that is rapidly converted to the 7,8-dihydrodiol 
by another Phase I enzyme, EH. The latter metabolite is fur-
ther metabolized by CYP450s to benzo(a)pyrene 7,8-dihydro-
diol-9,10 epoxide which forms covalent adducts with DNA.421 
FIGURE 4.39  Metabolism of AA-I behind BEN. (Created with BioRender.com.)

191
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
Benzo(a)pyrene can enter the lung via two main pathways. 
The first is by ingestion of charred foods. Benzo(a)pyrene in 
cooked meat can be as high as 62.6 ng/g in overcooked char-
coal-grilled beef.422 Ingested PAHs will be absorbed from the 
gut and transported to the liver where they are subject to first-
pass metabolism before reaching the lung through the systemic 
circulation.423 Another exposure pathway is via inhaled soot 
particles from incomplete combustion of carbon-based com-
pounds. Both direct measurements of particulates and model-
ing have shown that PM2.5 particles deposit primarily in the 
upper airway down to about the sixth or seventh bifurcation, 
where most tumors occur. Benzo(a)pyrene adsorbed in the oily 
surface layer of soot particles can enter lung cells at sites of 
deposition424 and be metabolized in situ. These pathways for 
benzo(a)pyrene exposure in the lung are not mutually exclu-
sive, as some benzo(a)pyrene and its metabolites can escape 
first-pass metabolism, and those particulates deposited in the 
lung can make their way to the liver via blood to induce DNA 
adducts.425 This makes source attribution difficult. However, 
given the relatively short half-lives of the reactive benzo(a)
pyrene metabolites, it is likely that a fraction of lung mutagen-
icity results from in situ metabolism, regardless of the source.
4-Ipomeanol: In 2011, Portage County in the state of 
Wisconsin reported that 200 cows dropped dead on January 
14. Investigations showed that the animals died of acute 
respiratory distress. A toxin from moldy sweet potatoes was 
shown to be the causal agent in the cow’s seemingly mystical 
death. Moldy sweet potato toxicity is caused by the inges-
tion of the furanoterpenoid toxin, 4-ipomeanol, produced 
by sweet potatoes in response to infestation with the fungus 
Fusarium solani. Historically, 4-ipomeanol has been shown 
to cause pulmonary toxicity in rats, guinea pigs, and rabbits, 
producing edema, congestion, and hemorrhage with specific 
damage to non-ciliated bronchiolar epithelial cells (Clara 
cells).426,427 4-Ipomeanol exerts its specific toxicity in Clara 
cells after bioactivation by microsomal CYP450. The oxida-
tion of the furan ring leads to the formation of an unstable 
epoxide intermediate, which degrades into 4-ipomeanol 
enedial. The enedial intermediate forms protein adducts and 
causes toxicities.428,429 In rodents, CYP4B1 catalyzes the oxi-
dation of 4-ipomeanol in the lung where toxicity occurs.430 
Studies have shown that in rodents CYP4B1 is present at 
high levels in the lungs, and its affinity for 4-ipomeanol is 
greater than that of any CYP450 expressed in the liver. When 
rats were pretreated with 3-methylcholanthrene, a classic 
CYP1A inducer, bioactivation of 4-ipomeanol occurred pri-
marily in the liver, shifting the target organ toxicity from 
Clara cells to hepatocytes. Furthermore, without pretreat-
ment with 3-methylcholanthrene, hepatic necrosis was not 
observed in mice receiving near lethal doses of 4-ipomeanol. 
These results indicated that reactive metabolites of 4-ipo-
meanol were formed in the lung.427–431 The human homolog 
of CYP4B1 is functionally defective and does not bioactive 
4-ipomeanol.103 However, human hepatic CYP1A2, 2C19, 
2D6, and 3A4 can catalyze the oxidation of 4-ipomeanol to its 
reactive metabolite to cause hepatotoxicity.428,432 Overall, the 
original in vitro and in vivo studies conducted by Boyd and 
his colleagues426,427,429 established the basis for our current 
understanding of the lung-specific toxicities of 4-ipomeanol. 
This example shows how in situ production of a highly reac-
tive metabolite can be the primary cause of organ-specific 
toxicity.
4.6.5  Cardiovascular Toxicity
It has long been recognized that the cardiovascular system 
has the capacity for xenobiotic metabolism and also plays an 
extensive role in the biotransformation of endogenous sub-
strates necessary for homeostasis.433–436 For example, CYP450 
enzymes are involved in fatty acid metabolism in cardiomyo-
cytes. Emerging evidence suggests a role for CYP450s in the 
cardiovascular system; however, the current understanding 
about CYP450 regulation and function within the heart is lim-
ited. Studies have suggested that CYP2J2 is a predominant 
CYP450 isoform that is expressed constitutively in the adult 
human heart.88 Several studies further demonstrated that the 
presence of functional polymorphisms in the CYP2J2 gene is 
associated with coronary artery disease.437–440 Although this 
enzyme plays a relatively minor role in overall drug metab-
olism, CYP2J2 has a significant role in the metabolism of 
some drugs, such as doxorubicin. Studies using transgenic 
mice overexpressing cardiomyocyte-specific human CYP2J2 
showed reduced doxorubicin-induced cardiomyocyte apopto-
sis compared with wild-type hearts. Moreover, cardiac func-
tion was significantly higher in the transgenic CYP2J2 ultra 
expressers than in wild-type mice. Microsomes from the wild-
type and transgenic mice showed that doxorubicin turnover 
was increased in the CYP2J2 mice. Moreover, when doxoru-
bicin was combined with a selective epoxygenase inhibitor, 
MS-PPOH, the protective effect of human CYP2J2 expression 
was lost.85 These studies suggest that cardiac-specific overex-
pression of CYP2J2 may protect against doxorubicin-induced 
cardiotoxicity. For an exhaustive review of CYP450 enzymes 
and many of their cardiac substrates, inducers, and inhibitors, 
see Table 2 in the 2020 review by Fatunde and Brown.436
Terfenadine: Terfenadine is an antihistamine previously 
used for the treatment of allergic conditions. It was withdrawn 
from markets worldwide due to QT interval prolongation and 
risk for cardiac arrhythmia. Although the metabolite is not the 
cause of the organ toxicity, it is worth mentioning as an exam-
ple of how drug metabolism in one organ can lead to toxicity in 
another organ. As a prodrug, terfenadine is rapidly and almost 
completely metabolized to the active form fexofenadine in the 
liver by CYP3A4, and terfenadine normally is not measurable 
in the blood circulation due to the fast metabolic conversion. 
Terfenadine itself is cardiotoxic at higher doses, while fexof-
enadine is not. Concomitant administration of terfenadine and 
drugs that inhibit hepatic CYP3A4 can prevent metabolism 
and increase the concentration of terfenadine in the blood. 
This can lead to life-threatening ventricular arrhythmia and 
torsades de pointes associated with prolonged QT intervals. 
The potent CYP3A4 inhibitors include itraconazole, ketocon-
azole, clarithromycin, erythromycin, nefazodone, ritonavir, 
and grapefruit juice.441,442
Ethanol: Chronic long-term abuse of alcohol can lead to 
cardiac failure. The clinical manifestations of alcoholic car-
diomyopathy include dilated left ventricle, normal or reduced 
left ventricle wall thickness with increased left ventricle mass,

192
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
and a reduced left ventricular ejection fraction at later stages of 
the disease, ultimately leading to heart failure. Investigations 
showed that acetaldehyde, the major metabolite of ethanol, is in 
part responsible for the observed cardiac injury. The metabolic 
enzyme that converts ethanol to acetaldehyde is ADH. This 
enzyme is present at high levels in the liver but is expressed 
at very low levels in the heart and other tissues.443 Circulating 
acetaldehyde was shown to accumulate in the heart where it 
causes adverse effects.443–445
Cyclophosphamide: Cyclophosphamide is an alkylat-
ing agent that has potent immunosuppressive properties and 
anticancer activities. High-dose cyclophosphamide is widely 
used in chemotherapy and in post-transplantation to prevent 
graft-versus-host disease after allogeneic hematopoietic cell 
transplantation. The dose-limiting toxicity of cyclophospha-
mide is cardiotoxicity with the most severe clinical manifesta-
tion being hemorrhagic necrotic perimyocarditis. Studies have 
shown that cyclophosphamide is metabolized to 4-hydroxy-
cyclophosphamide by hepatic CYP2B6 and/or CYP2C19. The 
metabolite enters cardiac cells as the tautomer aldo-cyclophos-
phamide, which is further converted to phosphoramide mus-
tard and acrolein through β-elimination. Levin et al. showed 
that the toxic metabolite, 4-hydroxy-cyclophosphamide, is 
responsible for cyclophosphamide-induced cardiotoxicity.446 
More recent studies indicate that both metabolites 4-hydroxy-
cyclophosphamide and acrolein are strongly implicated in 
cardiotoxicity.447,448
Allylamine: Allylamine is used in the manufacture of 
pharmaceuticals and rubber vulcanization. Chronic exposure 
to this compound can produce lesions similar to acute myo-
cardial necrosis and atherosclerosis. The Phase I metabolic 
enzyme, semicarbazide-sensitive amine oxidase, bioactivates 
allylamine to its aldehyde metabolite, acrolein, which alkyl-
ates and inactivates GST and allows excessive peroxidative 
damage.449 Damage occurs in the heart and aortic tissue, which 
have high amine oxidase activity.450,451 While a plasma source 
of semicarbazide-sensitive amine oxidase makes a significant 
contribution to the metabolic activation of allylamine to its 
toxic metabolite, the tissue specificity might be related to the 
relatively high expression of the amine oxidase in the heart.
4.6.6  Neurotoxicity
CYP450s have been detected in the brains of multiple species 
including rats, mice, dogs, monkeys, and humans. The total 
amount of CYP450 enzymes in the brain is only 0.5%–2% of 
that in the liver, and the low CYP450 levels are unlikely to 
significantly influence systemic metabolite levels. However, an 
increasing number of in vitro and in vivo studies indicate that 
brain CYP450s can impact acute and chronic drug response 
and susceptibility to damage by neurotoxicants.68,452 In gen-
eral, CYP450s are distributed in the brain with heterogeneous 
patterns, and enzymatic expressions and activities vary across 
different cell types and regions. For example, in the human 
brain, expression of CYP2B6 protein shows ~2.5-fold varia-
tions among different regions of the brain. Within any par-
ticular brain region, CYP450 expression is usually restricted 
to specific populations of neurons and/or glia. For example, 
in the frontal cortex of the human brain, CYP2B6 is found 
mainly in astrocytes surrounding the cerebral blood vessels 
in layer I, whereas CYP2D6 is expressed in pyramidal neu-
rons in layers III-V and in white matter. The localization of 
brain CYP450s to specific regions and cell types allows for a 
considerable impact on metabolism within brain microenvi-
ronments. The levels of certain CYP450s, such as CYP2B, in 
specific neurons may be comparable to, or higher than levels 
in hepatocytes.452 CYP450s can metabolize many drugs and 
endogenous chemicals that act on the central nervous system 
including antidepressants, antipsychotics, endogenous neu-
rochemicals, and neurotoxicants. Three interesting examples 
are provided to illustrate drug metabolite-induced neurotoxic-
ity. One example provides evidence that chemical agents can 
be metabolized in the brain to cause toxicity, and the second 
example explains how a drug can be metabolized in the liver 
and then how it can reach the brain to cause intoxication. The 
last example shows an example of neurodevelopmental toxic-
ity as a result of drug metabolism in the developing brain in 
utero.
MPTP: Between 1976 and 1984, sporadic outbreaks of 
drug-induced parkinsonism occurred among drug abusers 
in California, Maryland, and Vancouver, British Columbia. 
Investigations identified MPTP as a contaminant in under-
ground laboratory preparations of a narcotic analog drug, 
Demerol. MPTP-induced parkinsonism in man is remarkably 
similar to idiopathic Parkinson’s disease, including rigidity, 
tremor, flexed posture, and loss of postural reflexes. The neu-
rotoxicity of MPTP produced a severe, permanent parkinso-
nian syndrome in a number of drug abusers. In an autopsy, 
MPTP appeared to destroy nerve cells in the substantia nigra, 
an area of the brain that plays a major role in controlling move-
ment. MPTP itself is not toxic. As a lipophilic compound, it 
crosses theblood–brain barrier. Once inside the brain, MPTP 
is metabolized into the toxic cation 1-methyl-4-phenylpyr-
idinium (MPP+) by monoamine oxidase-B present in glial 
cells (astrocytes). MPP+ causes permanent symptoms of 
Parkinson’s disease by destroying dopaminergic neurons in 
the substantia nigra of the brain.453 A more recent study has 
shown that MPTP-induced toxicity in primary dopaminergic 
neurons is partially attenuated by a CYP2D6 inhibitor (e.g., 
quinidine), and by monoamine oxidase-B inhibitors (deprenyl 
and pargyline).365 This study indicates that CYP2D6 expressed 
in dopaminergic neurons might be also involved in bioactivat-
ing MPTP to its toxic metabolite.
Codeine: Life-threatening opioid intoxication has been 
reported in patients after taking small doses of codeine. In a 
case study, a 62-year-old man with a history of chronic lym-
phocytic leukemia presented with a 3-day history of fever, 
cough, fatigue, and dyspnea.364 Clinical and radiologic find-
ings indicated bilateral pneumonia with a yeast infection. The 
patient was treated with ceftriaxone, clarithromycin, and vori-
conazole for pneumonia, and he was also given oral codeine 
(25 mg three times a day) to relieve the cough. On day 4 in 
the hospital, the patient’s consciousness rapidly declined, and 
he went into a coma. Immediate noninvasive ventilation did 
not improve the patient’s neurologic signs. Codeine is a pro-
drug that is metabolized to morphine by CYP2D6 enzyme. An 
extensive assessment revealed that the patient’s plasma level 
of codeine was 114 μg/L (expected range 13–75 μg/L), plasma

193
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
morphine level was 80 μg/L (expected range 1–4 μg/L), and 
the morphine-6-glucuronide level was 136 μg/L (expected 
range 1–13 μg/L). Evaluation of the patient’s CYP2D6 geno-
typing showed three or more functional alleles, indicat-
ing that the patient is a CYP2D6 ultrarapid metabolizer. As 
shown in Figure 4.40, after entering the human body, 10% of 
the administered codeine is bioactivated by hepatic CYP2D6 
into morphine, which then undergoes glucuronidation to form 
morphine-6-glucuronide and morphine-3-glucuronide. Both 
morphine and morphine-6-glucuronide have opioid activi-
ties. The majority of codeine is converted to inactive norco-
deine by hepatic CYP3A4 and then by glucuronidation into 
codeine-6-glucuronide. Being an ultrarapid metabolizer, the 
patient had significantly elevated plasma levels of codeine 
metabolites, morphine, and morphine-6-glucuronide, which 
reached the brain and caused opioid intoxication. While tak-
ing codeine, the patient was also treated with clarithromycin 
and voriconazole (for pneumonia) that inhibited the CYP3A4 
enzyme, leading to decreased clearance of codeine and 
increased risk of opioid overdose. After identifying the cause 
of the coma, the patient was given intravenous administration 
of naloxone (a medicine used to block opioid effects) which 
resulted in a speedy recovery. This case report of central ner-
vous system depression after the administration of small doses 
of codeine in a CYP2D6 ultrarapid metabolizer supports the 
importance of pharmacogenomics testing in preventing seri-
ous drug-induced adverse effects.
Cocaine in the fetal brain: The final example concerns 
the role of CYP450-mediated metabolism of cocaine in the 
fetal brain. In humans, CYP3A family metabolizes cocaine.454 
While CYP3A4 and CYP3A5 isoforms are primarily expressed 
in the adult liver and small intestine, both are detectable in 
the adult human brain. By contrast, CYP3A43 is expressed at 
very low levels in the liver, but at much higher levels in the 
brain, suggesting that this enzyme might metabolize drugs 
in the human brain.455 Studies indicate that prenatal cocaine 
exposure in rats during the most active period of neural pro-
genitor proliferation induces cytoarchitectural changes in the 
embryonic neocortex. However, due to significant differences 
between humans and rodents in neocorticogenesis and brain 
CYP450-mediated metabolism, extrapolating findings in rats 
to humans have significant limitations. To overcome the limi-
tation of a rodent model, Lee et al. recently developed a cell 
culture system that allowed for the growth and differentiation 
of human peripheral stem cells into 3D neocortical organ-
oids.456 Although CYP3A4, CYP3A5, and CYP3A43 have 
been detected in different regions of the adult human brain, 
only CYP3A5 and CYP3A43 are expressed in the neocorti-
cal organoids.456 Exposure of the 3D prenatal cortex organ-
oids to cocaine led to premature neuronal differentiation and 
significantly inhibited the development of the neocortical 
areas. These data demonstrate that the prenatal 3D neocorti-
cal organoid cultures can recapitulate abnormalities seen in 
the developing human neocortex after exposure to cocaine in 
the uterus. The mechanism of toxicity included the generation 
of reactive oxygen species, inhibition of neocortical progeni-
tor cell proliferation, induction of premature neuronal differ-
entiation, and interruption of neural tissue development. The 
FIGURE 4.40  Metabolic pathways of codeine biotransformation: the majority of codeine is metabolized by CYP3A4 to inactive norcodeine that 
is then converted to codeine-6-glucuronide by glucuronidation. A small portion of codeine (~10%) is bioactivated by CYP2D6 to form morphine. 
Morphine is further metabolized into morphine-6-glucuronide and morphine-3-glucuronide. Morphine and morphine-6-glucuronide have opioid 
activity. Glucuronides are eliminated by the kidney. (Created with BioRender.com.)

194
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
investigators further showed that the knockdown of CYP3A5, 
but not CYP3A43, reversed the cocaine-induced alterations 
in neocortical development. Together, these in vitro studies 
strongly suggest that cocaine metabolized by CYP3A5 in the 
developing frontal cortex is the main driver for neurobehav-
ioral developmental deficits in vivo.
4.7  Regulatory Requirement on 
the Safety Evaluation of Drug 
Metabolites and Metabolism-
Mediated Drug-Drug Interactions
To evaluate safety for human use, regulatory agencies require 
sponsors to provide significant data on the metabolism of drugs 
and other chemicals (e.g., pesticides) that are under their juris-
diction. The amount and type of data requested depend on the 
proposed use of a compound, its potential exposure to humans, 
and the effects of metabolism on potency and toxicity. The U.S. 
FDA provides guidance documents to the pharmaceutical and 
biotechnology industry regarding approaches that are accept-
able for the evaluation of drug metabolism and drug-drug 
interactions at different stages of drug development. The U.S. 
Environmental Protection Agency also provides a guideline on 
evaluating the metabolism and pharmacokinetics of pesticides 
and toxic substances as a part of safety testing (US EPA 1998, 
Health Effects Test Guidelines, OPPTS 870.7485, Metabolism 
and Pharmacokinetics https://www.regulations.gov/document/
EPA-HQ-OPPT-2009-0156-0047). The two sets of guidelines 
are largely consistent with each other.
This section will focus on reviewing and discussing regula-
tory requirements for safety testing of drug metabolites and 
the evaluation of drug-drug interaction. During the drug devel-
opment process, early information on the metabolism of drugs 
is vital for the selection of drug candidates for further devel-
opment. In January 2020, the FDA revised its 2017 draft and 
published the final guidance entitled In Vitro Drug Interaction 
Studies – CYP450 Enzyme- and Transporter-Mediated Drug 
Interactions (U.S. FDA. Guidance for Industry: In Vitro Drug 
Interaction Studies – CYP450 Enzyme- and Transporter-
Mediated Drug Interactions, Silver Spring, MD, MD. U.S. 
Food and Drug Administration, 2020 https://www.fda.gov/
media/134581/download). This updated guidance stressed the 
importance of generating high-quality and relevant in vitro 
data on the human metabolism of a drug candidate during 
the early stages of drug development. These data are vital for 
evaluating the risks of drug-drug interaction and assessing 
variability in exposure resulting from individual polymorphic 
expression of metabolizing enzymes. The final guidance docu-
ment is based on the following general observations:
•	 The concentrations of a drug or its active metabolite 
in the systemic circulation determine the extent of its 
therapeutic efficacy and/or adverse effects.
•	 Metabolism is a major component of clearance that 
determines in vivo drug exposure.
•	 Large differences in blood drug concentrations 
can occur because of polymorphic metabolism. 
Drug-drug interactions can also alter the blood con-
centration of a drug.
•	 Major advances have been made in the availability of 
human tissue and recombinant enzymes for in vitro 
studies of drug metabolism.
The guidance document elaborates that the goals of in vitro 
metabolism and drug interaction studies are to (1) identify 
major metabolic pathways and the human enzymes involved 
and (2) explore and extrapolate the effects of the drug candi-
date on the metabolism of other drugs and the effect of other 
drugs on the metabolism of the candidate. To accomplish these 
goals, the FDA recommends the use of commercially available 
human hepatic microsomes as a first step, then moving to cell-
based systems that express specific human P450s.
A companion guidance document providing recommen-
dations for in vivo characterization of drug metabolism and 
potential drug-drug interactions was released in January 
2020 (Guidance for Industry: Clinical Drug Interaction 
Studies – CYP450 Enzyme- and Transporter-Mediated 
Drug Interactions. Silver Spring, MD; U.S. Food and Drug 
Administration, 2020 https://www.fda.gov/media/134581/
download). As the metabolic pathways of a given drug can 
differ dramatically between humans and other species, 
drug-drug interaction studies are usually not conducted in 
animals. When in vitro assessment of metabolism provides 
evidence for potential drug-drug interactions, pharmaceuti-
cal and biotechnology companies are required to perform 
clinical drug-drug interaction studies in human subjects in 
accordance with the requirements set forth in the FDA guid-
ance entitled Clinical Drug Interaction Studies--CYP450 
Enzyme- and Transporter-Mediated Drug Interactions. FDA 
emphasizes the importance of establishing whether metabo-
lism is a major mechanism for drug clearance. Both guidance 
documents recommend specific study designs to evaluate 
both inhibition and induction of the metabolism of the drug 
candidate. The guidance documents provide a systematic 
approach to evaluate the potential for the drug-drug inter-
actions with investigational drugs and outline recommenda-
tions for their mitigation.
In March 2020, the FDA issued a final guidance document 
entitled Safety Testing of Drug Metabolites (Guidance for 
Industry: Safety Testing of Drug Metabolites. Silver Spring, 
MD, U.S. Food and Drug Administration, 2020; https://www.
fda.gov/media/72279/download). The guidance includes spe-
cific recommendations for the pharmaceutical and biotech-
nology industry regarding when and how to identify and 
characterize drug metabolites. The guidance also sought to 
align with the ICH guidance for industry M3(R2) Nonclinical 
Safety Studies for the Conduct of Human Clinical Trials 
and Marketing Authorization for Pharmaceuticals (ICH 
guidance for industry M3(R2) Nonclinical Safety Studies 
for the Conduct of Human Clinical Trials and Marketing 
Authorization for Pharmaceuticals https://www.ema.europa.
eu/en/documents/scientific-guideline/ich-guideline-m3r2-
non-clinical-safety-studies-conduct-human-clinical-trials-
marketing-authorisation_en.pdf). The documents stipulate 
what in vitro and in vivo methods can be used to determine 
metabolic profiles for drug candidates that must be determined

195
Metabolism: A Determinant of Toxicity
at different stages of the drug development process. In vitro 
studies are usually acceptable at the early stage of drug devel-
opment and before initiating clinical trials. Liver microsomes, 
liver slices, or hepatocytes from both animals and humans 
are most commonly used for these in vitro studies, which are 
expected to reveal qualitative differences in metabolism across 
species. Subsequent in vivo studies are used to confirm in vitro 
data and provide a quantitative measure of metabolite forma-
tion and pharmacokinetics in humans.
Nonclinical evaluation of drug safety usually includes 
standard animal toxicology studies to evaluate drug expo-
sure and potential toxicities. Systemic exposures of the drug 
candidate in the animal species are then compared with pro-
posed human therapeutic exposure levels to assess potential 
risks in humans. In some cases, the metabolic profile of a 
drug in humans varies significantly from that observed in 
animal species used in nonclinical studies. For example, a 
metabolite may only be formed in humans or be produced at 
higher levels in humans than in the test species. As a result, 
the evaluation of a drug candidate using standard nonclini-
cal toxicology methods does not predict metabolite-medi-
ated toxicities in humans. To circumvent this pitfall, the 
FDA guidance requires that metabolites, especially those 
present at more than 10% of total drug exposure in humans, 
be evaluated for safety using an appropriate animal spe-
cies. This requirement can be fulfilled by using one of two 
approaches. The first is to identify a species that produces 
metabolites at levels equivalent to or greater than those 
formed in humans. The validated species can then be used 
to model toxicity in humans. The alternative approach is to 
synthesize the metabolites and directly evaluate the safety 
of the metabolite in animal species. The FDA recognizes the 
challenges associated with the synthesis of specific metabo-
lites and the complexities that accompany direct administra-
tion. Nonetheless, regulators emphasize the prominence of 
identifying and evaluating the toxicities of drug metabolites 
to ensure safety for clinical use. In practice, safety evalu-
ation of drug metabolites in animals is usually conducted 
after confirmation of a significant amount of active metabo-
lites in early clinical trials.
The FDA’s guidance on the safety testing of drug metabolites 
applies to the development of non-oncology drug candidates. 
For drug candidates that are intended for anticancer therapy, 
the regulatory requirements are less restrictive. Because anti-
cancer drugs are intended to treat patients with life-threaten-
ing malignancies, the aim of the regulatory guidance is more 
focused on accelerating the drug development process; hence, 
there is an attenuated requirement for metabolite testing. The 
guideline from the International Council for Harmonisation 
of Technical Requirements for Pharmaceuticals for Human 
Use (ICH) on Nonclinical Evaluation for Anticancer 
Pharmaceuticals indicates that a stand-alone toxicology eval-
uation of human metabolites that have not been qualified in 
nonclinical studies is generally not necessary for patients with 
terminal cancer (ICH guidance for industry S9 Nonclinical 
evaluation for anticancer pharmaceuticals, 2010 https://www.
ema.europa.eu/en/documents/scientific-guideline/ich-s-9-non-
clinical-evaluation-anticancer-pharmaceuticals-note-guid-
ance-nonclinical-evaluation_en.pdf).
4.8  Questions
	 4.1	 During the development of a new drug, it was 
decided that the introduction of a hydroxyl group 
onto the molecule would make the compound more 
water soluble, providing increased gastric solubil-
ity and improving the pharmaceutics properties. 
However, a pharmacokinetic study in rats showed 
that the plasma concentrations of the hydroxylated 
drug were maintained in the systemic circulation 
for a longer period than that of the non-hydroxyl-
ated analog. How would you explain this finding?
	 4.2	 As a toxicologist, you have been asked to design 
a program to assess the potential hazard of a 
chemical. What type of information concerning 
its metabolism would you want to have before you 
design the hazard assessment program?
	 4.3 	A cancer chemotherapeutic drug has been shown 
effective in treating a specific type of cancer. The 
drug is cytotoxic and produces severe side effects 
if it is not rapidly metabolized by CYP450. It is 
important, therefore, not to treat a patient with 
doses of the drug that are too high for the patient’s 
capacity to rapidly metabolize it to the less toxic 
product. What characteristics of the patient should 
be considered when attempting to choose a dose 
that will minimize the side effects?
	 4.4 	A compound is bioactivated by the CYP2E1 and 
then forms sulfate and glucuronide conjugates as 
well as mercapturic acid before being excreted. 
What changes in metabolism might contribute to 
severe toxicity as the dose is increased above the 
no-observable-effect level?
